THE RED PAGE
WINDS.
The wind takes color from the trees ;
Through trees the wind grows green,
And like a blue wave from the seas
The ocean wind is seen.
The red road paints the. road-wind red,
And down the ripe gold corn
The chuckling wind with golden head
Trots merrily at morn.
The black lake gives the black wind birth,
The brown breeze blows along
The ploughed brown cocked-up smoking
earth
To the tune of a hunting song.
The w r hite wind moves in the white moon’s
wake.
With straight, white, streaming hair,
And ever she wails in wmrds that ache
The burthen of her despair.
0 gusty soul that lives within,
Why dost thou flush and fade,
Wind-like, to flume and icicle-
Red Rahab and white Maid?
HUGH McCRAR.
NIGHT ON SYDNEY HARBOR.
Night slowly stooped in amorous wise to
fy earth,
Who smiled, and stretched her arms, and
drew him down.
A little breeze crept through the tired
town.
As their breaths mingled, like a thrill of
birth.
Beyond the hills, like a slim Oread,
Who sees her shadow in a quiet pool,
Nhked, bewilderingly beautiful,
And half draws back afraid, and half is glad
Of her own beauty, the moon tremblingly
Rose up, flushed with warm radiance, soft
as clings
To youthful lovers’ shy rememberings
Of love’s first unbelievbd ecstasy.
* * * *
Across the shadow of the sea there moves
The shadow’s shadow of a ship, that seems
Bound surely for some dreamless shore of
dreams,
Of silent rivulets and sleeping groves.
Like sorrow, but with gentler magic, night
Makes, visible the soul of common things.
Here in the moonbeams’ silver shadowings
Thev seem to breathe a mystical delight.
There, where awhile the sprawling city lay
Like a sea-monster, basking its huge frame.
Blossoms a secret garden, sown with flame
And set with strange bright blooms, unknown
of day :
As when the soul, set free ’twixt Heaven and
Hell,
Wandering beside the shadowy tides of
sleep,
Ere back to the abysmal dark they creep.
Finds a strange dream cast up thence like &
shell
Between its feet, and, listening, faintly hears,
Echoing across the unmeasured gulf sub-
lime
Beyond the utmost shores of space or time,
The matVix-musie of the primal spheres.
FRITZ S. BURNELL.
“Max"
"In certain congruities of dark cloth, n.
the rigid perfection of his linen, in the sym-
metry of his glove with liis hand, lay the
secret of Mr. Brummell’s miracles. He
was ever most economical, most scrupulous
of means. . . .Mr. Brummell was, indeed, in
the utmost sense of the word, an artist.”
These sentences from Max Beerbohm’s
essay on “Dandies” would do for Max him-
self. He, too, is a dandy, and, in the utmost
sense of the word, an artist. In his cari-
catures, particularly the early ones, there are
certain,congruities of dark masses, a rigid
perfection of white, and a symmetry in char-
acterisation, which fits the soul of his subject
like a glove. And in lines and words he is
most economical of means.
Like most geniuses, he began his brilliant
career very early. John Lane, his biblio-
grapher, and, incidentally, his publisher states
" Max, ” from a drawing by
J. Simpson.
that he was born
in London in
the same week
as Aubrey
Beardsley, the
precise date be-
ing August 26,
1872. His nur-
sery essays and
cartoons are,
doubtless, pre-
served, and will
see the light in
an edition de
luxe when Max
is no more. At
an early date he
was known as a
child author, and
as that he is
known still.
Only once has
he murmured —•
in a protest
against the mod-
ern cult of the
child :
The public knows me to be a child
author, and likes to picture me at my
desk dressed in black velveteen with legs
dangling towards the floor.
M hen Simpson portrayed him in the draw-
ing reproduced, he had got into long trousers.
“I was a modest, good-humored boy,” he says
somewhere. “It is Oxford that has made
me insufferable.” That is his gentle way of
referring to his distinguishing quality of
polished impertinence. It has made him the
idol of the British club men, the loungers
and persifleurs in literature. “Max” has
An early H. G. Wells,
by Max.
become a na-
tional institu-
tion like Punch.
He it was who
glorified rouge,
who took a luief
for George the
Four t h, who
raised Beau
Brummell to the
highest rank as
an artist, while
D’Orsay wa s
rated as “a way-
ward, excessive
creature, too
fond of life and
other follies to
a cliie v e real
greatness,” and
he, too, at the
very outset
struck the secret
of the great es-
sayi s t s, and
wrote about
himself. Here is
an extract from
his first book,
modestly en-
titled Works: —
In the year of grace, 1890, and in the
beautiful autumn of that year, I was a
freshman at Oxford. I remember how my
tutor asked me what lectures I wished to
attend, and how he laughed when I said that 1
wished to attend the lectures of Mr. Walter
Pater. Also, I remember how, one morning
soon after, I went into Ryman’s to order
some foolish engraving for my room, and
there saw, peering into a portfolio a small,
thick, rock-faced man whose top-hat and
gloves of bright dogskin struck one of the
many discords in that little city of learning
and laughter. The serried bristles of his
mqustachio made for him a false military
air. I think I nearly went down when they
told me that this was Pater.
Not that even in those most decadent
days of my childhood did I admire the man
as a stylist. Even then I was angry that
he. should treat English as a dead language,
bored by that sedulous ritual wherewith he
laid out every sentence as in a shroud —-
hanging like a widower long over its mar-
moreal beauty or ever he could lay it at
length in his book, its sepulchre. From
that laden air, the so cadaverous murmur of
that sanctuary, I would hook it at the beck
of any jade.
Lord Lansdowne and H. G. Wells.
Carlyle had written heavily about the sig-
nificance of clothes, but Max penetrated more
thoroughly that dark mystery, and showed
how subtly expressive a costume may be.
The bowler of Mr. Jerome K. Jerome is
a perfect preface to all his works. The silk
hat of Mr. Whistler is a real nocturne, his
linen a symphony en Mane majeur. To have
seen Mr. Hall Caine is to have read his
soul. His flowing formless cloak is as one
of his own novels, 25 editions latent in the
folds of it. Melodrama crouches upon the
brim of his sombrero. His tie is a Pub-
lisher's Announcement. His boots are Copy-
right. In his hand he holds the staff of The
Family Herald.
Max does not allow himself to become flam-
boyant; there is always a chaste restraint,
a dignified reticence, an air of imperturbable
calm in writing aoout himself or lesser mat
fers. Thus in 1896 he bade farewell to
Getters:—
Once in the delusion that Art, loving the
recluse, would make his life happy, I wrote
a little for a yellow quarterly, and had that
succes de fiasco which is always given to a
young writer of talent. But the stress of
creation soon overwhelmed me. Only Art
with a capital II gives any consolations to
her henchmen. And I, who crave no knight-
hood, shall write no more. I shall write no
more. Already I feel myself to be a trifle
outmoded. I belong to the Beardsley period'
Younger men with months of activity be-
fore them, with fresher schemes and notions,
with newer enthusiasms, have pressed for-
ward since then. (Jedo junioribus. Indeed,
I stand aside with no regret. For to be out-
moded is to be a classic, if one has written
well. I have acceded to the hiei-arehy of
good scribes, and rather like my niche.
“A Book of Ferses undernealh a bough," etc.
The charm of the first book was not quite
maintained in More, though there are many
delightful flashes in it, and Yet Again is
Max no longer dandaieal, but just as humor-
ous and more soberly elegant. It would be
pleasure to quote from his appreciations of
Ouida and The- Gentle Art of Making Ene-
mies, from his pathetic acount of the loss of
a fine collection of luggage labels and many
other exquisite passages. But for a paltry
five siblings or so you can get each volume
of Max’s cheerful Works. He promises a
novel this year which should be a thing of
joy.
Three books of his caricatures have
already been published. The earliest have
the most bite; the later are more broadly
humorous. An exhibition of his reeent cari-
catures was held a few months ago in Lon-
don, and celebrities had the pleasure of see-
ing themslves provide amusement for the
public. Nearly everybody of note is there,
including his half-brother, Beerbohm Tree,
and himself. The drawing of Wells explain-
ing things to Lansdowne was at the exhibi-
tion ; the illustration of Omar’s verse is in
the book called The Poets' Corner.
MORNING GLORY.
The sky has changed from black' to grey,
From grey to primrose clear,
The climbing stars have paled away,
The sun is very near.
The hills are flushing mauve and pink,
Translucent, cool and pale,
And soon the rising sun will drink
Their bubble-colors frail.
The soft, smooth bands of nightuuist lie
Across the mountain’s base,
So it is floating in the sky,
No more an earthly place.
What wonder? That rose-violet
Diaphanous and far
Was never this world’s treasure yet,
But from some younger star.
* * -X- *
The east is apricot—and see
The sun leaps out behind
The hills-—the cool dawn-colors flee
Like bubbles in the wind.
DOROTHEA MACKELLAR.
In re Marcus Clarke
Two or three people have been writing of
Marcus Clarke lately, and they prompt the
telling of the story of the killing of Rufus
Dawes. The Moloneys—Lawyer Jim and
Doctor Pat —were among the strongest
friends of Marcus. Doctor Pat had a liter-
ary turn himself. He wrote a good sonnet
on Melbourne by night, with the lines:
I see the parallels of thy long streets
Like golden angels shining all a-row.
Doctor Pat made a fortune —he was a very
skilful operator; retired, went to live in
London, and died of want of occupation
and the breaking up of lifelong habit.
Seven years ago we were walking from
Blackfriars Bridge along the Thames em-
bankment to Westminster, and he told me
what I would die of.
“A rush of blood to the brain,” he said.
“Apoplexy will settle you, old man.”
“It will prove I have a brain, anyway.
What will you die of?”
“Kidney convulsion.”
“One of these days they’ll find a solvent
for uric acid fat.”
“God forbid,” said Doctor Pat. “God for-
bid. It would wipe out Genius. The physi-
eal cnuse ° f great thought is a trituration
of the delicate blood vessels of the brain bv
urates.” -
“That’s new, anyhow.”
Look at them all. Kit Marlow stabbed
by a serving man in a brawl; Shake-
speare dying of a healing fever. It may be
only that the possession of great ability or
great strength predisposes a man to excess ”
“On the principle that all you have in
you should be used before you die?”
“Yes. And see how some of these poets
with more than the intuition of a woman
jump to the conclusion science wins through
centuries of labor. I’ve never forgiven Ten-
nyson for saying that Queen Victoria was
pre-eminent in grief when I know that there
are more heart-breaks in hovels than in
palaces simply because there are few pal-
aces,. and his utter snobbery disgusted me.
Think of that awful line:
The village ne’er had seen a costlier funeral.
But he had true vision and inspiration in
most things. D’ye remember that in the
“Dream of Fair Women”?
As when some great thought strikes along
the brain,
And flushes all the cheek.
Uric acid flush, uric acid flush, my boy.”
When Dr. Pat had his surgery in Lons-
dale-street, opposite Melbourne* Hospital,
Marcus Clarke, then writing “For the Term
of His Natural Life,” was a daily, or rather a
nightly visitor —for they lived all day and
talked all night. Lawyer Jim Moloney had
been arguing against a happy ending, and
therefore an inartistic ending to the
tragedy of Rufus Dawes; and failing to
move Marcus he led him out for a walk at
midnight. “It was a magic night,” says
Jim Moloney ; “the moon in her pride; and
we walked up Russell-street past the gaol
and down Grattan-street and along the Syd-
uey-road—the trees so still, the night so
fair. And I said to him : ‘Marcus, Rufus
Dawes must die.’ ‘I can’t, Jim,’ he said.
‘I can’t kill the poor chap.’ I said, ‘Mar-
cus, he must die! Consider! For years you
have held him bound in torture —-beaten,
disgraced, unmanned. The iron is in his
soul; Nature has borne all she can, and now
craves only the quiet of the grave. Kill
him. Kill the poor weary man. Marcus,
screw your courage up and kill him.’ ‘Oh,
Jim,’ lie said, ‘let him have a day or two
of happiness. His innocence is known—his
mother wants him in England. Let him
live.’ ‘No, he must die. You offer him a
new torture. You offer that poor stricken
unhappy sinful soul—his mother—a new
unhappiness, the knowledge that he knows.
The chain gang and the shadow of the gal-
lows have been over him, and, innocent
though he is, he can never escape their
memories. Indurated to insult, his soul
scarred by injustice as his physical body by
the lash—you may take him back to Eng-
land, but Norfolk Island and its memories
will go with him.’
“ ‘But he is still human,’ pleaded Marcus,
but more weakly. ‘Let him live.’
“‘No; use the humanity left him to do
some great deed, but he must die.’
" ‘Yes,. I could do that,’ said Marcus.
‘Make him save Sylvia from an elopement
with North, and give his life for her in the
end.’
“‘Do as you please with him ; but, Mar-
cus, he must: die. Come, no weakness.’
“Well, it was nearly dawn wlien we re-
turned to my brother’s surgery, and I said
to him: ‘Pat! Marcus and I have killed
Rufus Dawes.’
“ ‘l’m very glad to hear it,’ said Pat.
‘Let’s have a drink.’ ” '
And the three murderers sat down to beer.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
There is now a large, supply of reading
matter in French at Dymock’s Book Arcade.
In addition to the “Collection Nelson” (Is
od. per vol.), Dent’s “Les Classiques Fran-
Ca j s (Is. 9d.), and “Tousles Chefs-
D Oeuvre de la Literature Frangaise” (Is.
od.), they have stocked all the current fic-
tion issued by Calmann-Levy, at Is. 3d.
(paper), and the Modern-BibliothSque at
2s. (cloth).
D. McKee Wright’s “Viking Song,” which
appeared in The Bulletin some time ago, has
been printed with a musical setting by Cur-
wen and Sons, London. Without permission
from the author, the words have been altered
so that Wright appears to be barracking for
Britannia instead of Australia. No pub-
lisher has a right to make such a change
without authority, otherwise we will have
democratic verse perverted into songs for the
army of the Fat.
F. B. Wilkinson has compiled a necessary
Supplement to the 7th Edition of his father’s
Australian Magistrate. The new industrial
legislation, amending Acts and decisions up
tc date are arranged in order, with references
to the standard work and full indexes. Pub-
lished by The Law Book Co. of Australasia,
Sydney.
All the books of Gabriele d’Annunzio have
now been placed unon the Index at the Vati-
can, together with Fogazzaro’s last novel,
Leila.

CLEMENTS TONIC LIMITED.
It is with the deepest feeling of gratitude that I write to tell you that in my
opinion Clements Tonic is a remedy of most marvellous powers and the most
wonderful medicine known.
I have been for 28 years in business in Hobart. lam well known to every-
one there, and 24 years ago I was taken very ill with Hydatids of the Liver,
which resulted in several serious operations, and which led to continuous ill-
health afterwards. Like a sick woman will, I tried many experts and their
medicines, and these failing, I resorted to advertised medicines, without regain-
ing my health or strength.
I suffered, as the result of the operations, from LOSS OF MEMORY,
NERVOUS BREAKDOWN, DIZZINESS, CHRONIC INSOMNIA or
LOSS OF SLEEP, POOR APPETITE and INDIGESTION. I could not
walk across the room I was so weak; in fact, I thought I would never be able
to attend to my business again.
Seeing Clements Tonic advertised, I decided to try a bottle, and found it to
act like magic in giving me an appetite and better nerve strength, sol decided
to continue it, and this is the result: To-day lam alive and well. Although
this critical period of my life happened 24 years ago, I am here in the flesh
to-dav, thanks to that splendid medicine, which cured me of the ailments
specified in this letter, NERVOUS BREAKDOWN IN PARTICULAR. I
had no more sleepless nights, and in a few weeks, after taking it, I was strong
and well, and used to rise in the morning as happy as a lark. I can give you
the names of dozens of Hobart citizens who know these words are true, also the
name of the chemist there who sold me the tonic, Mr. A. P. Miller, Liverpool
Street, Hobart, and from whose pharmacy I have often bought it since.
I always recommend Clements Tonic to persons in ill-health, because I
know what it is and what its use means for women, and I hope you will use this
letter in the great cause of suffering humanity.
(Signed) MRS. MINNIE NIETOM ,
Witness to signature:
Mr. F. Fowler, J.P.,
Enmore, IS.S.W.
132 Liverpool Street,
Hobart,
21st November, 1910.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Bridges.
On the lake of the Four Cantons, where the polyglot
peoples go,
To the gem of the hills, Lucerne, when the waters
are blue and clear,
When the sun lifts the rolling mists from the heights
of eternal snow,
You may walk on the ancient bridges like men of
an elder year.
They are roofed from the sun and weather with
rufous, discolored tiles,
And one has an ancient tower that stems the force
of the flow
Of the flood of hurrying waters, drawn down from the
far defiles
To pay tumultuous tribute to the river that winds
below.
And here, in the time-worn bridges, you read, as
you pass across,
The legend of many a Switzer, the stories of old
Lucerne.
The deeds of a sturdy people, their prowess, and
gain, and loss;
Of guards of relief from Zurich, \of burghers who
rode from Berne.
For, set in the brown roof-rafters, in each triangular
space,
Is a painting that tells of tourneys, or a picture
of siege and war ;
Drawings of state processions, or quaint delights of
the chase;
Portrayals of old-time doings, by artists that are no
more.
You may follow the conte historic, the poste and
riposte of state,
The fights on the way to freedom, the failures that
breed success;
An order grown out of chaos, till a brotherhood made
them great,
And the hardihood of the mountain men triumphing
over stress.
SWITZERLAND.
C. G. A. COLLES.
Mudgee Town.
(To be read or sung with a touch of the brogue .)
I’m not standing on the platform for the gaping fools
to see:
Every train that comes to Mudgee is an empty train
to me.
Ah! me boy was quite continted to stay West and
settle down,
Till the d—n Pro-gress Committee brought the rail-
way to the town.
I am sitting by the river, listening to the sad old
song,
Where a sigh seems floating ever down the willow’d
Cudgegong.
Oh! I hate the cruel cuttings an’ embankments round
Mount Frome,
For they took me sweetheart from me and they took
me heart from home.
For he went by rail to Sydney, an’ he ne’er come
back again ;
An’ he left, where my poor heart was, just a dull an’
endless pain.
Oh! his arm was firm around me, an’ his eyes were
truest brown—
An’ I curse the day when “progress” brought the
railway to the town.
On the old coach road to Sydney there’s a mile-tree
by the track,
With a green branch pointing forward an’ a dead
branch pointing back ;
An’ the granite peaks behind it seem to wait in
vain and frown —-
They were grand before misfortune brought the rail
to Mudgee town !
I was fresher in me girlhood—-yes.! an’ greener than
the bough,
But me hands an’ heart are withered, an’ me life’s
the dead branch now.
Ah! I wonder does he flourish ? Does his track lie
up or down?
Does he curse the day whin fortune brought the
rail to Mudgee town?
Ah! I used to know in those days that a 1 woman’s
heart could ache,
But I thought that it was rubbish that a woman’s
heart could break.
Ah, but my heart was a girl’s heart, an’ a lovin’
heart at worst,
An’ a true heart to a devil!—an’ I know that that
can burst!
N.B.W.
HENRY RAWSON.
The Lady Barber.
The lady-barber soaped his chin,
With fingers soft, caressing,
She rubbed it well into the skin,
Whilst Bertie breathed a blessing—
A blessing on the Fates that gave
This new sensation to a shave;
And, as the fair deserve the brave,
His suit he started pressing.
Alas ! the bold and shameless knave
His suit was hotly pressing.
Quoth he: “There’s magic in your touch,
It has a wondrous virtue.”
She murmured: “Razor scrape you much?
Please tell me if I hurt you.”
“How sweet,” he sighed, “to rest like this!
But could you not complete my bliss
By granting me one little kiss?”
Said she: “You silly Bert, you!”
In business-tone the smiling miss,
Exclaimed, “You silly Bert, you.”
She rubbed more soap into his face,
Quoth he: “You little beauty,
I’d like to choose a time and place
To sip your lips so fruity.
I’d teach you better things in life
Than carving soapsuds with a knife;
I’m sure you’d make a stunning wife.”
Said she: “I’d do my duty.”
She liked the sound of “stunning wife,”
And said: “I’d do my duty.”
The tempter turned at this reply
(He yearned to clinch the matter).
Quoth he: “One sip before I buy!
The wedding fuss and clatter
Is not a thing you want to rush.
We could be married —-Now, don’t blush!”
But with the foamy shaving brush
The lady stopped his chatter.
In Bertie’s mouth she pushed the brush,
And stopped his lawless chatter.

IN BLUNDERLAND: THE KANGAROO AND THE CARPENTER.
The Kangaroo: “Here, what are you doing? What are you burning the lot for? Are they no good?”
The Carpenter: “Oh, yes, some of ’em are just the thing; but the jack-plane had a chip out of it, so I’ve got to get a new kit—though I know
most of the new ’uns won’t be as good as the old ’uns.”

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and. to an extent, themselves ) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the
form of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse,
Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially accept-
able), Paragraphs, Letters or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS.
when the address is specified and stamps are en-
closed. But no MS. will be returned, or preserved,
unless these conditions are observed. When MS. is
forwarded without stamps for return it will be un-
derstood that the copy is to be destroyed if not ac-
ceptable. Personal applications for return of MS.
cannot receive attention. The Bulletin desires,
further, those sending MS. to distinctly understand
that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 3, 1911.
WHEN THE GREAT WAR COMES.
The Spaniard lias a reputation for putting
off inconvenient jobs till the next day; but
the Australian has beaten him out of sight
by postponing nearly all his unpleasant-
nesses till the next generation. The ex-
planation of the tendency or folly or crime,
or whatever it may be, is that the men who
rose to the top, politically and financially,
in the past were often only here for what
they could get out of the country. They
hurried back “home” as soon as they had
made their pile, or looked forward to doing
so as soon as the pile arrived. Citizens of
that sort were not likely to be concerned
abopt Australia's ultimate destiny, for they
had no ‘intention of sharing it. The "old
spirit is still at work in a modified degree.
It accounted for a good deal of the hostility
that was shown lately to universal service
and an Australian navy. “Why,” these
people argued (and reasonably enough, from
their point of view) —“why should we be
taxed' now in order to avert a problem-
atical invasion whjeh anyway is too far off
to affect our personal interests?” Only last
week T. Waddell, an ex-Treasurer of
N.S.W., put the sentiments of Australia’s
Tooths, Wilsons, Coopebs, etc., as fol-
lows :
He understood the Federal Government was going
to spend £5,000,000 a year upon defence, dragged
out of the pockets of the people by taxation. If
this were so, it would be only a short time before
the p.eople hurled such a set of incompetents from
office.
On the same principle the same gang has
fiercely opposed all the ameliorative (or, as
the “Liberal” party has it, “Socialistic”)
legislation, which in most civilised countries
is recognised now to be the only barrier
against Revolution. As the Waddell breed
of patriot views it, it is worth sowing the
seeds of future anarchy, in return for un-
taxed land and cheap labor in the present.
And the borrowing craze, as a result of
which the Australian State Governments
now owe over 200 millions sterling—equal to
upwards of £5B per head of the population
—is a direct result of the same unpatriotic
point of view.
Ever since the first Australian politician
learned how to pawn clothes, mortgage real
estate, draw bills of sale and negotiate
I O U’s, he and his successors have been en-
thusiastically doing all those things with
the public assets, and passing on the
liabilities to generations unborn. Railway
carriages and sleepers and engines which
have been on the scrapheap for decades;
public buildings which are no more; free
seed wheat for anti-Socialist farmers,_ and
free picnics for anti-Socialist politicians;
telegraph poles which the white ant has
eaten; roads which are now quagmires—all
were provided by Israel at so much per
cent. In fact, Australia’s frenzied pawn-
shop frequenters (and among the most in-
corrigible and reckless of them was, quaintly
enough, the very Waddell who now objects
to the Federal Government’s defence ex-
penditure) have behaved to this country
after the fashion of the scandalous male
parent of melodrama who turns over to his
successor a house which is outwardly fair
but which inwardly is rotten with debt and
bailiffs. Australia only reached manhood,
in a national manner of speaking, the other
day, and it has yet to learn in the bitter
school of experience the wrong its forebears
have done it. Unless it has such luck as no
other country ever had, it must assimilate
its lesson soon.
* * * *
The great peril which menaces the
majority of the Australian States (apart
altogether from the possibility of invasion)
is the likelihood of England going to war
within the next few years. N. S. Wales,
for example, in addition to the £9,884,508
which it must hand the mostly-foreign
money-lender next year, will have to find
£12,826,200 some time in 1917 and
£16,698,065 in 3923. It may be that by
1917 England will have entered on its death-
struggle with Germany. It is almost as
certain as anything can be that the great
trouble will come within the lifetime of the
present generation. No nation, not even
Germany with its teeming prosperity and
its 62 millions of inhabitants, can afford to
go on indefinitely piling up the - armament
bills. And, on present appearances, finality
in this regard can only be reached by war.
One way in which the period of interna-
tional conflict may be fixed is by estimating
how long Germany must continue arming
and training in order to reach such a stand-
ard of efficiency and strength as will justify
it in assailing Britain. And there is the
further consideration that Britain, as a
matter of self-preserVation, may elect not to
wait. At present, while the United King-
dom is at peace and seasons are prosperous
and money is cheap, it is comparatively easy
for N.S.W. to raise funds to meet its obli-
gations, at 3 per cent, discount or there-
abouts, on the London money market,
though even now the underwriters are fre-
quently left with a large proportion of a
loan on their hands. But, assuming that in
any one of the years when the State has
to' make a heavy repayment, an Anglo-
German war breaks out, the position will be
very different. N.S.W. will then be offered
the alternatives of borrowing at the old
shop at anything up to 20 per cent, dis-
count ; or of approaching America, or some
other foreign nation (which, in view of the
possible downfall of the one country that
stands between the Commonwealth’s mort-
gaged assets and their annexation by Japan,
will certainly be none too willing to lend) ;
or of repudiation. The odds are all in favor
of repudiation. The politicians, who in-
stinctively suggest the pawnshop whenever a
bridge or railway extension or wooden post-
office of Burrinjuck outing has to be paid
for, do not ponder on the horrors of life in
a bankrupt State. Every individual citizen
is hit by a national bankruptcy as hard as
each member of a family is hit when the
father files his schedule. In those days the
banks will be driven to hurriedly call in
their overdrafts in the hope of averting
ruin. In the improbable event of Aus-
tralia being in the midst of one of the amaz-
ingly good seasons it has enjoyed of late,
some of the banks’ customers may be able
to square their accounts; in the other event
they will go broke. Most of them will go
broke, anyhow. They will not burst alone.
Shops, pubs, theatrical cos., restaurants will
all follow suit. The big city landlord will
fare as badly as the small ba deblock
trader, for tenants will be too hard up to
pay rents. Land values will slump, and
ferry and gas and sugar shares will follow
suit; and the maddened capitalist won’t
even have the dreary comfort of ascribing
the calamity to Labor legislation. N.S.W.
has merely been cited here because it was
the first horrible example that came to mind.
Victoria has over 36 millions to find in the
next 14 years, and Queensland must pro-
vide £U,72S,SO0 # in 1914.
In the first place, the States ought to in-
sist on their Governments ceasing to run up
the foreign interest bill. They should also
insist on proper provision being made to
pay off the liabilities that have been already
incurred. Westralia and South Australia
are the only States which have anything
worth calling a sinking fund. N. S. Wales,
with the stupendous debt of over 90 millions,
has no real sinking fund at all, and neither
have Victoria and Queensland. If, however,
the wicked principle is accepted that the
States must go on paying their way by
pawning their socks, so to speak (and since
State Labor Governments have shown
themselves as frantic borrowers as
“Liberal” ones, the calamity may as well
be accepted for the moment, for all the
good that protest does), then the people
should at least insist that we should give our-
selves our own lOU instead of giving it to
a foreigner. A country which is dependent
on the foreigner for food or clothes or coal
is in a dangerous way, and the same applies
to money. Admittedly there has been a disposi-
tion of late years to raise'more and more
loans in Australia. Thus, while in 1901 as
much as 80 per cent, of the public debt of
Australia was held abroad, now less than 73
per cent, of it is held outside the Common-
wealth. This country would benefit wonder-
fully if it held not merely 27 per cent., but
97 per cent, of its own debt. The issue of
Treasury Bills, after the British mode],
should accelerate the movement. They would
absorb much of the fixed deposit money which
now goes to the banks. A deposit receipt
can’t be transferred like a bank-note, whereas
a Treasury Bill can: the Government is the
best of guarantors, and for a host of similar
reasons the Bills would be as popular as
are the articles the “Liberal” press brightly
termed “Fisher’s flimsies” a few months
ago. It is clear that something drastic must
soon be done to ensure that, if further money
is borrowed by the States, it, at least, shall
not be added to Australia’s outrageous for-
eign debt. States whose financial lives de-
pend on the hideously uncertain peace of
Europe are bad risks these days.
ON THE MAKING OF THE CITY
BEAUTIFUL
From an American lawyer at present in
Australia: —
At this time, when various plans are being sug-
gested for the resumption of lands in Sydney, and
there is a certainty of Australia having to face the
problem of a national capital, it may be of interest
to note how things are done elsewhere, and why. You
hear a great deal about the American millionaire,
our dash and our sudden growth ; and occasionally I
am asked: “Is it so, that you have lawns and no
fences between your houses?” What you do not hear of
is the common, garden variety of American, the mil-
lions of him; his struggles, hopes and ambitions; his
strivings after culture, happiness and home. For,
above all, the States is a place of homes. The for-
eignor—the real one, I mean —laughs at us, calls us
crude, vulgar, a sort of living cash-register and many
other things. What seems so sudden, so wonderful, to
you who are friends of our nation, to him seems
childish and crude. It’s “Look at Paris!” or this
and that other place. Well, what he misses is us.
You are a young nation, travelling much the same
road as we had to travel; and, as far as lies in my
power, I want you to know us, not as an occasional
wire may flash some new millions to Rockefeller, or
bring you word of a new scandal, but as we live and
breathe and are. A big undertaking—but we were
on the city, and to that we will stick.
* * * *
Come with me to New York —to that city in particu-
lar, because it is the biggest, is most nearly allied
with your future on your seaboard, and because I know
it best perhaps. When I was a boy New York was
hideous. Starting at the Battery, with a bit of
green and old-fashioned mostly-red colonial dwell-
ings, it went over a sea of hideousness and vulgarity
to Washington Square; patches of green at Old
Trinity and St. Paul’s, and City Hall Square; for
the rest, hideous and unintelligent! Then, above
Washington Square a sea of grim, high-storey brown-
stone houses and a park in the making. Boss Tweed
planned stupendous improvements; but his downfall
and the scandal of his motives were such as to
deaden all progress for many years. And here one
human word of a noted man, he must have been,
withal, a great one in some ways, for every important
physical improvement since then was planned by him .
Well, the first step was the forming of a municipal
art commission, entirely amateur and idealistic, at
first very impractical and unwieldy, now all-powerful
in fact and in creating and directing public senti-
ment. The activities of this commission or associa-
tion of architects, artists,-lawyers, merchants, men of
ideals and hopes, carried in every direction, forced
attention. The commission even carried the war into
the National Architect’s 'office, which was little more
than a construction department at that time, and was
putting up- hideous buildirigs that were marring the
city. Now the idea of characteristic, dignified public
buildings is a realised fact, no longer a fad of a few
“busybodies.”
* * * *
This idea of a municipal art commission has been
carried out, with variations to suit the locality and
temper of the people, throughout the Union. Cleve-
land has a permanent body seeking the best profes-
sional advice, and planning for the future. Each town
has seized the idea, and, by looking ahead, safeguards
the present, so that natural advantages —lakes, rivers,
country, mountains —are utilised and become the “pride
of the town.” Denver, Colorado, has spent thousands
in planning its plateaus and approaches to the
Rockies. And what of results? Let us go back to
New York. I was talking to a friend, highly travelled
and cultured, who occasionally “goes through’ there,
and he said: “Really very fine, those tall buildings,
very imposing! But, frankly, the rest is rather a
fad, isn’t it?” “Haven’t been above 42nd, have
you?” “Well, to a theatre once or twice.” And
there were my childhood impressions, on a fresh
mind! But had the friend taken a ’bus or a taxi he
might have gone through the Park down 72nd ; up
Riverside, an unequalled drive, park and playground ;
through trees, flowers and beautiful vistas ; on to the
La Fayette Boulevard, and to more parks and drive-
ways. As planned, there will in time be 62 miles of
beauty before my friend will be back to the 59th
again, not all of it finished, but all of it planned:
and, what there is of it, most beautiful, and all the
work of 20 years or less. But the beauties of that drive,
or the Lake drive in Chicago, or the park on the slopes
of the Rockies in Denver, are nothing compared with
the actual public sentiment, and the strivings after
beauty in private homes and public and office build-
ings. It has become a question of. “How will it
look on our block f” Even the speculator is awed,
or forced, for his goods are unsaleable if garish or
out of tune. Senator Clarke built a monstrosity
on sth Avenue, in the centre of what the foreigner
calls our vulgar millions; and the price of the op-
posite corner went down $200,000, and the propertv
was affected for some distance either way. Facts:
plain, cold facts. On the other hand, Standard Oil
interests, represented by the U.S. Realty Co., put up
an office building adjoining Trinity Churchyard, and
it is so in keeping with the sacred place as to seem
to have always belonged there. Don’t think I am
trying to tell you we are perfect. We ard not. As
Choate said of Stanford White, “He has a great
deal of taste—some of it good!” But I do say we
are striving, and that is the first step towards suc-
cess.
I have attempted to show how a movement started
here and there by a few big-minded men has gradually
forced action through public opinion, and has suc-
cessfully guided and helped that opinion until it has
swept the country and made men think for them-
selves, the idealist to study harder, the man of busi-
ness to realise the present value and future great
benefit of this idea, until each town wants precedence,
and each man, eacli home, wants to co-operate to se-
cure it. And then you have the result —our fenceless
avenues of trees and lawns, and our commons and
gardens of smaller towns ; and the multitude of othei
things, and the more things that are to be. We have
supreme confidence in ourselves and in each other—
at least as a body. It is every man to the wheels
and no backwash from anybody. The artist nature is
a generous nature, a giving nature. It feeds on
what it does, and grows and grows. We owe much to
it And in that, as in all other parts of the country s
real greatness, loom our women. If the .business
man follows and approves, it only makes the change
the easier, the quicker and the more thorough.
* * * *
This brings me to the only other practical sugges-
tion that occurs to me out of our experience, and
that is the innumerable associations we have for the
purpose of improving and safeguarding our own im-
mediate neighborhood. hot New York only, but
everv town and village, has its own provincial society
in some guise or other. Often social is
mixed with other aims, or some other form of interest
is offered. But always we remember this, that no
liodv of men, however well intentioned be they legis-
lature, civic council, or art and improvement com-
mission-can possibly take the interest or have the
knowledge that you have in your own immediate sur-
roundings. We don’t make the mistake of thinkim,
we are too big, or too little, or too many, or too few.
Or.e determined man can do more harm or good than
?0 “don’t cares,” and he will soon get 10 or 20 or
more to help him. Work ? Of course it means
work. But it is pleasant work as it grows and results
show. In the first place, your associates are mostly
cultured or high-thinking people. Interested? Well,
why not? Their interest is yours, and, as time goes
on, and you find that you have better roads, better
kerbs and sidewalks, freer air, more trees, and more
feeling of interest in the proprietorship, your pride
in your neighbor grows, and much that was hard be-
comes easy, much that was meddlesome becomes a
matter of course. At home it started in all sorts of
ways, alike in country and city. Perhaps some
“fussy” old lady put baskets tip for papers and scraps.
Perhaps some artist thought the new Town Hall (20
x 30) looked like a coffin—who knows? Perhaps a
wo’gl of praise from some great one. But it started;
and from the beginning came —everything!
Sydney alone has dozens of points, and beautiful
ones, but, so far as I know, not one local committee
looking after its neighborhood. This is not criti-
cism ; I don’t want it to be. But I want to say
that the results will come if a start is made. True,
you must get it out of your system that you can do
things overnight. Heartburns, struggles, the politi-
cian’s “glad hand,” the real-estate operator’s frowns
and sneers —all and more have had to be met and
overcome with us. and they have been met and
overcome, not at first by the man seeking to make
money, but by the man and woman seeking to make
a home, and to make it attractive and happy. That
is the States as it really is. Home, home, everlasting
home.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Some More Political Sportsmanship.
0. G. Wade’s latest censure motion —an
effort which both the Sydney dailies had
reprobated as “foolish,” “blundering,” “un-
generous,” and so forth —acquired a wholly
unexpected measure of justification by the
eleventh-hour resignation of two Laborites.
It was lost, all the same. Nevertheless,
pending the return of two new legislators,
there was no likelihood of the McGowen or
any other Government being able to carry
on with dignity, in the face of set hostility;
and so Holman suggested a truce. And C.
G. Wade, the alleged patriot, the gentleman
who last April based much of his claim to
public support on the alleged fact that lie
was “a sportsman,” firmly declined to agree
to anything of the sort. It is easy to pic-
ture what “Liberalism” would have said
to a Laborite who acted similarly in a like
position. There would have been a con-
certed howl concerning the professional poli-
tician’s criminal indifference to the public
welfare when office was in the balance. The
fact that a heap of urgent business is wait-
ing to be done by someone would have been
mentioned. "What earthly justification,” it
would have been passionately demanded,
“has a politician to hold up the entire
machinery of government in order to secure
a petty personal advantage, when crowds of
non-contentious measures —the Criminal
Appeal Bill, the Consolidation of the Lands
Act, etc. —are clamoring to be handled?'
Labor’s sickening want of sportsmanship
would have been animadverted on blister-
ingly. With fancy’s ear one can listen to
“Liberalism” expounding the position as
follows : “Here is a Government 44 strong,
despite its' two defections. The Opposition
numbers only 37. And there are seven In-
dependents, whose sympathies, if their past
votes and protestations go for anything, are
with the Government. The Opposition
leader has no more hope of carrying on the
business of the House, as it is at present
constituted, than he has of drinking Sydney
Harbor dry. His grab at a position on
which, he has no shadow of a claim is an
unusually disreputable party move, inspired
by the fact that certain campaigning advant-
ages accrue to the party which is in power
when an election begins.” The N.S.W.
Liberal party said none of these things last
week, for the reason that its own leader was
the offender. In his frantic manoeuvrings ( to
push Holman into some sort of an awkward
hole at any price, Wade has shown up in a
sorrier light, perhaps, than ever before.
He was foremost in urging ex-Premier
McGowen to go to the Coronation, and
loudest in his assurances that nothing un-
toward would happen to the party as a re-
sult of its leader doing “his duty by the
State.” Yet McGowen was hardly on the
blue water before Wade was trying to oust
his administration (a move which was de-
plored even by the party press) ; and now
McGowen has been ousted. Yet last Satur-
day, the politician who has broken every
rule of the game for the last two months,
had the nerve to snuffle in this fashion :
I do not desire to discuss the general situation, be-
yond referring to the attitude of the Labor party and
the late Government, now that, according to their
own admission, they are unable to carry on, and have
resigned accordingly.
One would expect that in these circumstances, they
would adopt the ordinary attitude of an Englishman
and take their defeat'., like men. Australians are
lovers of sport and fair play, and no, real sportsman
can approve of such tactics.
It suggests a third-rate Yankee, pug chiding
an adversary for sharp ring practices.
A Matter of 4d. per Week.
A month ago, when the text of the British
National Insurance Bill arrived in Austra-
lia, this paper remarked :
One thing that is going to kill the Lloyd-George
scheme in its present form is the circumstance or
tragedy that three-fourths of Britain’s indigent
toilers, unlike those of Germany, do not earn enough
to give them a hope of finding even the paltry sum
which is required to earn the Government contribu-
tion.
In the last few days the English Laborites.
who greeted the Bill with cheers on its first
appearance, have changed front; and Labor-
leader Ramsay Macdonald has made re-
peated demands that the State shall assume
various of the burdens that the Government
proposed to put on the employees. f ol , l '
night ago Tim Healy, speaking at Cork,
said it was “exceedingly unfair to dock 4u.
per week from the miserable wages ot tm
Irish laborer.” And a little later the cab* 1
told us:—
Mr. ivEiR Hardie, M.P. (Labor), states that the
National Insurance Bill is doomed. The defects a =
closed in the measure are more serious than r
were at first imagined.
It is more eloquent of the hopelessly ston p
broke condition of Britain’s submerg
third than a thousand “hunger marcfie.
could be. Never did a Government lay
self out to do more for a body of P e °P
outside the pauper class. To achieve
benefits offered, male employees were cal
on to contribute only 4d. a week,
females 3d. a week. The State and the -
ployers, between them, were to find tne


maining cash necessary to supply 15 million
people with, among other things—
(l) Free medical attendance for life.
(2) 10s. a week in sickness for men and 7s. 6d.
for women for the first 13 weeks, and thereafter ss.
a week for both.
(3) A benevolent’ fund.
(4) A convalescent allowance.
Germany, where the alleged Curse of Pro-
tection has been in operation since ISSO,
has enjoyed a National insurance scheme
for upwards of two generations. The main
difference between the German arrangement
and that which has been proposed for Bri-
tain is that under the former the employer
and employees find the entire cost of the
contributions between them, while under
Lloyd-George’s proposal the State offers to
contribute two-ninths of the insurance
money in the case of the men, and one-
fourth in the case of the women. And even
with that vast subsidy the Freetrade worker
sees no hope of saving the few pence weekly
that are asked of him!
Wanted a Statue!
Randolph Bedford makes a call:—
Not a. statue only, but all it means —intelligent ap-
preciation of effort, love of the real leader, and re-
cognition of duty well done. And these are the
things which in their most eloquent expression Aus-
tralia has most ungratefully forgotten. Man does
not live by bread alone: hero worship must be. And
if ever a country had heroes, and, careless of them,
went lusting after false visions, that country is our
own.
A statue should have but one of two considera-
tions —the praise of beauty, or the immortalisa-
tion of worth. And these two considerations con-
demn almost every sculpture which we have in Aus-
tralia. The Sydney statues of Cook, Phillip and
Bourke are well enough; those of Albert and of
Victoria are of value neither to art nor to Australia,
and never were. Chinese Gordon, in Melbourne,
means nothing; Burke and Wills, the well-equipped
showmen who failed where dozens of ill-equipped
at&l unadvertised Australians have succeeded, are
nothing. Fitzgibbon, looking at the city that paid
him very well for being its town clerk, is as ridicu-
lous as the man was kindly and energetic; and Bat-
sman, whose pioneering work brought him less than
easne to Fitzgibbon in half-a-year’s salary, is for-
gotten. Flinders, the greatest of the world’s hydro-
gfaphers, ife a large creditor of Australia’s gratitude,
and has n6t been paid; Torres and de Quiros,
and AbEl Tasman and Dampier and D’Entre-
casteaux, Captain Sturt, McDoualt Stuart,
and Kennedy, and Mitchell, and Hovell, and Hume
are more to Australia than all the Victorias and
painfully good Alberts could ever be; and these
creditors have not been paid either.
The other day a rich and swollen Melbourne fund
materialised in the unveiling of a statue to Hope-
toun, a kindly, loveable man, an unostentatious figure-
head, whose virtues shone the brighter because of
their immediate comparison with the uncertainties
of Brassey. Hopetoun, the first Governor-General
of Australia, cumbereth the ground; in Melbourne
Charles Cameron Kingston is as forgotten as John
Batman, the currency lad who found so much that the
English had missed 31 years before him.
Everything given to the Hopetoun statue fund is a
misuse of money while Higinbotham remains only
an intangible memory. The class that subscribed
to the one would probably never pay for the other —••
the snobs have not yet forgiven the great jurist his
illuminating phrase in describing them as “the
wealthy lower orders.” But there are others, the real
people of the real Australia, who know that for the
Australia-yet-to-be Eureka was much more important
than Trafalgar.
Does the new-generation Australian know the man
Higinbotham as he was? The unswerving man of
great brain, high courage and tender heart, who chal-
lenged Constitution and Empire when he knew that
'so to do was right? The man who‘earned the hatred
of the ignorant monied class which ejected him from
Brighton, and chose Bent, much as the Jews chose
Barabbas? For the people are often Pilates,
and Barabbas has been chosen more than once.
Higin BOTHAM’S private life was sanctuary; his
public acts showed the same stainless purity.
He was incorruptible, yet with genius; simple, but
dashing and energetic; courageous, cultured and re-
sourceful. In a country where almost all forceful
men were narrow-brained and book-ignorant, he met
all adversaries at all points, and vanquished them;
but though he won, yet in the end money and cun-
ning wore out honesty and singleheartedness, for,
as in all time, a coward can deal a mortal blow to
the brave. He was beaten, and the land boom came
to show how right he had been, although, by then, the
fact that his defeat had been owing to the votes of
men who were themselves land-boomers was forgotten.
As a lawyer he put his life into the colossal labor of
codifying the statutes, in itself a monument, but one
felt more than seen. His contribution to the 1890
strike funds marked him more and more for the
hatred of the class he had cut to the bone with his
just gibe. A leading man of the agent class, on the
day the donation was published, proposed, amid gen-
eral applause, that the Chief Justice should be torn
from the bench by a company of artillery, and shot, as
a reward for his sympathy for the strikers. And
that fact should be sufficient to make every trades
unionist in Australia put up his shilling or half-crown
for a permanent memoral to Higinbotham.
Student, scholar, statesman, journalist, publicist,
jurist, and Parliamentarian, he gave his State his
best, and in giving it to his State he gave it to the
Commonwealth. His work belongs not to Victoria,
but to Australia. How many Australians are ready
to recognise the splendid life of a man the ancients
would have deified —for he was statesman and hero
both? Thousands of pounds are subscribed for well-
advertised people Australia never knew, and to futili-
ties who never mattered much to anybody. How
much is ready for a memorial to the infinitely great?
How much?
An Official Whose Ukase is Law —and Not
Always Good Law.
The N.S.W. Stamp Commissioner holds his
appointment under the Stamp Duties Act,
and it is his business to collect the duties
payable on all conveyances, transfers, pro-
bates and so forth. A couple of cases in-
volving large sums which recently came be-
fore the Supreme Court exemplified, in a
rather more glaring fashion than usual, the
dangerous powfer this official enjoys, and
likewise his arbitrary methods of using it.
In the one case, the Commissioner claimed
£7OOO stamp-diity, and in the other a some-
what smaller sum. The beneficiaries and
their legal advisers were agreed that the
Commissioner had no legal right to claim a
farthing; and that view was subsequently
upheld by the. Supreme Court. But they
were forced to pay the sum demanded of
them on the nail —as they would have been
forced to pay had it been three, four or 10
times as much—under penalty (1) of having
the deeds in the estates impounded, and the
whole business in connection therewith held
up indefinitely, and (2) of being fined
heavily for their recalcitrancy. In the case
of wealthy estates, such as the ones in ques-
tion, the injustice done is comparatively
trivial. Under the Act anyone who dis-
agrees with the assessment of the Commis-
sioner may call upon him to state a special
case, as a preliminary to referring the dis-
pute to the Supreme Court. The big man
plunges into litigation more or less cheer-
fully when he has strong reason to believe
his cause is just. Not so the poor one.
Haunted by the memory of a Wade
Government which pursued one of its
own servants to the Privy Council over
a question of a few shillings, he possesses
a healthy dread of going to Law with
“the Crown” ; and there is the further con-
sideration that the poor man may find him-
self so situated that he cannot go to law
even if he wants to. A man, for example,
dies with assets worth £20,0()0 and liabili-
ties amounting to £19,000. The Commis-
sioner puts in a claim for stamp-duty which
the executors cannot pay. But if they
don’t pay they are unable to get probate,
and the estate, consequently, can’t be real-
ised, or otherwise dealt with. Even assum-
ing, in such circumstances, that the execu-
tors managed to raise the sum demanded,
they would still require to wait months be-
fore their case came before the Supreme
Court, and also they would have to find a
big sum of ready money to meet current law
costs. When “the Crown” litigates it in-
variably briefs a strong bar, and, in general,
proceeds regardless of expense. It is not
strange, therefore, that in nine cases out of
10 the citizen who has been held up by a
demand for stamp duty which he more than
suspects to be illegal, yields miserably rather
than take his chance of financial ruin by
being dragged possibly as far as the Privy
Council in the matter. The Stamp Com-
missioner’s office is a section of the Civil
Service which fairly shrieks for reform. The
province of the Government is not to tear
revenue out of all and sundry regardless of
equity, and that being so, it is altogether
desirable that a legal expert should be
added to the strength, of this department.
His status should be that of a District Court
Judge or Master in Equity, and it should be
his province to arbitrate in all disputed
assessments between the Commissioner and
the public. His appointment should, of
course, be for life, thus rendering him inde-
pendent of the Government of the day. and
his decision should be f final. It is only by
some reform’along these lines that a parti-
cularly evil - ; state of things is going to be
remedied.
A Quack Cure for an Old Complaint.
A one-eyed professor - of practical politics,
named Lawson, M.L.A., is urging the Vic.
Government to assist manufactures in inland
centres by ordering railway concessions in
freight for raw material and finished goods,
which demand is supported by the con-
tention of Bowser, M.L.A., and others, that
“metropolitan determinations with regard to
wages and conditions of work cannot be ex-
tended to country districts unless substantial
reductions are made in fares and freights.”
Setting aside all question as to whether
the Railway Department charges op-
pressive rates or otherwise, it seems that
according to Lawson and Co., decentralisa-
tion in Victoria can be best effected by
legislating for the encouragement of inland
manufactures which would find their market
mostly in Melbourne. Because Melbourne
contains half the population of the State,
and is the greedy port where all the raw
material from abroad is unloaded, the rail-
way freights between there and certain inland
towns should, it is alleged, be greatly re-
duced —if not abolished —on behalf of de-
centralisation. As regards other country
districts afar off, it is obvious that goods
from inland manufacturing centres could
reach them more cheaply than from the
bloated metropolis; therefore a future Mel-
bourne demand for “encouraging” conces-.
sions in railway freights would be just as
logical as the present cry from Lawson’s
own particular Maryborough. Meanwhile,
Melbourne’s manufacturing interests continue
to thrive on the density of its own local popu-
lation and the lack of powerful outside com-
petition, which latter is due to the paucity
of up-country population. The Government’s
only effective way of encouraging manufac-
turers ,in inland centres can be by putting
people on the land, and opening up new
ports—natural ports —in opposition to the
Melbourne port that has stolen their natural
commercial rights. The idea bf charging the
Government railways with the cost of ereat-
ing false manufacturing centres —even if it
could be done under present Melbourne con-
ditions —is worthy only of little local minds.

“Wireless.”
Thebe is trouble over the installation of
“wireless” in Australia; and the public is
becoming interested in the matter, on account
of the delay that the trouble is causing,
There are, it appears, two or three varieties
of “wireless,” or, rather, of “wireless” ap-
pliances. The Italian, Marconi, worked out
one system; other European scientists, re-
cognising the deficiencies of that system, pro-
duced the “quenched singing spark”; and,
after investigation, the British Admiralty
approved of the adoption of the latter sys-
tem for Australian use. There appears to be
now no question of its efficiency; that, in-
deed, has been amply proved in Australia,
and the Commonwealth Government has
raised no question on that score. The
trouble seems to be that the Marconi people,
with a natural desire to get their own ap-
pliances in use, breathe vague threats into
the ear of the Government that the use of the
singing-spark appliances may infringe the
Marconi patents; wherefore the Australian
Government hesitates. As a matter of fact,
these appliances, owned by an Australian
company, have been granted patent rights by
the Commonwealth : so we have the curious
spectacle of the Commonwealth throwing
doubt on the validity of its own patents.
But, apart from that, the Australian co. has
undertaken to indemnify the Australian Gov-
ernment against damages, in the extremely
remote event of the Marconi Co. making
good its claim. Everybody will approve of
the Government’s desire to avoid a pitfall;
but in this case there can be no danger—the
indemnity already given covers the Common-
wealth, or, if that indemnity is not judged
sufficient, then it is open to the Government
to demand some other guarantee. The
quarrel is really not between the Marconi
people and the Government, as purchaser of
the quenched singing-spark appliances ; it is
between the Marconi people and the seller
of those appliances. No action whatever has
been taken against that seller anywhere,
from which it may be reasonably assumed
that no action will lie. But, whether or no,
the Government is not concerned in that
phase of the matter. Its concern is merely
to get the best; and if its advisers say that,
especially when cost is taken into account,
the singing spark is best, then the singing
spark it ought to get, just as it ought to get
the other if the other were reported by its
experts to be best. The ridiculous thing just
at present is that the Government should
hesitate to adopt that course, for no better
reason apparently than that vague threats
and hints and alarms have been made—hints
and threats and alarms of things against
which the authorities can get complete pro-
tection. It recalls the time when the anti-
Federalists warned N. S. Wales of the dread-
ful things that might happen to it if it
helped to form a Commonwealth—a bluff that
nearly came off, but, fortunately, didn’t
quite. And in that case there was nobody
standing by who could offer a complete in-
demnity against those dreadful catastrophes,
such as there is in this case.

The Tote: Two Viewpoints.
“Pro-Book” puts the case for the Man with
the Bag:—
The N.S.W. Totalisator Bill proposes that the Gov-
ernment shall take the Tote turnover to the extent of
10 per cent.—s per cent, is to go to the Government
and 5 per cent, to the clubs which run the Tote, for
the increase of stakes and for permanent improve-
ments. Also, the clubs will take all fractions of a
shilling, which work out at 1 per cent, on the turn-
over. Therefore, the Tote is to take 11 per cent, of
all moneys invested before any dividend is declared.
Now, on an average, 198 bookmakers work the Syd-
ney metropolitan district throughout the year; eight
average £4OO a race, 20 £250, 30 £l5O, 40 £lOO,
and 100 £25, or an average total per race of £19,200
for six races each day, or a grand total of £7,488,000
for 65 days. A tax of 11 per cent, on this is
£823,680, of which the Government takes £374,400,
and the club takes £449,280. Large figures, un-
doubtedly, but substantiated by prominent members
of the ring.
Consider these facts and their bearing on the com-
munity seriatim :—
(1) The general public attends racing—a few for
the racing itself, the vast majority for the general
excitement and prospect of making money. With
the introduction of the Tote (a) the excitement and
the prospect of great gain vanish; (b) the glamor of
the noise, the joy of racing round to get the longest
odds and the satisfaction of seeing the backed horse
.shorten in price, with the possibilities of hedging, are
gone; and (c) the gambling element is reduced to
the purchase of a ticket and the automatic collection
of dividends on the winners.
(2) It is claimed the punter gets longer odds. In
practice the machine prices compare with book-
makers’ starting price as ll to 9. But before the
“books’ ” starting price is fixed, much longer odds hare
been laid; and it is clear the “book’s” average price
must be in excess of the Tote’s price.
(3) The 11 per cent, tax leaves the punters’ poc-
kets for ever, except what comes back through the
owners by way of increased stakes and through per-
sonal well-being by way of permanent improvements.
(4) The Government receives £374,400 gross.
This, under the Bill, is to be devoted to charities,
but it is not new income. The Consolidated Revenue
loses the income tax paid by the “books” and the land
tax paid on their investments.
(5) The clubs under the Bill will receive £449,280
less the cost of working the Tote. But the clubs will
lose nearly £50,000 a year in license fees, £22,000
a year in entrance fees for the “book,” his clerk and
messenger; £5OO for clerks’ registration fees, and
the decrease in the value of the bar —say. £lO a
day for 65 days = £6500; total, £79,000. Also,
there is the cost of the Tote and working exes., at
£5O a day = £2350. Grand total, £81,350.
And, lastly (6), where the Tote has been worked
for a time, as in Maoriland, what is the result?
Racing stakes increased by leaps at first, and now
are almost stationary. Tote receipts are increasing,
but not pro rata with the population. Therefore,
the public is becoming more moral, or it is tiring
of losing 11 per cent, in every investment.
To which The Bulletin replies:—
(1) (a) The fool-section of the public, which goes
racing with the object of making money out of the
almost obscenely-affluent Ring is in urgent need of
being protected against itself, (b) If the Tote is
going to do no more for N.S.W. than still the in-
fernal howls of the bookmakers, and discourage pub-
lic nuisances from galloping about in an atmosphere
of dust, profanity and rotten tobacco, in quest of
long odds, it will justify its introduction here. (c)
The probability of the Tote reducing the gambling
element among racegoers may be admitted; but the
change will leave the average citizen unperturbed.
And the “automatic” collection of dividends on win-
ners, as opposed to the existing system, under which
such dividends are often not collected at all, owing
to the “book’s” sudden departure, insolvency or what
not, will be all to the good.
(2) Undoubtedly the common or garden punter
does get longer ‘odds from the Tote. Those who
occasionally do a bit of good fo.- themselves under
the present arrangement (at the ultimate expense, in
every case, of the general public) are the “big
punters,” professional backers, commission workers,
and so on. Their operations are responsible, directly
or indirectly, for about 95 per cent, of the stiffened
horses, jockevs and trainers, and for about the same
proportion of the ground and lofty swindling gener-
ally, which disfigure the N.S.W. and Victorian turf.
(3) It is a bookmaker’s yarn, as old as it is
fallacious, that the 11 per cent. Tote tax “leaves the
punters’ pockets for ever” ; while, inferentiallv, the
percentage taken by the Ring does not. Sol Green
has invested upwards of £ 50,000 in Sydney house-
property, and £lOO,OOO in Melbourne property, in the
last few years. The money which has gone into those
investments has left the punters’ pockets as absolutely
as the cash which the smaller penciller puts into
diamonds, champagne and villas at Randwick.
(4.) “The Government... .will lose the income tax
paid by the ‘books’ and the land tax paid on their
investments.” But the “books” are not going to
become Domain-loafers when their present occupation
is gone. Presumably, they will earn money at some-
thing else. And, meanwhile, under the Bill, the
State hospitals will be £374,400 per annum to the
good.
(5) The £81,350 which “Pro-Book” claims will
be lost to the racing clubs by the abolition of the
“books,” is a somewhat inflated sum. The Tote, for
example, by attracting more racegoers, will probably
swell rather than diminish the bar-receipts; and the
expenses of the Tote are not likely to approach £5O.
a day. The machine is worked by three clerks. At
Longehamp and Auteiul the Totes are worked by
regular employees of the clubs as part of their ordin-
ary duties. In any case, it is really the N.S.W. public
which pays over £70,000 per annum for the privilege
of betting with bookmakers. Racegoers will assur-
edly accept the loss of the Ring with fortitude, pro-
vided the clubs use their new incomes—the sum
vvhich stands for the past income of the “books”—to
give the public cheaper and more comfortable racing,
increased stakes, and such-like benefits.
Item (G) doesn’t impress this paper as enough of
a tragedy to be worth controverting.

The Union Jackals who were waiting on the
wharf to drag Andrew Fisher down, and
meantime were fouling the Labor party, have
had a piece of bad luck. Fisher might have
said just about all he was reported to have
said without saying anything dreadful,
though, if he had said it, he certainly might
have said it in a better way and at a better
time; but he cuts off the jackals’ tails by re-
pudiating the whole business, and unloading
the responsibility on to Stead- —or Julia,
the spirit lady who guides Stead’s hand.
It seems about time Stead sacked Julia.

Now that they have laid down the burdens
of office, Holman, Beeby and the other State
Frighters may have time to sum up the net
results of their action during the Referenda
Campaign. Within the year they have —
(1) Defeated the biggest and best attempt to
control monopoly, aggressive capitalism, and private
company-sharking ever made by a party in this
country ;
(2) Disintegrated the great Labor party;
(3) Rehabilitated the “Liberal” press, the “Lib-
eral” party and the “Liberal” influence which had
been east down by the Federal and N.S.W. State
elections;
(4) Disheartened the rank and file of the Labor
workers, discouraged the Labor sympathisers who
make Labor Governments possible, and paralys'ed
organisation. <;
It will need a severe surgical operation to
rip out the provincialist cancer from the La-
bor movement. But it must be done, even
though in the process ten times as many pic-
turesque figures had to be sacrificed by the
scalpel.
Some time ago a few enthusiasts formed
an Anti-Asiatic League in Maoriland, and
tried to convince the white man that the
easy way to knock out the competition of
tne degrading yellow man was to quit pat-
ronising the agony. The League presently
found that, though the M.L. Government
was howling Empiah, and shouting Dread-
noughts, it wasn’t giving a hand to those
who were doing their little bit towards
strengthening the position of the white rape.
Allegedly white Labor inspectors allowed
discolored aliens to keep their fruit-shops
and laundries open when the decent white
was compelled to close, and latitude was
allowed them in other ways, on the ground,
apparently, that they were. only “blanky
Cnows,” or “asterisk Dagoes.” So the
League asked the Government about it; and
the comforting reply of Minister for Labor
Miller was that “you will no doubt under-
stand that it is quite outside the province
of any Minister to interpret an Act of Par-
liament; which is under his administration.”
It no doubt may be outside the capacity of a
Minister to interpret an Act; but if he
doesn’t try to do it, Heaven alone knows
how he is going to administer it. Appar-
ently. when it comes to giving a lift to the
white race, the M.L. Government’s idea is
that it should be so densely ignorant about
the meaning of every Act that it shouldn’t
attempt to enforce even its most obvious
provisions.
—— ♦
Bruce Smith stumbles over himself in ob-
jecting to the D.T.’s unintoxicated and un-
usually Australian view that the Brit.-Jap.
treaty-renewal is a good thing, in that it
gives Australia time to complete its defence
preparations. The fatuous old gentleman’s
belief is that all “doubts” (about Jap aggres-
sion) are now' at rest by reason of the
treaty; as if treaties had never become waste
paper; as if self-interest were not thbir
only binding agent; as if diplomacy could
not and does not break the most honorable
of understandings in the most dishonorable
of ways, and find highly virtuous reasons
for doing it! What is a treaty but a truce,
and is a truce anything but breathing space
for the armorer V “England has made ius
safe against Japan,” he says ; and there is
no truth in that either, because only our-
selves can make us safe, though the Panama
Canal can help us by helping America.
Further, Australians are “being asked to
get ready to do something which they can
never hope to be able to do” (presumably
the repulsion of an invader), and it “would
be better to preach a spirit of international
amity tow’ards our declared friends.” Natur-
ally the place to buy “the spirit of inter-
national amity” is the pub of Mr. B. and S.,
who believes in a German bogey against
England, but refuses to admit of a .jap
bogey for Australia. Finally Mr. B. and S.
has “always foreseen that Australia would
have to shake hands with Japan some day,”
which proves absolutely that he knows
nothing about the sentiment of White Aus-
tralia. He can talk of the “aggressiveness”
of a country deciding to defend itself
against aggression, and yet lie could en-
dorse the crime of the Boer war because the
criminals were not common Australians but
Heaven-born Britons. What he is doing in
an Australian Parliament no man can dis-
cover. To Australian sentiment he is as
foreign as a cinder in the eye.
During the Referenda uproar, the Weary-
Watt Ministry _and its Melbourne press
allies made frenzied appeals to the electors
to “vote No and preserve the Wages Board
system” ; and Fat men, whose hatred of any
interference with “the law of supply and
demand” was notorious, yelled their loudest
in favor of the same Boards, as against the
On the Edge of War.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
London, Friday.—The Prime Minister said the
Moroccan situation had become difficult and harass-
ing.— Cable.
The deep-sea cables have spoken
’Neath keels of the waiting fleets ;
Now the long, long truce is broken,
And rumors harry the streets.
Unarmed in the house of Britain,
Unarmed and alone we snore,
With our hist’ry yet unwritten —
And Ma on the edge of War!
A fig for the Coronation,
And the smiles of Kaiser Bill !
They gave him a fine ovation
When he drove up Tower Hill!
What is the worth of his bowing
And what of his kingly call?
Lo! here are the nations rowing!
(Last month he danced at the hall!)
The princes may intermarry,
And visit, and brag, and breed;
But it’s Capital plays Old Harry—
And Labor supplies the bleed ;
When Money is wild there’s worry,
And battleships—pretty things !
Start raising steam in a hurry,
And nobody heeds the kings.
It’s Labor must do the fighting,
Or pay for the Jingo brag,
And keep all the scribblers writing,
Each one for his screaming rag;
The spoils go not to the victors
Who fight when they get the range,
But the gold-scaled boa-constrictors
Who swallow the world on ’Change.
We wait for the clash titanic,
For the first red flash and roar,
For the fall of stocks, the panic
That follows the blast of War;
But not as a well-armed nation
In a solvent land afar;
We shake like a poor relation
’Neath the Dear Old Flag of Ma!
N.S.W.

SYDNEY MELBOURNE BRISBANE—ADELAIDE.
Anthony Horderns’ three-weeks sale began on July
31, and the end will come on August 19. It takes
a volume of about 300 pages to list the reductions
offered. Still, the book is scarcely necessary these
times; all that most people,need'nowadays is to be
told when the big annual event' starts, and on past
exoerienee they can guess the rest-*
MOTOR CARGO BOAT VOYAGE.
LAST week a Motor Cargo Boat, 40 tons
register, installed with a 40-h.p. “ INVIN-
CIBLE ” Slow Speed Heavy Duty Engine,
we have sold to Mr. J. Mackenzie, of Merinihula,
made the trip from our Works at Woolwich Dock
to that port.
There was some heavy weather south of Jervis
Bay, and Mr. Mackenzie’ writes that for some three
hours the boat was continually deluged. The
voyage took 30. hours, a good record, as the boat
was carrying a fair cargo and had to contend with
such rough weather.
Mr. Mackenzie concludes : “ A feature of the trip
was the easy running of the engine, whose CLOCK-
WORK REGULARITY never ceased throughout
the entire journey of over 208 miles, doing all that
was asked of it in a highly satisfactory manner.”
