Federal Arbitration Courts. Just about that
time the Weary Government was trying to
establish a few more of the blessings; and
it managed fairly well so long as the bless-
ings were confined to the towns. It hap-
pened, however, that two of them splashed
out into the country and dealt with mining
and dairying. Both these proposals excited
the House of Fat; but eventually they were
passed, though the Dairying Board’s opera-
tions were limited to workers in butter and
similar factories. That was several months
ago; but, up to date, there has been no sign
of the constitution of a Dairying Board. It
requires an Order-in-Council to set things
moving, and apparently Watt is either too
tired or too Tory to get the formal order
signed. Yet this Wages Board is urgently
needed. There are 221 butter-factories and
creameries in the State, and only about 21
of them pay decent wages and observe fair
conditions. In addition, many new milk
factories are starting, and a big German co.
is going to begin making casein out of skim
milk all over the Western District. Also,
there are numbers of big private cheese fac-
tories. It is merely a matter of justice
that these new and projected concerns shall
be told without delay what wages they will
have to pay and what conditions they will
have +0 observe. The time is thus certainly
“ripe” for the establishment of the pro-
mised Board; and since Watt and his
friends gave the system such an unqualified
blessing last April, they can have no excuse
for not hurrying along with it.
♦
C. G. Wade is the N.S.W. Labor party’s
best asset at present. lie is so cantanker-
ous, so blundering, so utterly impossible as a
leader of the opposing party. Nothing but
blind cantankerousness could have been re-
sponsible for the bad break he made last week.
After two members of the almost evenly
divided House had resigned, Holman asked
for a truce till the vacant seats were filled.
A leader who knew how to make the best
of a poor position would have promptly con-
sented ; and up would have gone his reputa-
tion as a “sport.” But Wade, the champion
blunderer,, snarled a refusal. Holman, of
course, had him in a cleft stick. “Then
form a Government of your own!” he said ;
and Wade was sent for. At just about that
point Wade woke up to his own folly.
With no possible hope of carrying on, he
had to decline the Commission. In other
words, while he declined to allow Holman
to carry on the business of the country, he
refused to carry it on himself, because he
couldn’t. In the circumstances there was
nothing for it but for the Lieut.-Governor to
send for Holman, tell him in effect that
Wade was a person who threw bricks and
then scuttled round the corner, and ask
Holman to carry on on precisely the terms
which he had asked for at the very first —-
that is, with the help of a prorogation till
the vacancies had been filled. No wonder
that even the Daily Telegraph draws
Wade’s attention to the hole where lie
fell in, and remarks: “The Government’s
strategy has been entirely successful.”
The N.S.W. political tangle screeches once
more for the Elective Ministry. Only a few
months ago the country was put to the
great expense of a general election. The
McGowen Government then arrived, and
Ministers tackled departments of which they
had previously no personal knowledge, except
as outsiders. After six months they prob-
ably begin to know something about the
work and the executive officers. Now, how-
ever, though the Labor Minister of, say, Edu-
cation may have proved himself a better ad-
ministrator than any of his predecessors for
20 years past, and though it would be a
great advantage to the State to keep him in
the job, he must go out if a bare majority of
the members do not agree with one fragment
of the policy of one Minister. Even the general
policy of that one Minister may have the
cordial approval of a majority of the House,
which may also have the highest opinion
of him as an administrator; but the ninetv-
and-nine things don’t count in the stupid
game of party politics—the one thing counts
for everything. The stupidity didn’t matter
much, perhaps, when there were no great
business departments of State; but, to-day,
the State is a huge commercial concern, and
this constant shuffling of portfolios, and their
allotment with hardly any regard to the
personal fitness of the holders to do the solid
work of business management, must have a
most serious effect. How, for instance, would
a business house in the city fare if all the
heads of departments or even all the
directors were liable to be tossed out at a
moment’s notice, because one of them pro-
posed to do something of which the share-
holders disapproved? And how much worse
would it fare if there were a hard-and-fast rule
that, no matter how supremely able one or
more of those men were, they should not be
qualified to go back to their old places on the
re-shuffle ?
Last week an Adelaide Civil servant, wear-
ing an 1.5.0. label, announced his intention
of “pistolling with his own hand” anyone
who was responsible for the hauling down of
the Union .Tack in Australian waters. Ac-
cording to a cable message (31/7/T1). one
of the terms of the Naval Agreement is
The ships of a Dominion shall fly a White Ensign
as the emblem of the authority of the Crown, with
the' Dominion’s own flag at the .jaekstaff.
And the jaekstaff is where the U. Jack has
flown hitherto. If the S. Aus. Jingo is other
than an utterer of sound and blither, signi-
fying nothing, the lives of Asquith and his
colleagues shouldn’t be worth more than a
few weeks’ purchase.

11. B. Irving will be seen in “Louis XI.”
at Her Majesty’s .(Sydney) next Saturday.
Modern thinkers hold that the eleventh
Louis was, among other things, the first oi
the Wowsers; and, indeed, Sir Henry
Irving’s portrait of the monarch, gibbering
insanely to the silver images on his hat, re-
called, vividly, the fanatical parson wor-
shipping the futile gods Sabbatarianism,
Teetotalism, etc. This role is said, by the
London critics, to be one of the finest stage
things that H. B. Irving does.
** * *
On Saturday night William Anderson will
break out in three places simultaneously in
Sydney—-pantomime, at the Palace; drama,
at the Criterion; monologuery, at the
Y.M.C.A. Asked what a Person was to do
about it, the new three-card man said a
Person could (1) take his choice and send
his family round to the other place; (2)
book a seat for each of the shows, and, with
the aid of a taxi-cab engaged for the night,
see a little of them all; (3) draw lots for
it. Which doesn’t seem to relieve a Per-
son’s predicament.
A MAID OF HONOR at HER MAJESTY’S, SYDNEY
The Maid (Dorothea Baird) appeals for her lover's life. Cromwell (Frank Tyars) refuses it
“The Power of the Cross,” the new' melo-
drama at the Adelphi, is a weird mixture of
mystery and sensation, the weirdness being
mostly supplied by a villainous half-cousin of
the dreadful vampire. ' This cheerful gentle-
man, Baron Woulfe Curzola —had devils
all these foreigners are! —wanders up and
down seeking whom he may devour; and be-
fore he is finally stiffened by the “Power of
the Cross,” he has bagged six victims, three
of whom die. Incidentally, there is one mur-
der, one case of arson, several attempts at
homicide, and a forgery. The plot thickens
through; the villainy of Rita Lascelles, and
culminates in her death (another of the
little jobs of the vampire), followed (as the
proprieties demand) by the end of Curzola.
H. W. Diver is the villainous vampire of
foreign extraction, and it is a part he could
play in' his sleep. Robert Inman hasn’t
much chance as Gerald Fairfax, the good
young man who is lured to destruction by
the bad woman; Cecil Mannering does well
as Leslie Fairfax, especially in the lost-
memory scene; Timothy O’Dowd and Dolly
Gray (J. P. O’Neill and Ethel Buckley) are
the two funny persons who are not victims
of the vampire, which show's how malevolent
a vampire can be: Margaret Seaton (Louise
Hampton) and Blanche Kingsley (Essie
Clay) suffer in silence; and Nellie Fergusson,
as Rita Lascelles, the villainess, is respon-
sible for most of the dreadful trouble.

“Sergeant Brue” yields up his place at
Sydney Criterion to “The Man from Out-
back” on Saturday next. The outback per-
son is one of those who pass their stage life
in an atmosphere of frantic excitement—a
hero to whom firearms and assault and bat-
tery are as necessary as the breath or life or
salary.

“Balkan Princess” at Sydney Theatre Royal
next Saturday.
Lmx * *
Joseph Blascheck makes a hurried de-
scent on. Sydney with William Anderson as
engineer-—which shows that Anderson aims
at steering more than.one kind of aeroplane.
Upon an Australian foundation Blascheck
has built a solid European reputation as a
monologue entertainer; and with the help
of Miss Mildred Wrighton he will give the
first of his Mel B. Spurr-like shows at the
Y.M.C.A. Hall on Saturday night.
*# # *
Millie Doris continues to be a tower of
strength and a blizzard of mirth at the
National Amphitheatre. Other attractions
are McGee and Reece: the Yagges, skilled
ball-smiters ; and the Piquays, one of whom
edifies and instructs by dancing on his hands.
m* * *
Lamberti, the musical impersonator, with
his fine library of whiskers, supplies the
chief novelty of the week at Sydney Tivoli.
As Liszt and Paderewski, he ripples the
keys of the yawning grand ; as Hollmann, he
embraces a ’cello; and, as Sarasate, he lov-
ingly nurses the ’cello’s little brother. This
sort of turn, besides entertaining, is an ob-
ject lesson on the importance of Hair; the
difference between one celebrity and another
never seems to be more than a pair of eye-
brows, a slight rearrangement of the roof
garden, and a handful of whiskers. The
success of Pastor and Merle, a mixed eccen-
tric team, is principally due to the male
partner, who tumbles entertainingly, and
gives a joyous imitation of Sousa. The bird-
like Alcide Capitaine, Ted Kalman, Binns
and Binns, Fanny Powers, and the Austra-
lian Dartos are the other headliners. The
bioscope assists with some interesting
pictures of the naval review' at Spithead.
Emile Lazern, illusionist, and Marguerite
Ismay and Co. are announced for Friday
night.

The Lyric Picture Theatre has this week
starred the Coronation Naval review, along
with that good old dramatic stand-by, “The
Temptations of a Great City.” The naval
review pictures are away ahead of the Cor-
onation series, which, one way and another,
were disappointing. “The Panama Canal”
arrives with to-night’s change of programme
—a great film of the world’s biggest engin-
eering job. Colonial 1 has had a good edu-
cational study of the boot industry, and fol-
lows it with another, “The, Rome Exhibi-
tion.” “The Indian Sharpshooters” and
Pathe’s Gazette—the latter putting current
Australian news in a nutshell—have been the
most graphic features at Colonial 2. To-
night’s new films include “lee Effects iu
Odessa,” and there ought to be something
worth looking at in that.
ffc * * *
Saturday’s bill at Melbourne 1 loyal will
be “Lady Frederick,” the bright comedy in
which Miss Ethel Irving reached a top rung
of celebrity in Smokeville some four years
ago. “Lady Frederick” is limited to three
acts, so the programme has a tail-piecelet,
written by H. A. Jones for Miss Irving, and
entitled “Dolly’s Little Bills.” Meanwhile
there are two more nights of the co. in the
story of the wife who made herself a widow
and the barrister who blew himself out.
# *■ * *
The amateurs have presumably retired
from the field at the Princess’s, taking their
baggage and their bouquets with them. The
“Hook of Holland” Co. ran for six nights
and finished with a bit in hand, after which
the “Veronique” Co., starting last Saturday,
was to run for three nights. The beginning
was deplorable and the end was probably
shocking.
J. C. Williamson, Ltd., is about to resume
business at the Princess’s for a while. On
Saturday begins a one-week season of “The
Silver King,” the drama which promises to
live so long that, when it dies of natural de-
cay, it will probably be resurrected in the an-
tique costumes of the period when it was born.
William Desmond is to figure beauteously iu
the name part, and an interestingly new cast
will include Atholwood as Dan’l Jaikes.
Atholwood has been a very mixed lot of old
men in his time, but Melbourne has rarely,
if ever, seen him as a very meek and mild
old man, in a poor but virtuous bell topper.
*# . *
At Her Majesty’s the nights of “Our Miss
Oibbs” are numbered to Saturday, when
Melbourne will say of the “record-smashing
success” that it was an excellent show of
millinery and costumes, and a sufficiently-
pleasing kind of musical comedy in the alto-
gether. After the last echoes of “Dip-ti-
giddy” have dissolved into silence, the
present theatre will pass away, and
the public will see it no more. A
new and festive-looking Her Majesty’s is to
reopen three weeks later, and a crowd of
recollections incidental to the occasion have
already booked their seats, so to speak. The
hand of destiny and the decrees of fate have
operated on the house so often during its
career that its remnants of identity with the
original Alexandra might as well be torn off
and distributed as souvenirs.
»* * *
At the King’s the second and last perform-
ance of a casual passing revival of “The
Christian” comes due to-night (Thursday) ;
therefore persons desirous of seeing John
Storm converted from fanaticism to Kissti-
anity by Miss Quayle, of the music-’alls, had
better haste to the spectacle. On Saturday
a new strength of Anderson forces will be
brought to bear on “Tommy Atkins,” a
drama George Rignold staged at the Loyal
long ago. Two of the prime movers in this
military outbreak will be Walter Baker and
Miss Ross.
* * *
A new turn at the Opera House is pro-
vided by a pair of roller-skaters, whereof the
male partner exploits the art of step-dancing
on rollers as though they didn’t roll at all.
and the lady partner responds to a recall (or
she did last Saturday, anyhow) by leading
forth an infant child, as though this evidence
of her maternity were expected to melt the
audience to tears. Another new turn is com-
posed of two jugglers, both male, who are
about as fresh and original as their old im-
plements of trade will permit. The strong
man of the two plays with a cannon-ball in
Cinquevallian style, whilst the mature low
comedian preserves an air of youth in an
Eton jacket. There are other people, old
and new, including the lugubrious evergreen
Dawson. Also, one of the turns is a flicker-
graph, which is no better than it ought to
be, considering that it reflects on the
majesty of King George, and the glory of
the British Navy.

At West’s Building, Melbourne, ■ large

have his hair oiled, attended by a quite un-

“The Two Sides” provided a blinding con-

Eagle” narrated a white man’s hunt for a

supplied an interestifig view of black brother
and his capacity for doing a little work in a
long while.

At the P’ran Lyric (Vic.) the Coronation
picture has been moving across the screen,
with, a convoy of plain and colored films to
port and starboard. Among the latter “The
Gorges de Loup” acquainted people with a
part of France which has many recommenda-
tions to the searcher for scenic effects. “How
Bella Was Won,” a familiar Dickensian
story, exhibits some notably clever acting, and
“The Barber of Seville,” which was shown
on Friday, gave all the points of Beau-
marchais’s play, to the sweet accompaniment
of Rossini’s music.

Died last week, in the Far West
(N.S.W.), the Rev. Wilfred Mailler, who
was held in more than ordinary esteem by his
parishioners in the Mulga country. He had
had a varied experience of life before enter-
ing the Church, and wrote some good verse
for The Bulletin.

From Sydney Telegraph (31/7/T1) :
A Liberal threat: “We will placard Mudgee with
the words, ‘Vote for Labor and the hauling down of
the Union Jack.’ ”
And the same morning the leader of “Liberal-
ism” was reported to have said:—
Australians are lovers of sport and fair play, and
no real sportsman can approve of Labor tactics.
“Der”: Broome (W.A.), horrorstricken at
the Australian Government’s intention to
abolish Asiatic labor in the pearling in-
dustry, says it only sticks to the colored man
because the white is no good for diving. For
all that, it gives all, or nearly all, its shorn
jobs to the smtidgy races. The municipal
council employs one European laborer, and
he drives the sanitary cart.
It is pretty difficult to get a judgment re-
versed in the S. Aus. Supreme Court, as
S. Aus. owns only three Judges. So. when
the _ judgment of any of them is appealed
against, the Full Court consists of himself —
and naturally lie thinks he was right all
along—and two others, both of whom have
to be persuaded that he was wrong. Any-
body who appeals is, therefore, “laying a bit
to nothing.” Yet a widow-Woman has man-
aged it; and, moreover, she has managed it
against the powerful municipal Trust that
runs the tramways. Her husband was a
country carrier, and was jogging along a
suburban road, on a dark, wet night, when a
car came up behind—at 20 miles an hour, it
is suggested—bunted into his van, and upset
it, killing him on the spot. In the widow’s
action for damages, Judge Gordon held that
he was on the line at his own risk, and re-
fused to let the case go to the jury at all.
His colleagues, Way and Homburg, have
agreed, on appeal, that this was wrong.
They don’t say' that the Trust was to blame,
but they consider that it is a fair thing for
the opinion of a jury. So the questions of
light and speed and so on have to be threshed
out all over again, to the great joy of Costs.

Coronation letters to hand indicate that George’s un-
paralleled show was a faiilure, owing to the excessive
barricading which offended and frightened off the
walking classes, who were expected to provide the
loud cheers. There was Some hooraying, certainly;
but it was thin and heavily adulterated with silence.
Stage-manager Churchill is blamed for the compara-
tively poor audience drawn by the performance. For
fear that one of his kings* should be trumped with a
bomb, he excluded nearly all the jacks, spades and
clubs, and played only with kings, queens and dia-
monds. It resulted in a dull game.
“Meljh”: Her Majesty’s closes for three weeks.
Melba said the acoustics were not quite good enough
tor her opera season ; but it is not this alone that
causes the Williamson people to spend £5OO0 —the
third time they have touched up this play house,
which holds more than any other in Australia. 1
hope the pit will get a better view. The theatre was
built by Joubert, as the Alexandra, on the model of
the Vienna Ring theatre, and was opened about the
same time as the new Princess’s, 26 years ago. The
site appeared very bad, yet the theatre has obviously
not suffered on that account. Joubert, who had been
secretary of the N.S.W. Agricultural Society, pos-
sessed a right instinct; but the success was not for
him. In the early part of 1900 Williamson was hard
pressed by his late partner, Musgrove, who secured
a monopoly of the Princess’s. The Alex, had been
almost a persistent failure, except in spurts. Wil-
liamson took the haza'rdous-looking step of leasing
the forlorn place, and re-chYistened it Her Majesty’s.
By a lucky accident he opened on Mafeking Night,
with “11.M.5. Pinafore.’/ Now the theatre ranks
only second to the Royal, and the erstwhile vic-
torious Princess’s has declined to dispute the third
place with the King’s.
From London: Eileen Castles made her London
debut under the wing of the massive and melodious
Clara Butt, at a Saturday afternoon “grand pat-
riotic concert,” in the Albert Hall. The concert was
called patriotic partly because it was held a few days
before the Coronation, and partly, because Clara’s
Kenner ley was to warble a couple of new songs about
Mary’s George—songs which were most unmercifully
slated by the critics for their banality. West-
minster Gazette wonders how Rumford had the
“hardihood” to present them to an intellectual aud-
ience. The Westminster describes Eileen as “a
young Australian soprano with a pretty voice which
is deserving further cultivation,” and adds a hope
that she will “put more expression and individualitv
into her singing.”. The Daily Chronicle says,
“Pleasing quality, but lacks power.” And the
Morning Post thought she sang “with sweetness of
voice and purity of means”—a peculiar way of put-
ting it.

lan Penny, the somewhat bulky and beautiful
young man who Orlandoed to Lily Brayton’s Rosa-
lind in Australia, has seemingly dropped out of the
Asches’ co. When they revived “As You Like It”
for Tree’s recent Shakspeare Festival in London,
young George Relph was the Orlando. Grimwood
and Tripp Edgar repeated their excellent work as
pious old Adam and amiable courtier Le Beau, who
are, when fitly impersonated, the most convincing
characters in the play. Even the artificial Penny
was made to look real what time Adam fed him
with moral discourse, or Le Beau briskly advised
him to escape from the jurisdiction of the usurping
Duke.
A very foolish young man, named Eckard, at
Rockhampton (Q.), battled against Nature for 74
hours continuously on roller skates, and thus estab-
lished another silly record. When finished, his feet
were so swollen that the boots had to be cut off.
And probably his head was no smaller than it had
been.
Napier (M.L.) has accepted a Melbourne archi-
tect’s plans for a municipal theatre to seat 1600.
The playbox will cost about £20,000. Welling-
ton Opera House Co., after long years of stalling off
the inevitable, is also going to bang a saxpence or
two, and has decided to knock up a new theatre (not
on the old site) at a cost of £70,000. There has
been quite an epidemic of theatre-building in M.L.
during the past couple of years, and at the present
rate of progress there will soon be as many show
houses as pubs, and, after the clustering I O U, pubs
are the country’s most numerous asset.
“Vagabond” : It is probably true that Monday or Wed-
nesday is as good a night as Saturday so far as the
play goes (B. 27/7/’ll), but personally I prefer the
Saturday night, because half the enjoyment of it is
in the thought that to-morrow will be Sunday, and
we’ll have no work to do. If a fellow “does” a
show with a pal, he is apt to have a gingerbeer or
two, and he will have them in a lot more peaceful
frame of mind if he knows that in the morning,
even if he is a few minutes longer a-bed, he won’t
have to try to put his boots on with one hand and
perform circus acts with a poached egg with the
other. And if he trots his best girl to the theatre,
the result is much the same. So, Saturday night’s
show for mine, thank’ee.

Tree was not, as “Wing” alleges, the original Rev.
Spalding in the “Private Secretary.” Original as
far as London is concerned, decidedly; but the farce
was tried “on the dog” in the country weeks before
the metropolis sampled it. It ( was produced at the
T. R., Cambridge (afterwards/going on to the rival
’Varsity city), with a clever little mummer, yclept
Arthur Helmore, in the role of the goloshed and
bath-bun’d individual. When the piece got its
London production a “name” was wanted, and Tree
was engaged for the part. Helmore, the original,
was subsequently given a show on tour, and then
everybody said he copied Tree.', ,As a matter ,o,f fact
he did, for Tree did a lot in inventing “business,”
and generally building up the 'part. His perform-
ance, indeed, no matter what “Wing” may say as
to the character not being “of much account at
first” was a tour de force, and, practically made a
“go” of the piece—which didn’t “go” at all at
the start. Penley took up the Rev. Robert when the
show moved from the Prince of Wales (then
Prince’s) to the Globe, and when its success was
assured. By the way, “Dot” Boucicault figutes in
the programme of the original Cambridge produc-
tion —as actor, not stage-manager. He played
the son of “the Squire in the red coat,” Harry
Marsland by name.

returned to his home in South Melbourne. The an-
cient is in his 7lst year, and has been “travelling”

“Harrison O.”: Gregan McMahon intends running
an Australian night, in connection with his Reper-
tory Theatre, in December. A couple of brain-
children, whose parentage is blushfully acknowledged
by Louis Esson and Blamire Young, will crave at-
tention, and one of “Billy” Moore’s pieces may be
revived. McMahon is anxiously awaiting the arrival
of a first-class Australian drama, but so far he has
not heard the thud in his letter-box.
“Ami”: It would indeed be ridiculous to call
George Rignold a one-part actor. To be sure, his
Henry V. whips the whole record, but that is be-
cause his physical qualifications have left such as
Calvert, Coleman, Benson and Waller hopelessly in
the shade. Brooke acted it in Australia; Kemble,
Macready, Phelps and C. Kean acted it in London.
But all such great men would resign it after seeing,
point device, the very man, in Rignold. Yet it was
somewhat of an artistic misfortune to be chained on
to Harry for about 2000 performances, to satisfy the
craving appetite of America and Britain. What a
beautiful performance was his Colonel Challis, in
“Alone.” He toured Australia with this, followed by
“Black-eyed Susan,” and surely, since T. P. Cooke,
there was never such a breezy William ! His Claude
Melnotte was a New York matinee furore; his
Othello the best in Australia since Brooke. His Cali-
ban is remembered in London, and towers above the
eccentricities of Tree and Bensoii in the part. The
only man to equal George R. in it 7 will be Asche.

Presently came George and Mary, with small boys

“Vulcan”: Some recent very exhausting disserta-
tions on one Shakspeare remind me that Ibsen once
delivered himself on his commentator-cranks gener-
ally : “They are always trying to impute allegory or
symbolism to my writings, and seem to imagine that
1 imagined all the fool-things with which their own
minds are filled. One learned commentator even
likened one of my dramas to something the Apostle
Paul is supposed to have written. 1 never read Paul,
and don’t care about him, anyway.” I have an idea
(which I keep dark) that Shaksbacon’s greatness rests
not so much on what he thought as on his ability
to make the centuries’ “intellects” think he thought.

Just sailed from Sydney the yacht Norma,
90 tons, to search for the lost Yongala and
the £lOOO reward offered by the Queensland
Government. The vessel is owned by Dr.
Cassidy, of Sydney, and has had a chequered
career. Built in 1880 by a New Yorker as
an ocean-going yacht, she saw wild times
before she came down to be a wrecker, em-
ployed in nosing round and picking up re-
mains of derelicts lying on various reeD
in the Pacific. The Yongala reward expires
on September 30.
The 49th anniversary of McDouall Stuart’s
feat in penetrating the continent, right to
the shores of the Indian Ocean, was cele-
brated in Adelaide on July 25. Next year
is to see a bigger-affair for the jubilee; but
every year counts with a batch of men at
that age. Stuart did not lose a man on his
journey, and was himself the first of his
party to die (in I 860). Since then, Ivek-
wick, Frew, Waterhouse and Thring have
gone; but Auld, King, Nash, McGorry and
Heath remain, all in Adelaide ;• Billiatt is in
London. The Geographical Society of S.A.
entertained and toasted them last week; a
bit of the dried meat used on the journey was
Through the unexpected death of “Tommy”
Taylor, M.H.R., at Christchurch last week.
M.L. politics loses the packet of crackers
that has been tied to their tail for 15 years.
In many respects he was a clever man, but
as a politician he leaves little in the way of
reputation except his ability to flay an op-
ponent with an unmerciful tongue, his
powerful driving of the water-cart, and the
thud of his fall over the Seddon “voucher.”
He was a sound democrat in principle, and
only lacked the balance that would have
made him leader of a big party. When old
warrior Seddon was pulled down by Death,
a lot of the fire went out of Taylor, although
now and then he burst into flame and
scorched the whiskers of Opposition-leader
Massey. He left at 49.
The alleged People’s Party of Victoria is
now roping in the bewildered children of
simple parents to form junior branches. The
dreadful practice was lately begun at Pres-
ton (Melb.), when several boys and girls of
tender years were organised into a militant
political body. It is not precisely known
what the youngsters are to do, though the
Age solemnly declares that they are to be-
come “a source of strength to the party in
Preston.” Possibly it will give some idea of
the intellectual status of “the party in Pres-
ton” when it goes to the perambulator for
help and support. But quite likely, when
the perambulator finds out the I O CJ’s and
the big landlords that the “Liberal” party
has burdened it with, it may give that party
a lift in an unexpected quarter.
Young Maoriland is leading the strenuous
life these times. The compulsory military
service officers are calling round for him
with great regularity to do a bit of drill;
the police are pursuing him into the hotels
and cross-examining him before the beaute-
ous bar-lady as to whether he is above the
age of consent, which is 21, as far as drinks
are concerned in M.L.; herds of wild can-
didates, ravening for the general elections
in November, lie in wait for him, and de-
mand proof that he is on the roll and a
guarantee that he will vote for the Right
Man to Save the Country; the Wowser is
tearing after him waving the water-cart
and urging him to strike out the top line:
the employer is nagging at him that he
doesn’t work as hard as he used to and not
nearly as hard as he ought to; and the M.L.
Worker is calling upon him to arise and
put himself on the roll of dishonor which
it has inaugurated to enshrine the name of
every lad “who goes to prison in protest
against conscription. . . So that, please God,
the present form of compulsory military
training will be knocked into Hades.'”
Meanwhile, the National Debt threatens to
topple over and break him.

THE COMIC JURISDICTION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Smithers, S.M., urged Mr. Passmore to get on
with his examination of the witness. He did not
want jokes all the time.
Mr. Passmore: Your Worship lias been the worst
offender.
The Magistrate: In a case like this one can’t help
being witty. —Sydney Stiff.
An atmosphere of chronic wit
Would somehow seem to impregnate
My Court. I cannot banish it.
Despite myself I scintillate.
On to the Bench at ten I trip:
The constables, in snowy breeks,
Stand round like mutes. I make a quip.
Jley, presto! They are all in shrieks.
The drunk who hiccups in the dock
is wont to be supplied by me
With jokes as well as gaol. The lock
Is turned on him ’rfiid yells of glee.
No case is so imbued with hate,
And rage and malice and the rest,
But I will cheer it, soon or late.
With some well-timed and sprightly jest.
That humor is infectious, all
Who go to law are bound to know.
Let doubters pay my Court a call,
Atid they must own that this is so.
The plaintiff’s counsel turns from law
To epigram and .paradox;
Defendant’s man emits a saw,
■ At which the very courthouse rocks.
One member only of the crowd
Sits sullen, mum and dissonant,
His face disfigured by a cloud—
-1 mean, of course, the litigant.
N.S.W.
10-2-4.

The origin of paper bag cookery is lost in
the mists of time. Nick Soyer, late chef
at Brooks’s Club, London, grandson of the
famous Alexis, merely shows how it can be
more generally adapted. He was appren-
ticed to Papon, a French confectioner, who
encouraged his lads to think. Soyer’s
thinking turned to paper bags, and he prac-
tised with them while chef in the ducal
bouse of Newcastle, 15 years ago. But he
could not get rid of the taste of the bag.
nor could lie later on when he became chef
to Naylor-Leyland, in Hyde Park House.
So the matter was hung up till this year,
wjien a firm made him a special paper
that removed every objection. (P.S.—But
don’t try it for boiled cabbage.)
The M.L. red-flag Socialists and a few
other cranks, whose policy appears to be
that it is better to have their throats slit
lying down than to argue the point with
the enemy, have been sooling the youth of
the country into resisting the compulsory
defence regulations. So far they haven’t
done much harm, except to have a number
of lads dragged before the police court and
fined for failure to register. But if they have
their way they will induce further defiance,
and the next thing will be the irremovable
stain of the gaol. The other day a boy who
had refused to register was accompanied by
his father to the business premises of a
magistrate in Nelson. The parent an-
nounced that he had refused to allow his
son to register because the Lord’s com-
mands didn’t fit in with those of the M.L.
Government, and that, as he had to defy
one or the other, the Government’s would
have to go by the board. To which the
magistrate replied that he would give the
Scriptural gentleman a week to think it
over, and at the end of that time the maxi-
mum penalty would arrive. Unfortunately,
it is the boys who will suffer, and the magis-
trate made a good, workable suggestion when
he said that obstructing parents should be
made to toe the line. Already one young-
ster is in a Wellington prison for defying
the law, but that long-eared animal doesn't
appear to have thought of anything to say
to the demented barrackers for the millen-
nium who howled him into going there.
* * * *
Years ago Dick Seddon told a Maoriland
election audience that • he would use the
prisoners of the State to plant forests. The
audience laughed, whereupon Richard of
Kumara banged his masterful fist on the
table with : “You may laugh, but I tell you
it shall be done.” To-day Maoriland has
7000 acres reclaimed and planted with com-
mercially profitable timber. That is but one
—and not the brightest—side of the ques-
tion. A brighter is that prisoners, instead
of being degraded by idleness or employment
in useless toil, are being built up physically
and mentally by out-of-door work, and have
a chance to gain self-respect by employment
in a useful occupation. So far, the “prison
camps” have given less trouble in super-
vision than (he walled and barred gaols of
Australian States, and there is no reason to
suspect that it will ever be otherwise.
“Pip”: The Bulletin’s
recent talk about forest
schools makes me won-
der again why city
schools do not abut on city
parks. I have been at the
school business all my life,
and always I have found
mean “play-grounds”
crowded with pale-faced
mites, hemmed in by smell-
ful back yards, though often
there was a beautiful
grassy park quarter of a
mile away. If the school
were part of the park, and
the park part of the school,
the children would be prac-
tically in the country;
“nature studies” would be
facilitated ; the elder pupils
could help to plant trees
and shrubs when necessary ;
and the vegetables thus set
going would be afterwards
looked upon as their “very
own,” with the result that
much of the present van-
dalism would disappear.
Many people never think
of the situation of the pub-
lic schools; others think
anything good enough for
the common herd, never
realising that if the com-
mon herd was allowed to
browse more freely in the
sweet open spaces, their
own ewe lambs would have
a better chance of escaping
infection.
There was a strange little strike in Ade-
laide the other day. The wine-and-spirit
firms’ men wanted a rise. That was not
strange, considering the pay they were get-
ting ; the extraordinary part was that the
Brewers’ Association sided against the
liquor-vendors, and told hotelkeepers that
there would be no beer for anyone who
patronised blackleg firms. The strike itself
was soon settled; Wilson, Minister of In-
dustry, got both sides to confer, and any
points that they could not agree on were left
to Judge Gordon, who is rapidly coming to be
accepted as the ideal man to put things of
this kind straight. Now the vendors of the
two kinds of liquor have got to settle their
differences, and the poor devil of a publican
-—who often is “tied” (which means fet-
tered) to both of them —hopes they will do
it quickly. The grievance seems to be that
the costly job of fighting the no-liquor party
has been left to the brewers alone, and they
urge that the work and the expense must be
shared by the sellers of hard-stuff also, since
the reduction of licenses hits them about
equally.

If you want to know which political
party to vote for at next election in N. S.
Wales, ask a policeman. Since the Labor
Government came into office some months
ago it has done more for John Hopkins than
the “Liberals” promised to do in as many
years. A few of them :
Reasonable clothing to keep the men from being
frozen while following on the trail of W. Sikes
during winter; £2O a year put on to the salaries
of superintendents (an encouragement to the men to
push on towards the top) ; extended leave after long
service; more reasonable expenses on transfer from
one district to another; the gateway to promotion
widened and the hinges oiled; and a system of
Sundays off introduced.
Now the Ministerial head might look in-
to the detective (criminaLinvestigation)
branch, and consider whether the pay and
other things offered are likely to attract
brains of the special quality needed for that
job. Also, it might find out what chance the
brains have of getting an appointment if
they are not accompanied by 13in. feet.

Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy is not a common,
everv-dav cough mixture. It is a meritorious remedy
for all dangerous and troublesome complications re-
sulting from colds in the head, chest or lung3.*
A Sydney magistrate rose in wrath the
other day and fined a citizen 40s. or 34
days for insulting one of the lawyers en-
gaged in a case. The precedent is worth
following up. For instance, a lawyer not
engaged in a case might be fined 6s. Bd. or
six months for chipping in with an alleged
witticism —a thing that frequently hap-
pens. Or a police-prosecutor might be re-
lieved of a month’s pay and two stripes for
making a feeble joke at the expense of a
timid or confused witness. Or now and
then a chronically abusive and vulgar at-
torney might be sent along for a “Kathleen
Mavourneen” , for insulting a witness or
party in a case. Or, best of all, the magis-
trate might giye himself six months for play-
ing cornerman in his own court. The im-
position of a few such penalties might turn
even police courts into places to which de-
cent people could turn without dread in pur-
suit of justice.
“Locust”: Harking back to The
Bulletin’s reference to George Rex’s
invitation fo his perplexed people to
unite with him in solving their per-
plexities, and the instances given of
the horrors of the Londoners starving
. in cellars, absolutely ignored and neg-
lected, one cannot help contrasting this
callous anarchy and absolutely un-
christian carelessness of the purple to-
wards the poor of England with the
ordered system called the “Elberfeld”
in Germany. There each responsible
citizen—banker, merchant, mechanic —
takes his part in the organ-
isation which seeks out the
poor and needy and the un-
employed, and assists them
to food, shelter and employ-
ment. This private work
supplements the State insur-
ance measures of the Ger-
mans, and so arranges things
that there is no submerged
tenth in Germany. No won-
der Emperor Bill’s nation is
advancing by leaps and
bounds in commerce, popu-
lation, and general pros-
perity !
Victoria’s energetic police
force isn’t much of a success
fit capturing burglars and
bag-snatchers, but it can
shine sometimes. Lately it
brought up a man who had
been observed biting his own
arm, on the charge of “offen-
sive behavior,” and had him
fined £2. in default one
month. No reason was given
for the simpleton wanting to
consume himself, but as be
was in a Salv’army Home at
the time he was possibly a
little peckish.
“Justus” lodges a complaint:—
I have a brother under 30 years of age, who, as an
apprentice, served five years in sail. He has climbed
the ladder, and now holds a master’s certificate, with
exemptions for all Australian ports, and has been in
command of large deep-sea steamers. There is not a
blemish on his record. Some six weeks ago, full of
enthusiasm and patriotism, he made application for a
position as junior officer in the Navy, but up to the
time of writing he has had no reply. He heard out-
side, however, that they were taking Australians only
as cadets, and were bringing all the officers from some
place referred to as “Home.” It will be some years
before the cadets can complete their training. In
the meantime we have young Australians, who have
served their time and know the Australian coast
better than any Imperial Service man; and are they
to be driven away when making application ? The
authorities are very glad to get men. Why should
they not be as keen to secure able officers when op-
portunity presents itself?
GUFF.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“We must not forget the vastness and splendor of
the British Empire, which covers over 12 million
square miles, and which, if cut into strips one mile
wide, would go 450 times round the globe.— lira.
John Wheeler, R.W.G.M., speaking on the Day of
Dutch William.
If all the hairpins in the world
Could reach from Hell to Scone,
It follows that a flageolet
Is but a mutton-bone.
If all the beer in Budapest,
And all the Spanish port,
Were put into a glass, ’twould make
More than a British quart.
If all the whiskers in the world
Were made for stuffing pillows.
The kapok trade would have a slump
At Waring’s and at Gillow’s.
If all the matches used at night
Were used for breakfast food
For pauper infants in the slums.
Their health would not be good.
If Brother Squeeler’s brains were teased
And drawn than raw silk thinner,
From April First to Dill Maeky,
They’d reach ’twixt now and dinner.
And that good man’s intelligence,
That won’t let him be merry,
Has never known Australia yet—
It’s camped in Londonderry.
VICTORIA.
MARTIN LUTHER.

Twaddle, whose dastardly attack on High
Court Judge Higgins from the coward’s
shelter of the N.S.W. Assembly evoked a
reply that was mordant for all its dignity,
floundered badly in his alleged “reply.” He
said that he had not attacked Higgins in
anticipation of Higgins dealing with Arbi-
tration Court cases affecting pastoralists;
and then he wiped out the denial by say-
ing—
In referring to the Bill, it came into my mind
what a terrible position it would be for the farmers
and graziers of this country if a certain clause was
passed in the measure, and by a strange misfortune
a man such as Judge Higgins should be appointed
by any Government of this country.
After that, a very wandering Twaddle said
lie regarded Judge Higgins as “an honest
and upright man,” and then he said that the
same party had uttered “a vile slander.”
And, finally, when he was pulled up for
going beyond a personal explanation, and
was asked why he didn’t make the state-
ment outside Parliament, this cowering
patriot offered the specious excuse that he
might have to defend an action for libel,
and “the greater the truth the greater the
libel”—rwhicli is a lie, anyway, because
truth (if told for the public good) is a com-
plete answer to a charge of libel.
A strenuous clerical agitation is afoot in
.Melbourne against the reserving of all easy
and fat billets in the Anglican Church in
Victoria for fa tted parsons from abroad. The
Australian-bred parson begins to insist that
he should enjoy some of the loafs and fishes.
The E. and A. boat Eastern, which re-
ceived a bad knocking about on the Sala-
mander Bank, near Brisbane, at the begin-
ning of the year, and was afterwards re-
paired at Sydney, last week completed a
quick run of 24| days from Kobe, in
spite of having to tight a howling southerly
gale nearly all the way from Brisbane. The
Papanui, which tottered out of Melbourne to
get itself repaired in Japan, had at least two
stops for repairs on the subsequent voyage
from Melbourne to England. Thus the cheap
brown shipbuilder show's up just as badly as
the cheap brown anything-else when his work
is compared with that of the white man.
The latest star actor on the gallows at
Fremantle (W.A.) was supported from the
condemned cell to the drop by the execu-
tioner and a priest. With all due respect to
the gentlemen supporting him port and star-
board, this paper is reminded of the old
Italian saying: “ ‘This comes of keeping bad
company, quoth the' thief, ‘as he went to
the gallows between the hangman and a
monk.’ ”

The S. Aus. Attorney-General is advertis-
ing instructions to country J’s.P. regarding
legal procedure in certain cases. This ex-
pense might have been saved if Australia
had adopted the idea of John Walpole Wil-
lis, first resident Judge at Port Phillip, who
opening Melbourne Supreme Court in 1841,
intimated that he would insist upon the at-
tendance of all J s.P. a t future criminal
sittings so that they might learn something
about the business. The J’s.P. took no
notice, and Willis threatened to fine them
as., and have them struck off the rolls. This
according to the chronicle, “offended the
gentry/- who about two years later succeeded
in getting Willis the sack. The J.P has
been contributing to the humor of the
country ever since.
The Argus put “Un-Australian Con-
duct over the cabled item telling of Billy
Waites ungentlemanly action in biting a
horses ear at the “Wild Australian” show
in London. Since when has infamous con-
duct ceased to be “un-British”? If the
Argus goes on like this, the Imperial
Wowsers will be suspecting it of wanting
to cut the painter.

DOLLY : “Oh, dear , no! Yon see, Mr. Newly-
wed was married before, and she vows the

The other day the disruption of the Em-
pire was threatened because State Excel-
lency Carmichael proposed to wait a few
hours so that he might foregather with his
old pal and successor Fuller. Now it is
G.-G. Dudley who is imperilling the Con-
stitooshun by staying where he is for a few
days beyond the anticipated time, owing to
Captain Rome s illness. It seems that it is
highly improper, not to say immoral, for
one ornamental functionary to be in the
Commonwealth after his successor arrives,
inasmuch as the two gentlemen would bite
each other, or something. Or it may be
merely that the “authorities” fear that Aus-
tialia, having already as manv Governors
as it can stand, might burst Reloaded with
another. Meantime, the Commonwealth
might drop Precedent a hint to knock off
being an idiot. This severely practical
country has very grave doubts about the
usefulness of the whole frilly business that
surrounds Government House, and inci-
cients like tins are not calculated to remove
any its suspicions.

You could er knocked me down wif ’arf a brick '
les, me, that kids meself I know their ways,
An as a name fer smoogin’ in our click!
I just lines up an’ tips the saucy wink.
But strike! The way she piled on dauw ’ Yer’d
think
A bloke was givin’ back-chat to the queen.
’Er name’s Doreen.
I seen ’er in the markit first uv all,
Inspectin’ brums at Steeny Isaac’s stall.
I backs me barrel- in—the same ole wav—
An’ sez, “Wot O! It’s bin a bonzer day
Ow is it fer a walkOh, ’oly wars!
Jhe sorter look she gimme! Jest becors
I tried to chat ’er, like you’d make a start
Wif any tart.
An, I kin take me oaf I wus perlite,
An’ never said no word that wusn’t right,
An never tried to maul ’er, or to do
A thing yer might call crook. Ter tell yer true
I didn’t seem to ’ave the nerve—wif ’er.
1 felt as if I couldn’t go that fur,
An’ start to sling off chiack like I used.
Not intrajuiced!
Nex’ time I sighted ’er in Little Bourke,
AVhere she wus in a cop. I found ’er lurk
Wus pastin’ labels in a pickle joint,
A game that- any’ow, that ain’t the point
Once more I tried ter chat ’er in the street,'
But, bli-me! Did she turn me down a treat!
The way she tossed ’er ’ead! an’ swished ’er
skirt!
Oh, it wus dirt!
A squarer tom, I swear, I never seen,
In all me natcheral, than this Doreen.
It weren’t no guyver neither ; fer I knoo
That any other bloke ’ad Buckley’s ’oo
Tried fer to pick ’er up. Yes, she wus square,
She jest sailed by an’ lef’ me standin’ there
Like any mug. Thinks I, “I’m out er luck.”
An’ done a duck.
Well, I dunno. It’s that way wif a bloke.
If she’d ha’ breasted up ter me an’ spoke,
I’d thort ’er jest a commin bit er fluff,
An’ then fergot about ’er, like enough.
It’s jest like this. The tarts that’s ’ard ter get
Makes you all ’ot to chase ’em, an’ to let
The cove called Cupid get an’ ’ammer-lock;
An’ lose yer block.
I know a bloke ’oo knows a bloke ’oo toils
In that same pickle found-ery. (’E boils
The cabbitch storks or somethink.) Anyway,
I gives me pal the orfis fer to say
’E ’as a sister in the trade ’oo’s been
Out uv a jorb, an’ wants ter meet Doreen.
Then we kin get ter know .’er, if we’ve luck.
’E sez, “Ribuck.”
O’ course we worked the oricle; you bet!
But, struth, I ain’t recovered frum it yet!
’Twus on a Saturdee, in Colluns-street,
An’ quite be accident, o’ course—we meet.
Me pal ’e trots ’er up an’ does the toff—-
’E alius wus a bloke fer showin’ off.
“This ’ere’s Doreen,” ’e sez. “This ’ere’s the
Kid.”
I lifts me lid.
“This ’ere’s Doreen,” ’e sez. I says, “Good day.”
An’ bli-me, I ’ad nothin’ more ter say!
I couldn’t speak a word, or meet ’er eye.
Clean done me block ! I never been so shy,
Not since 1 wus a tiny little cub,
An’ run the rabbit to the corner pub—
Wot time the Summer days wus dry an’ ’ot
Fer me ole pot.
Me! that ’as barracked tarts, an’ torked an’ larft,
An chucked orf at ’em like a phonograft!
Gorstrooth ! I seemed to lose me pow’r o’ speech.
But, er ! Oh, strike me pink ! She is a peach !
The sweetest in the barrer! Spare me days,
I earn t describe that diner’s winnin’ ways.
The way she torks ! ’Er lips! ’Er eyes ! ’Er
hair! . . .
Oh, gimme air!
I dunno ’ow I done it in the end.
I reckerlect I arst ter be ’er friend ;
An’ tried ter play at ’andies in the tram,
A thing she said she ’ad no time fer. .. . D n !
I gotter swear when I think wot a mug
I must er seemed to ’er. But still I ’ug
That promise that she give me fer the beach.
The bonzer peach!
Now, as the poit sez, the days drag by
On ledding feet. I wisht they’d do a guy.
I dunno ’ow I ’ad the nerve ter speak,
An’ make that meet with ’er fer Sundee week!
But strike! It’s funny wot a bloke ’ll do
When ’e’s all out. . . . She’s gorn, when I come-to.
I m yappin’ to me cobber uv me mash
I’ve done me dash !
•Er name’s Doreen An’ me—that thort I knoo
The ways uv tarts, an’ all that smoogin’ game!
An’ so I ort; fer ain’t I known a few'?
Yet, some’ow / dunno. It ain’t the same
I carn't tell wot it is; but, all I know.
I’ve dropped me bundle — an’ I’m glad it’s so.
Fer when I com,e ter think■ o’ wot I’ve been,
’Er name’s Doreen.

A BORIGIN-
ALITIES
“X-Eurner”: “Stickpicker” (B. 6/7/T1)
wants to know if the locally-named pine-adder, blue-
mouthed species, is venomous. I’m a scrub-clearer,
so come in contact with dozens of the reptiles daily.
The little animal is übiquitous over this planet, and
the types vary little. In Europe it is called “gecko”
—tease him and you’ll hear his miniature warwhoop:
“Geek-o!” Allied more or less to the newt, it is
in North America called the salamander, from the
mythical belief of the Indians that it could go
through fire unscathed. Those familiar with the
soft, cold, clammy properties of the lizard-like crea-
tures will readily understand how the idea
started. “Stickpicker” nn st have been bitten, as the
little beast’s only poison depot lies within its venom-
ous-looking blue mouth. The flimsy, cobweb-like
substance found adhering to his hand after handling
the lizard was part of the thin gossamer membrane
(very like that encasing a sheep’s kidney) which
covers the body, and in itself is innocuous. I know
of two cases of gecko-bites on the arm ; in both cases
being neglected, they culminated in the flesh around
the affected part having to be removed and the bones
scraped. It exudes no poison from the body, as
“Stickpicker” seems to imagine, and the ill effects
of the bite seem to be local. Brushed one off the
nape of my neck the other day, and was wondering,
in view of the two cases mentioned above, how I
would have fared had the same knifing process to be
gone through in that locality.
“Grand Slam”: Talking about the ob-
jection of certain birds to the touch of a human
hand upon their eggs, “Boswell” (B. 15/6/’ll)
omits the case of the emu. When she scents the
horrible smell of man upon her labors, Ma straight-
way demolishes the whole setting. Last week I
cautiously stalked a mob of 10 eggs, and having a
notion that they would be useful to the cook, I
humped as many as I could conveniently carry, in-
tending to bag the balance next day. But next day
v;as too late. Ma had been back in my absence, and
had completely broken up the happy home, smash-
ing all the furniture, and clearing out to pastures
new, in the hope of evading the defiling hand of
man.
“Eryander”: “E.11.P.” (B. 6/</ll)
makes his kangaroos outpace a train travelling 50
miles an hour. Now, first, no ’roo that ever hopped
could get away from the big Scottish deerhound.
Next, it doesn’t require a thoroughbred horse to
“cut down” the deerhound on a straight and level
track ; any moke with a tolerable turn of speed can
do it. Lastly, even the thoroughbred’s best pace
over a mile never exceeds IS yards a second. Yet
the 50-miles-an-hour speed of the ’roos aforesaid
means nearly 241 yards a second. Which all shows
that the Aboliar should beware of the Man with die
Mathematical Gun. I have seen much money change
hands over horse-and-deerhound matches. The horse
always won, because the confidence of the dog-
backers was founded on an optical illusion. The
smaller animal, when performing alone, always
seems to be getting over the ground faster than the
larger could possibly shift himself; but, when the
Iwo come together, the deerhound, though he gener-
ally gets a preliminary lead, is always collared
within the furlong, and well beaten over the, mile.
But neither horse nor dog nor ’roo is “in it” with
that speediest of all animals —for a sprint—the
cheetah. This flier has been timed, “from cart to
buck,” 18 seconds; and the afterwards-measured
distance was 216 yards, giving a velocity of 27
yards to the second, or at the rate of about 55-J miles
an hour.
“L. 8.” : “Montalex,” usually so very cor-
rect on natural-history matters, confuses (B.
13/7/’ll) the basking shark with the bone shark,
and speaks of them as one and the same creature.
They are entirely distinct. The basking shark,
common to the west coast of Ireland (especially
in the summer months), is a huge creature, but has
no baleen. The bone shark lives in tropic seas
only, has small baleen plates like those of the or-
dinary fin-back, and measures 50ft. or 60ft. It is
very pronounced in coloration—irregular black
patches on a yellow ground. There, are (or were up
to 1882) several baleen plates in the Custom house
at New Bedford (U.S.A.), extracted from the only
bone shark that was ever taken by a New London
whaleship. It was killed by a bomb in shallow
water off Mindanao.
“Rechab”: You might tell “K.P.,” in an-
swer to his question (B. 29/6/’ll), that I am a
sober Abo., who has seen a pure yellow snake. 1
was driving about two miles out of Port Pirie, when
the mare suddenly tried to climb into the waggon-
ette and throw her arms around my neck. At the
same time a big yellow snake made even going for
the scrub. Despite the fact that I called on him
to stand, he got clear away. He wouldn’t even
give his name, so all I know about him is that he
was about 7ft. long and as yellow as the 12th of
July.

“Warradulla” : I enter the Darling River
’possum for “Jimmie Crow’s” Friendliest Bus'i
Animal Cup. He is the only beast in the running.
My swag and I camped one sultry night on the banks
of the Big Trickle some distance below Ivinchega
station. The spot was infested with the tree-clim-
bers. After boiling Billy, I had nearly finished my
meal, when I found myself being quizzed by four
ring-tailed ’possums, who sat on their haunches around
me. I threw them a few crusts of my latest sod.
which they ate without immediately fatal results.
Then I rolled in. At daylight 1 awoke with a pained
sense of being hanged. Starting up, I discovered a
burly ’possum with his tail looped round my throat.
The scene made my sleepy eyes bulge with incre-
dulity. The fire was blazing merrily, the billy boil-
ing, a ’possum was empyting tea into it, another was
sweetening it, a third was selecting a gum-stick,
with which he presently carried the billj' to me and
carefully deposited it at my feet. I chucked them a
bit more damper, and they scampered off after giving
three cheers and singing “Auld Lang Syne.”

“Brass Buttons'’: Why is it that in these
company-floating days some shindykit doesn’t get
out and round up the sharks that pervade these
waters? There is good money in the game, I be-
lieve, for there’s a whole lot of things that the car-
case can be used for. The liver produces good oil;
in some parts of the world the mainstay ,of the
shark-fishing industry is the cod-liver oil of com-
merce which the shark provides. Shark fins are im-
ported to this country for the use of epi-
curean Chows, and if that’s not carrying
coals to Newcastle I want to know. The
skins can be cured and worked up into
fancy ’baccy pouches, etc., and the teeth
sold to the curio shops for the tourist.
Finally the carcase turns into first-rate
fertiliser, though, I suppose,
if the business once got fairly
going the Dago fish-shops
wouldn’t let us waste good
“sehnapper” in that way. In
Iceland the fishermen have a
curious item in their shark-
catching operations. The boats
are rigged with a gallows-like
fixture, and when a shark is
hooked he is hauled up to
this. A noose is then slipped
over his tail and hauled taut;
then the patient is slit
and his liver extracted.
Before his hook is chopped
out of his jaw, his eyes
are poked out. They say
that if they don’t do this
the shark would come back
and take another bait, and
having no liver to give in
exchange he would be a
nuisance. Any ordinary
fish would have his appe-
t i t e spoiled b y the ex-
traction of his liver, etc.,
but I’ve seen enough of
sharks to believe pretty
nearly anything about
their persistence.

“Yub”: Was out rabbit shooting one re-
cent snowy morning, and on going to pick up the

“Montales”: To “Chloe" (B. 33/7/T1).
Naturalists are like horses —“they run” (that is,
“naturalise”) “in all shapes.” Some of them, like
those “Chloe” was unlucky enough to strike, arc
certainly of the dry-as-dust order, but I have known
not a few who were wet-as-beer. In fact, the best
practical naturalist Australia has produced was wet-
as-anything-he-could-get. The naturalist proper does
not stop short at beetles and butterflies. His study
of Nature culminates in the study of Man, and, in-
cidentally, of the sex which “Chloe’s” pen-name
would appear to claim for her. Consequently, he
is generally a man of genial nature and social dis-
position, armed with so many knowledges that there
is no need for him to inflict his technology upon
those'to whom it would be but a weariness of spirit.
“Chloe’s” mummies were probably naturalists only,
and I have always paradoxically maintained that the
naturalist only is not a naturalist at all. The labels
“F.R.Z.S.” and “F.R.A.S.” —to which I plead guilty
—are not to be obtained by any mere smatterer in
science.; yet, if I should ever have the pleasure of
meeting“'Chloe,” she would find me just as ready as
herself to talk about “ships and shoes and sealing-wax
and cabbages and kings.”

“Gip”: “0.L.” can tell those squatters
who argue that a fox has never been proved to have
killed a sheep, that it has been proved all right in
the Monaro (N.S.W.) district. Three of them were
seen to kill a healthy wether last week, and two
others were driven off a ewe, but not before her
tongue and ears were torn away. We’re laying
poison baits thickly, and getting results that \ve
couldn’t get a year ago when I asked for advice in
this page. Then we Were enclosing strychnine in
small squares of suet; and, although the baits were
taken, we never got the corpse. Now we are put-
ting the poison outside, toasting the bait on one
side so that the sudden death will adhere to the fat.
Got a bag of eight last week in one gully, within a
few yards of the baits, and three more a short dis-
tance away. But the brutes have grown wise to
made-up dishes in the locality, and if I want any
more six-shilling pelts I’ll have to try elsewhere,
and the recollection of a dying weaner makes me
want them very bad. In that case the blood was
trickling from an empty eye-socket, one ear was
eaten and probably the tongue—-I hadn’t the heart
to 100k —and a hole had been bitten in the poor
creature’s back. My skinning-knife was sharp, but
I hadn’t the pluck to end the little thing’s misery,
and there wasn’t a man within miles; but if I’d
got those foxes 1

“Tiaki’s” account of a day out: —
You know, I terra te missis I not ket te trunk now
1 pe te Mormon. Te nlissis he werry preese. Werra,
te time I see Rutene in te town he terra me cum an’
get te trink. I terrim I te Mormon; I no get te
trunk now. Rutene, he say, “Oh, tchust te one pia,
tass all.” Py korry, I tink werry try. Rutene, he
sout; ten I tink te petta way I sout for Rutene, too;
ten te parmaid, he sout, ten Rongo an’ Rangi conic
in an’ I ket te krad in an’ I sout. I tanse te haka,
preak te winta. I sout ewery man —pakeha an’ all.
I say, “To Herra te Mormon.” Me an’ Rutene (he
te kusun for me, you know) we get te fis’ suppa.
Py korry, no good tat ferra kip te fis’ sop ; he sharge
me fiwe pob—he say we eat orra fis’ in te prace. 1
wanna fight him. He sav to pring te preeceman. 1
terra him I fight him, too, quick—l not friten’ enny
man. He kimme te pig kraw-fis’ an’ say te bes’ way
Iko home. Oh, werra, I tink he te tecen’ ferra, so I
'ko pack te pa. Py korry, my missis he ket te
spike. i I terra ’im I karn’ herrup it —Rutene, he
make te sout. My missis, he say I te riar, an’ he
krear out wit’ Hawea nex’ day. Py korry, good job.
I tink I ket Himerangi te petta wahine than te
missis. I terra you.
A NOCTT RNE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To be sung slow and sad-like, and rather wail-
somely; I di.nno the musical terms.
The night is done and overdone, and burnt a bit at
that;
It is the time when She puts out the milk jug and
the cat ;
It is the time when bad men rise, prepared to do a
crime—-
And this, in many a happy home, is Curtain Lecture
time.
An hour goes past (or we go past, it doesn’t matter
whi ch),
I know the time—the wife’s dear voice begins to get
its pitch ;
I burrow deeper, edgeways, as one in slumber deep ;
Her elbow digs my side: “Now, don’t pretend to be
asleep !”
1 doze. She turns away (thank God!). I wake
upon the rack ;
She is at rest, her icy feet are planted in my back !
It's no use wriggling off—they’ll warm, and that
will soon be right!
I settle down to listen to the Voices of the Night.
Another drowsy interlude, and I’m awake, and
wide . . .
1 thought she had begun again, but those were
sounds outside;
Strange voices as in anguish, but I know the voice
of him —
1 know it’s Dooly (Thomas cat) engaged in combat
grim.
I slip out, careful as a cat, and nip the blind aside,
And look into the broad moonlight, and there I note
with pride,
Upon the slippery wash-house roof, unbarracked for,
alone,
Beset by three great mangy Toms, our Dooly holds
his own!
lie was a hay-and-cornstore cat before he came to
me
(Or I stole him from his loneliness, and greed and
cruelty) ;
To live he had to catch the mice in hay-and-corn-
store days,
And when he’d nothing else to eat he’d whet his
teeth on maize.
To save a pint or two of grain (’twould make a stone
cat swear)
They’d shut him up from Saturday till Monday
mornjng there.
On holidays his claws he stropped on hay-and-corn-
store bags,
Until each bunch of schnapper-hooks could tear a
cat to rags.
The last strange Tom goes backward from the wash-
house roof with growls,
And strikes a loosened roost-end (whence complaint
of wakened fowls) ;
The dogs cease to congratulate, and peace is with
the night.
And Mrs. Dooly from/'a ledge sat looking on the
fight.
And I retire to rest once more, ere day be well
begun;
The council clock is striking four, which means it’s
just on one. . . .
I thought I heard the wheels of Time go hast’ning
to decay—
’Tis but twp men connected with a lantern and a
dray.
They take me back to boyhood scenes I ne’er may
see again—
The moonlight and the horse and dray and those
nocturnal men ;
The wash-dirt stealers that we caught (but these
be just and right) ;
The honest, jovial citizens; the Pilgrims of the
Night!
N.S'.W.^
HENRY LAWSON.

'Eddyson" : Collett is conspicuous in my
gallery of cranks. Collett’s weakness was an irre-
sistible disposition to go to law. He would sooner
go to law than to meals. It was a grievous misfor-
tune to be Henry Collett’s neighbor, since he had no
use for neighbors but as defendants. Liven was his
particular victim. AVhen Liven’s skewbald pony
shied at a traction-engine, and threw him on his
head into Collett’s bed of mignonette, Collett sued
him for trespass. Three times he sued Liven for acts
of misconduct on the part of Liven’s dog Oliver, and
then Liven got rid of the dog. But that made no
difference to Collett; he took Liven to court if his
rooster crowed or his hens came over the fence. In
sheer disgust Liven purchased another tyke, a liver-
colored animal with black patches. This was notor-
iously a stay-at-home terrier; but it had not been
behind Liven’s palings for a fortnight before Collett
was out with a charge of malicious assault against
Tige. He had the wound in his trousers to show.
In court he told a cirpumstantial story of being
dashed at in the darkness, and severely bitten in the
wearing apparel. He described the aggressor as a
liver-colored dog with black spots. He was positive
it was Liven’s dog. lie had often been threatened
by the same ferocious animal. He went in fear and
trembling because of its frantic demonstrations.
Liven denied that his dog was capable of such un-
becoming conduct. “Here’s me trowziz t’ prove it,”
veiled Collett, brandishing the fractured garment be-
fore the Bench. “And here’s my dog to prove it,”
said Liven. The dog was brought into court. He
was stuffed! (A’erdict for defendant, with costs.)

“W. King”: Lodge a protest against

Brand watch the process, and go green with envy.
AVhen not adding to the population of the blankets,

herd) to muster and yard 1500 sheep, and after
wiping the sweat off itself, would climb on to a
rafter and watch me enjoying my pipe. But just
murmur ‘Rats!” and he would get as busy as a
AVowser who has scented a half-crown donation.

“The Moke”: If nominations have not
closed I want to enter my black colt Philip, of
Torrens Creek (Q.), for the Aboriginal Hilarity
Stakes. Philip had been bending too close to the fire
while cooking his evening gohanna, and his whiskers
caught alight and got singed. Next day, appearing
at the works with his facial decorations frayed at
the edges, he was asked by the boss what had hap-
pened. The nigger grinned as he replied: “Had
plurry pfellar bush fire last night, boss.”
* * * *
“Gip” : Climbing trees is not by any means
a new habit of the foxes. Have seen them shot from
high branches here (Monaro, N.S.AV.), mostly in the
spring. A friend of mine had quite a flirtation
with a tree-climbing fox yesterday. - She raced up a
tree, popped into a hollow and watched the pro-
eedings from the window; but as often as the gun
was brought to bear she coyly withdrew. “You
ain’t goin’ to play peep-bo with me all day,” said
the bushman indignantly, and bashed the tree so
suddenly with a log that the startled lady gathered
up her skirts and leaped for the open. The rest was
easy.
* * * *
“Gouger”: What comical beggars the
spotted brown bower birds are when they get a bit
tame! Two or three call at our camp every morn-
ing for any scraps or damper crust that may be
lying about, and in return they give us an enter-
tainment in mimicry, imitating the cat, the dog
and the hush birds perfectly. But one of them is
a real dandy. My mate has a chronic cough which
makes me anxious sometimes, and early the other
morning, when I heard him coughing and spitting
more distressingly than usual, I got out of the sunk
to see if I could do anything for him. To my sur-
prise when I laised the flap of the tent I couldn’t see
him, and I was going back quite mystified, when I
heard a whistle above me. It was one of the bowers.
He cocked his snaky neck sideways at me, and
coughed and spluttered tike a whole galvanic bat-
tery of asthma. I had nothing to offer him buf> a
handful of language, and I gave him the lot un-
grudgingly.

“Wang”; In reply to a recent paragraph
of mine salting the cow-cocky for his bowelless
treatment of the young unfortunates that get into

when I say that the alleged food some of those State
children are given would make a dingo toss up
whether to eat it or go hungry.

“The Lyre”: vVhite ants in the Bunda-
noon (N.S.AV.) district have formed the tobacco
habit. I haven’t seen them smoking cigarettes to
any extent, but I know they favor the pipe, i had
the strongest pipe in the State, and it was not until
my friends forsook me, my children rose up in
anger, and my wife spoke openly of a judicial
separation that I decided to part from that pipe.
Then I buried it. A few weeks afterwards, I resur-
rected the old friend, and found it absoblankylutely
riddled by white ants.
“Windy”: Snakes can not only “charm”
birds; they can do the same thing to other snakes.
I have seen them at it. Have also seen a snake
charm a lizard in the manner described (8.29/6/’ll).
I know an old battler who swears that a snake can
charm a man; he was charmed himself for two
hours, he says. He doesn’t know whether the snake’s
intentions were strictly honorable or not. Still he
reckons the beast was a bit greedy, and wanted din-
ner enough for two navvies on one plate. Besides, it
wasn’t good whisky. As to the two parts of a snake
trying to join themselves, it’s a fairly common ex-
perience. I cut a snake’s hindquarters off one even-
ing, and buried them, and the next morning, when I
was passing, the snake was wriggling about looking
for its rump. I always bury the head now.
* * * *
“Feenix”: “Geofred” (B. 13/7/’ll) is
vrong when he puts the canary among the birds that
will burst up the happy home if a hand touches the
eggs. Have been breeding these birds for 15 years,
and always, during the season, take the seeds from
the nest as they are laid; when the third arrives
I replace the lot and allow the hen to sit. By doing
this you can generally make certain of your birds
hatching on the one day, and the youngsters, being
then all of equal strength, have the iame chance
in the handicap.

“R. A. G”: Very glad “K. P.” (B.
29/6/’ll) saw that yellow snake, because it removes
a suspicion cast upon my otherwise untarnished
character by several people, when I mentioned an
interview I had with a similarly decorated reptile
while prospecting between Teesdale (Etheridge
line) and Oaks Rush (N.Q.). He was 3ft. 6in.,
bright yellow, and crawled over the rocks near where
I was boiling the billy. I offered him the shovel
with all the politeness I could put into my biceps,
but it didn’t reach him, and he disappeared in the
water of the creek. I presume, therefore, he be-
longs to the water tribe, and, failing the scientific
name, may be classed with AYowsers generally.
“Shangeye”: “Snakebite” (B. 15/6/T1)
says there are no rabbits in South Gippsland, and
while “Rabbit-trap” and others are after his gore I
want to sidle in with the whisper that there arc
none in North Gippsland. At Moondarra (now called
Harris, after the late Albert Harris, M.L.A.) rabbits
have never been seen, though hares are rather plenti-
ful. Ask any cocky the cause of bunny’s absence,
and he will say the hare. He seems to 'think the
hare is a cannibal. Personally I don’t go much on
this yarn, and would take it kindly if anybody in
Bunny’s confidence would supply me with a better
one.

“Climax”: During the late autumn and
early winter in Central, Western, and S. Western
Queensland this year the übiquitous blowfly put tip
a good performance amongst the sheep. Although
it was not his first appearance, it was his most
strenuous, many stations having to take to crutching,
hitherto almost unknown. Amongst the many and
varied methods tried for his destruction was the
taking of half a bullock (lumpy or other worthless
beast), an empty oildrum, and some arsenic to a
sheep camp. The arsenic was boiled in water, and
hunks of meat dipped in the soup, and hung on trees
in the hope that the buzzy inhabitants of the neigh-
borhood would be poisoned to an insect. I wasn’t
able to see the scheme worked out, but I anticipated
that it would be merely a waste of good arsenic. Can
anybody with a first-hand knowledge enlighten me?
SOCIALISM DEFIED: A LEGEND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At the Farmers and Settlers’ Conference, Mr. ,J.
Wetherspoon, M.L.C., referred to the encroach-
ments of unionism, as s’.iovvn in the claims of the
Rural Workers’ Union. The agitators who were fos-
tering this movement, he contended, aimed at in-
vading the sanctity of the home, and interfering be-
tween parents and children. There would he no
such invasion of Ins home, he assured his audience.
He had a gun, and his son had a gun, and he added
with some warmth, “We won’t be afraid to use them,
1 can tell you.” —Sydney Telegraph.
The Honorable James Smith-Brown,
The Anti-Labor M.L.C.,
Resided in a district where
Australia’s great cow-industry
Prevailed. He made vast stacks of coin,
And was as happy as could be.
Year in, year out, his progeny
Leapt from their beds before the sun,
And rounded up and bailed the cows,
And milked them deftly, one by one.
Brown’s youngsters drew no wage for this;
They did it, as it were, for fan.
The milking o’er, the yards were cleaned,
The house was swept, the beds were made;
Pigs, horses, fowls and such were fed,
And then Brown’s morning meal was laid.
His kids did all these little jobs,
And none of them, of course, was paid.
One rift there was within Brown’s lute:
His children labored with a will,
Both boys and girls were strong as mules,
They milked with wondrous speed and skill,
Excepting always one—Brown’s Cros3,
His sickly youngest offspring, Bill.
Bill hardly even earned his hoard,
So weak he was. His visage thin,
And frail, emaciated frame,
And hollow eyes and sallow skin
Moved Brown to rage. Bill’s uselessness
Brown looked on almost as a Sin.
Bill rose at four o’clock each morn
And sought the cow-hails with the rest;
He grubbed and dug and fenced by day
With all a grown-up worker’s zest;
But that he was incompetent,
It must, with sorrow, be confessed.
Eftsoon a Labor Government
Gained office, and a flood or mass
Of Socialistic Acts and things
Proceeded ruthlessly to pass —-
Which ill-considered actions stunned
The members of the Better Class.
And thus it came about, one day,
While Bob was chopping wood, and Lil
Was littering the pigs, and Maud
And Joe were ploughing on the hill,
A Laborite arrived and asked
About the health of little Bill.
His luck was out: Smith-Brown was in!
With yells of fury Brown reviled
Such wanton interference ’twixt
A parent and his lawful child.
The Labor emissary quailed
Before the ancient’s anger wild.
“Mv gun!” Smith-Brown howled finally;
“He’s brought it on his own rash head!
That gore would follow deeds like this,
I’ve often in the Council, said.”
So speaking, he took careful aim,
And shot the interloper dead.
Absurd though it may seem, Smith-Brown
Was forced for this to face a Court.
The Judge and jury—honest men—
However, cut the business short.
Brown pleaded “The Unwritten Law”:
His prosecution came to naught.
Bill perished shortly afterwards:
The steady overwork which kills,
Poor food, ill-cooked, and chronic dirt,
And lack of sleep, and constant chills,
Had all combined to saddle him
AVith half a hundred deadly ills.
Smith-Brown is still alive and well.
AA’herever “Lib’ral” banners wave
llis name is reverenced as one
AVho did his level best to save
A father’s sacred privilege
To sweat his offspring to the grave.
N.S.AV,
