THE BEST YOU CAN TAKE.
“I had colic and diarrhoea, and the pains were so
bad that 1 would have taken anything,” writes Mr.
Harry Vernon Wyatt, of New Brighton, N.Z., “but
one dose of Chamberlain’s Colic, Cholera and Diar-
rhoea Remedy fixed me right up. Repeatedly I have
given this remedy to people in the town, and they
swear by it. There is no question about it, Cham-
berlain’s Colic, Cholera and Diarrhoea Remedy is the
best you can take for colic, cholera and diarrhoea.”*
Spring Modes.
Slender lines prevail. There may be another half-
yard in the width of the skirt, but the Spring Show
in David Jones Ltd., which includes the newest
French models, is adamant on the subject of cir-
cumference. Race coats —those in black, with
splashes of emerald green in cuffs and revers, an a
black with rich cream applique, are special tempta-
tions —are exhibited in varied and original designs.
As worn at Longchamps, the draped effect is noticed
on the handsomest coats. Soft silks, flung in lus-
trous folds over show-stands, give David Jones s rooms
the fasc nation of an Eastern bazaar. Colors also are
go{ t_pastel tones of green, blue and pink are blended
together on the new chene silks. Designs are rather
large—coin spots, scrolls and squares. Lace ana em-
broidery—much of it wrought by hand—is used even
more freely this season on lingerie frocks. the
sleeveless little lace, muslin, or chiffon mantle—an
airy fairy garb of conspicuous charm —is another
feature of David Jones Ltd.’s alluring display of
feminine fashions. In millinery the Lancerfeather
struggles with the “weeping plume f« r P™ e
place The elegant mannequin, parading fragile
muslin and beautifully-built tailor-mades before the
admiring glances of her own sex, adds a new vim to
shopping at David Jones Ltd.
PERSONAL
ITEMS
According to a London book-circular, some
unpublished verse by Sydney’s late Bishop
Smith are to be issued shortly under the
title of “Capernaum and Other Poems.”
His sisters are credited (or otherwise)
with the editorial work.
A young Maorilander, w r ho can’t help his
name being Diamond Jenness, has made
such a success of anthropological study at
Balliol, Oxford, that the authorities are
sending him out to report on the manners
of the natives of New Guinea and neigh-
boring islands.
JULIUS, ACTING
PRIMATE OF
MAORILAND.
The Comte Alphonse de Fleurieu, who is
a great-nephew of the French Minister of
Marine who sent out La Perouse and the
D’Entrecasteaux in the seventeen-nineties,
is touring Tasmania in search of informa-
tion with regard to early French explora-
tions on the coast. He wants the names
given by French explorers restored to their
“proper places” on Australian maps, and is
billed for a lecture on the subject before the
Royal Society.
George Jenkins’s “Reminiscences” were
one of the jokes of the Victorian Parlia-
ment. Several times a day the pompous old
fellow would breathe confidentially into
someone’s ear that he was soon going to
begin them. But, alas! the effort of dress-
ing and dining left S’George no time to put
his secrets into writing. It is even credible
that Victorian histox*y lost a little by this;
for George found many opportuixities of
meeting Australian politicians in moments
of indiscreet self-rex-elation.
ENDING THEIR SHIFT.
I What is the cause of so many railway accidents? The whole show is
“nervy,” and getting worse daily.— Vic. railway man.
BYSTANDER: “What! been another railway disaster?"
PORTHER : “Oh, no; only some of our staff knocking off work."
“Wild Wright,” whose exploits and
alleged exploits in the Kelly country are
supposed to have had something to do with
turning the youngsters’ thoughts to bush-
ranging, died the other week at Newcastle
Waters, on the Overland Telegraph Line.
Of late years he had been on the droving
tracks between the Territory and North
Queensland; and malaria beat him while
the old chap—quite harmless in latter days
—was travelling with Wave Hill cattle.
Manoel, the dispossessed Dago of Portu-
gal, is said to be spending a large portion of
the fortune he didn’t earn in an unheroic
endeavor to win back his precarious job as
caretaker of the throne. This Rings him
down to the levei of the exiled president of a
gunpowdery South American republic, who,
after squandering the country’s revenues on
a necklace for the prima-donna of a second-
rate opera co., exits on a rust-streaked
banana boat, and spends the rest of his life
intriguing in a New York boarding-house.
George Judah Cohen, chairman of half a
dozen of the biggest concerns in Sydney, has
just completed 50 years of commercial life.
He is the son of George Cohen, who, in the
first ’so’s, occupied a house in O’Connell-
street—a house which still stands, and was
long noted for the two splendid pines that
stood in front of it. “George Judah” —to
distinguish him from all the other Cohens —
was born on the Tank stream, near wiiere
the A.M.P. offices now are; he is head of a
firm which was established in Maitland three-
quarters of a century ago.
Harry Barnett, just dead in Perth, was a
popular sportsman in Adelaide for years, and
a prominent broker during the Broken Hill
boom. Hopelessly handicapped by Nature,
he yet kept his end up in various ways,
being notably brilliant at billiards, owner of
Quality and Jericho (an Adelaide Cup win-
ner), and on the committee of Tattersall’s.
In 1908, the Westralian Turf Club imported
him as a stipendiary steward, and at the
time of his death he was chairman.
E. H. Cameron, M.L.A. (Vic.), reached his
eightieth year on July 25. Having been a mem-
ber of the Assembly for 37 years, E. H. is
the father of the House; so members of both
Chambers met in a committee room on the
birthday and drank champagne enthusiasti-
cally. Many nice remarks were made about
Cameron, who is credited with having been
the prince of all whips. That was during
the regime of the Gillies Government. And,
strenuous fighter as he was, he never made
an enemy. There were several other octo-
genarians at the gathering, including
Davies, Balfour (author of the historic Dar-
ling grant), and Harwood, all M’s.L.C. Each
of these declared that he was still young,
and threatened to live to middle age.
Artist John Longstaff left Sydney early
this week for Melbourne, and thence starts
back to Europe in about a week’s time. Less
than two months ago he landed alongside
the Harbor with the idea of having a holi-
day ; since then he has painted four big por-
traits and two little ones —and one of the
four, that of Professor MacCallum, is just
about as good as anything he has ever done.
So the holiday has had to wait; there hasn’t
even been time for any play with brushes
and paint in the sunlight, and Longstaff
badly wanted to enjoy himself that way.
But he promises himself something better in
two or three years, when he hopes to be
back in the country where he admits he is
more stimulated to work than in any other
place in the world.
“Ben” Alley, who has been trading to the
Richmond River district (N.S.W.) for half
a century, has crossed the bar for the last
time. “Old Ben” knew every eddy and cur-
rent in the river, and, when he wouldn’t risk
the entrance, not another skipper would
look at it. On the northern rivers the coast-
ing skippers are the most important persons
going; on them depends communication with
the markets. And, when Ben Alley failed
them, the River folk knew that human effort
had done its best.
Wetherspoon, M.L.C., who told the
Farmers and Settlers’ Conference in Syd-
ney that he had a gun and his sons had
guns, and they would know what to do with
the weapons if the State started to “inter-
fere” with his farm-workmen’s hours and
pay, lives at Glencoe, near Glen Innes. Up
that way they think the results might be
much more dreadful if Wetherspoon recited
something from a volume of “verse” which
he once fired at a defenceless public—
“Warblings from the Bush.”
The once-notorious “Dan” Mayne died on
July 26 at Bathurst (N.S.W.), aged 85
years. He was a native of the Hawken-
bury, and one of triplets; his father was a
maker of nails, and Dan was apprenticed
to a shoemaker. After a trip with cattle to
Melbourne via Bourke, Dan settled at
Bathurst, commencing as a trainer of race-
horses for George Lee, M.L.C., and others,
and he had a few good ones of his own.
Later on, he started the Bathurst Sen-
tinel, and made it a very warm production.
On the staff in its early days were A. G.
Taylor, Richmond Thatcher, John Hunt
and others who became well known in after
life. Dan got into more libel actions than
any other newspaper proprietor of his time,
and he fought most of them with tooth and
claw. After leasing the Sentinel he re-
turned to Windsor, but went back to the old
battlefield to die.
From an old M.L. Gallery hand :
T. E. Taylor, M.H.R., who died last week, was a
great joy on many a long, dreary night in the Gal-
lery when Seddon was monarch of all he surveyed.
He could raise Cain at a minute’s notice, and —-
from 1902 to 1905, anyhow—-he mostly did it.
Those nights flash back now, with “Tommy’s” lean,
striking figure standing above a squirming House
and lacing every man who backed into the track of
his “few remarks.” He’s had them so infuriated
that they would flay one another; and whenever I
get down-hearted, I think of one grey dawn when
an aged and whiskered legislator staggered from the
lobbies with gore streaming from his nose into his
hands held scoopwise. Straight up to Seddon he
walked, and, “Look at me nose!” he wailed. “Bah,”
said old Dick; “take it away! ” I’m glad Taylor
died fighting. Not many days before the end, he, as
Mayor of Christchurch, presided over a meeting at
which Tory leader Massey was billed to speak, but
which terminated in a riot. The chairman of the
Deform League blamed Taylor for the trouble.
“What!” snarled Tommy. “If you were not an old
man I would not keep my hands off you.” If T. E.
Taylor accidentally drops into the place to which
so many people have consigned him, the head cook
will have to keep his spiked tail on a hair-trigger.
A rather remarkable man got on to an
outgoing boat last week—J. D. Williams.
Just under two years ago he arrived with a
heap of Yankee notions in his head and no
great weight in his pocket. He saw what
was going to happen in the picture-show
business, and he jumped in, carrying his
Yankee experience with him. First came
the Colonial Theatre Co. That had a capi-
tal of a thousand or two —and a good many
of the shares were taken by people who
were given work to do in getting the show
place ready. In plain words, they “took it
out” in shares. The J. D. Williams Co.
came next. That was a bit bigger. Then fol-
lowed the Greater J. D. Williams Co., to
absorb the rest; and this has a capital of
£150,000. Three picture theatres are run-
ning in Sydney now, and one in Melbourne:
and half a dozen more are in view in various
places. And besides the £150,000 co. (with
the shares at over 50 per cent, premium),
two or three other cos. have been whipped
up. It is a pretty fair record in hustle for a
man who dropped in, a perfect stranger, less
than two years ago.
Appendicitis is having another innings in
Sydney, among the sufferers being It. E. P.
Hickson, chairman of the Harbor Trust, and
lately chairman of the Decentralisation
Commission. The patriarch—he is the oldest
public servant in an important office in
N. S. Wales —is just on to 70.
Captain Edwin, for many years Maori-
land’s official weather prophet, has gone to
regions where the temperature is never
sultry. He was an old R.N. man with a
service record that included the Crimean.
China and Maori wars. Though he left the
Navy as far back as 1871, he was still only
71 at his death.
The Adelaide Bench isn’t very hilarious.
Chief Justice Way’s wit is bitter, but his
frank and frequent yawns and the way he
pushes the horsehair back in order to
scratch his head are redeeming features of
many a dull case. He laughs in office hours
more than any of the others, and likes to
“reminisce” on the Bench. In a recent pro-
longed assault case he appeared delighted to
recollect the matter of a clergyman whose
assailant simply waved a stick over his
head and said, “Consider yourself thrashed.”
The clergyman considered as he was told,
and the jury assessed his humiliation at
£2OO. Judge Gordon —who was “Jaunty
Johnny” when he was in politics—is as
dignified as a dromedary and doesn't make
jokes; neither does Judge Iloinburg (not.
intentionally, anyhow;. And Russell, of the
Insolvency Court, hasn't time to grin, much
less laugh.
“Scribe” gives two good ones a joint send-
off :
Two Melbourne pressmen have just gone—Jack
Nish and Jack Harding. Nish came from the peer-
less Bendigo fount. What is the printing ink ele-
ment in the Sandhurst air ? Nish’s father, Dr. Nish,
of St. Andrew’s, Clerk of the Victorian Presbyterian
Assembly, was unquestionably the best cleric for the
business of church finance ever produced or educed
by Australia. I have a vivid memory of the style
in which he twiddled the whole Assembly round his
little finger. Nish, the younger, was a slap-up, all-
round pressman. He might have produced a Ben-
digo Advertiser all by himself. I think he was
rather lost as an Argus sub-editor, though, undoubt-
edly he was efficient. I only recollect one mistake of
his, and Topp shared that. Both fell into a hole through
making a trifling correction in a rather awkward
correspondent, and there was the worry of an action.
Everybody agrees that another worry shortened
Nish’s life—his trusteeship in the Alf Joseph estate.
We all hoped that many years were before him in his
easier billet on the Herald, and truly he had re-
cuperated in a wonderful manner. J. T. T. Harding
was much less known. Many years ago he was on
the Age, and rapidly wrought up to a good position.
He turned newspaper man through Civil Service re-
ductions ; later on, offered his old billet again, he
went back. Yet this was a man, who, in my opinion,
knowing him well, might have workpd to the highest
place in journalism.
“LOST HIS PUNCH."
Three ex-Governors of Maoriland are on
a London committee which wants to erect
a statue in Wellington to Edward Gibbon
Wakefield, for his services in promoting emi-
gration to the South. Mr. Wakefield was a
gentleman who lived down a “past,” and be-
came prominent in schemes for land settle-
ments in S. Aus. and Maoriland.
AT THE CLUB.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The meeting decided to establish a Liberal club, to
unite the Liberal forces of the State, and to provide
a place of meeting for members of the party.— Perth
paper.
Club meets, and members gather. Enter first
Mister Mac Sweat and old Sir John Obese,
Both of the firm of Fat, Obese and Co.,
Mumbling and grumbling. “Folly!” cries Sir .John,
“This pampering of workmen ! Bless my soul,
Why can’t men live on seven bob a day?”
Whereto Mac Sweat bows low and mumbles back,
“You’re right, Sir John.” (Sir John’s the senior
partner.)
The fat knight drops into a seat, and cries,
“Steward! Where’s that damn steward? Bring me
whisky.”
Anon come others—members fat and lean;
Sour-faced and hungry-gutted; red and paunched;
Employers and employerdom, who rail
Quite in the old Emplo3'ers’ Union way:
“Class legislation! Confiscation! Faugh!
Where’s freedom? Jack’s his master’s master now!
It’s ignorance on horseback ! Where’ll they be
When Foreign Capital is scared away?
My boy, WE made this country ! Pioneers !
Ay, weren’t we pioneers ? Look at Sir John:
He pioneered a hundred thousand acres,
And got ’em for a song. Those were the days !
Steward ! Where’s that damn steward ? Bring more
whisky.”
Amid this chorus, note the “Bright Young Men”
Of Liberal instinct, fawning round the chairs;
Applauding here and sympathising there,
Which means —a gift of Influence from Mac Sweat,
A brief or two from Shark and Co., perhaps
A nomination into politics,
Or (for the doctors) by-and-bye a chance
To flesh a lance in Lady Blank’s appendix.
Butter your bread by being “Liberal” !
Meantime —confound that steward ! —have a whisky.
“More immigrants!” That’s Adipose’s voice,
Which promptly adds, “Sir John, have one with
me.”
Forthwith the talk is strong on immigration.
“Plenty of immigrash’n—thswash we want;
Then put these unions backsh their proper place/’
So says Fitzgrunt, who isn’t used to whisky,
Though very used to paying factory fines.
And while the steward dodges here and there,
You hear the grumble and the muttered roar,
‘ ‘lmmigrants—-wages—hamper enterpri se —
What’s Gov’ment doing?—agitators—bah !
All ruined —country sick —damned Socialism.”
With what deep unanimity will moan
The frenzied “Liberals” in the Liberal club !
westralta.
B.

This good Australian gum-tree needs no paint. Its
general manager may be 79 ; and mostly when a man
is 79, he says, “Leave well enough alone!” But this
one isn’t content to stand still. Here is the remark-
able change in.the figures in 1.0 years: —
Liabilities.
June, June, Increase
1901. 1911. p.c.
Notes and bills .. £1,472,840 £1,370,999
Deposits.. .. .. 11,557,615 20,090,168 73‘9
Assets.
Liquid assets .. .. 4,922,263 11,539,087 134"4
Advances .. .. 9,771,028 12,48 a, 627 27’8
Premises, etc 426,900 6i2,1i3 20'0
The slignt shrinkage in the first line is due to the
introduction of Government notes to replace private
notes. Everywhere else there is growtn; and tne
biggest growth is where it is best. The accumula-
tion of liquid assets certainly has been on the big
side. This isn’t a complaint that has often to be
made: it is mostly the otner way about. The Com-
mercial of Sydney has, however, 54 p.c. of its total
liabilities held in liquid form ; and perfect safety can
scarcely demand that much. The management might
freely admit that. Ten years ago it held under 38
p.c., and no doubt considered its position then was
quite right. The trouble now is the lack of securities
that appeal to the management as being good enougfl.
It is unquestionably a serious thing mat one bank
should have 54 p.c. of all its deposits in cash and
other liquid forms —that is to say, not doing ordinary
banking business. And we ought to find out whit
is the matter.
This paper’s idea is that at least one-third of the
money ought to be busy helping to make for Aus-
tralia some of the tens of millions’ worth of goods
that are now made in foreign factories, with the aid
of foreign banks. Without those factories (which
we certainly shall not get without high Protection,
and assured high Protection) there is little employ-
ment for money, except in the nationally unprofitable
“industry” of land speculation. And even then, when
Jones and Brown and Smith buy stupid suburban
allotments, for which they have no use, from Robin-
son, Robinson as often as not drops the money back
into the bank; and the transaction hasn’t added one
farthing to the produced wealth of the country. Aus-
tralia is rolling in money; and it is letting it go to
waste, and this bank’s figures prove it. of
course, that this bank is making nothing out of any
of its liquid assets. It has, for instance, £1,010,375
on short call in London, and another £2,841,175 in
Consols and other Government securities. But it has
£.5,216,788 in actual coin and bullion, and there is
no direct return at all on that lot. With so large a
holding of liquid assets, very big profits in proportion
to capital and reserves are out of the question. Recent
figures have been: —

Considering the size of the liquid assets £8 p.c. is a
very good return; it is pretty evident that assets
which yield that much don’t include many lame
ducks.
In the past 12 months more liberal advances cer-
tainly appear to have been made; but that has doubt-
less been partly due to the subdivision of country
estates, thanks to the graduated land tax. The exten-
sion of old-established businesses, following on an-
other good season, has also called for more accommo-
dation. Here are the figures, showing all the move-
ments in the past year:—

The capital is £1,500,000, and the reserves have now
just shown their nose past that good, round, corpulent
figure. The liabilities exceed the capital and reserves
by slightly more than seven to one; and, though that
is usually reckoned just about as much as a bank
ought to risk, the hazard is much reduced in this
case by the very large holding of liquid assets.

The Australian Bank of Commerce (Sydney),
the Phoenix that rose from the ashes of the old A.J.S.
Bank:—
To Re- Re-
Profit. Dividends. serves, serves.
June, 1910.. £11,670 3 p.c. = £8,441 £3229 £3229
Dec., 1910.. 18,421 34,, ~ 18,103 318 3547
June, 1911.. 14,957 24,, ~ 14,550 407 3954
Six months ago The Bulletin printed the speech
which the chairman might have made if he had been
quite frank: “Gentlemen, you see we are paying you
34 p.c., and we know it is more than you ought to
get. We had only a wretched little £3229 of un-
divided profit at the end of the previous half-year;
and after you have cut up this 34 p.c. among you,
i. erc will be only £3lB to put in the stocking.” And
so on. Then another lot of directors and another
chairman arrived; and another view was taken of
the situation. The old directors, fighting then
for their lives (so far as sitting on this Board was
concerned), were naturally inclined to show share-
holders that they were doing very well for everybody.
The new Board was more inclined to say: “We arc
not responsible for anything that has happened
hitherto. Our job is to build up this bank. If we
can’t pay you a big dividend now, you know it is not
our fault. But you just wait and see what we’ll
send you by-and-bye.” So down the dividend comes
to 24 P-c., which, as the report adds, makes 3 p.c.
for the year. The one justification for the previous
34 p.c., which meant putting by a wretched £3lB to
the poor little reserves, was that the assets had been
written down so hard in the reconstruction that the
real reserves were possibly greater. The present
directors don’t say that there are not hidden reserves.
On the contrary, the chairman says: “The realisation
of the properties transferred to us bv the old bank
has proceeded very satisfactorily ... It will be satis-
factory to shareholders to know that, so far, the valu-
ations of the securities have been more than borne out
by the sales that have been made.” What has become
of the surplus is not disclosed. It may have been
passed to Profit and Loss: it will be better news to
learn later on that it has been transferred to a con-
tingency account, to make good any losses that may
be made on subsequent sales.
The paid capital is £1,180,812, and there are also
£1,730,197 of 4 p.c. inscribed deposits repayable
very gradually by a sort of sinking fund, “to be
created out of the profits, or corpus, or both, at the
rate of £15,000 half-yearly, or at the option of the
Board, on giving three months’ notice.” Half a crown
in the £ of the old deposits, carrying 3 p.c., is pay-
able in two years—that is to say, on January 1 next.
These special deposits total £416,812. All the old
paid capital was written off; and shareholders who
were liable for £2 10s. per old share, got £1 ss. in
hew shares. And, finally, 2s. 6d. in the £ of the
old deposits of the old (A.J.S.) Bank was written off.
In this way the assets were written down until they
were reduced to below present value, so far as that
value has been tested by actual sales. As one-quarter
of the old deposits was converted into share-capital
(and subsequently another small proportion), the share
capital is exceptionally large for the size of the busi-
ness at present. The liabilities, including the special
deposits previously mentioned, total £2,916,859.
The paid cap'tal and inscribed deposits together
amount to £2,911,009, so that there is more than£l
of what may be called shareholders’ capital for every
£1 of the public’s money; wherefore the security for
deposits and other debts is extraordinarily good. It
is, in fact, doubtful whether any other bank in Aus-
tralia could offer so good, and that is saying a lot.
Still, the bank’s biggest trouble is, no doubt, not to
get money, but to get customers for suitable ad-
vances. Sooner or later the A.B.C. should certainly
be able to command more custom. For, after all, it
is almost as important for a borrower to “do with”
a strong bank as it is for a depositor, because the
stronger the bank the less liable it is to have to call
on borrowers to reduce their liabilities at a time
when reduction is most difficult. It is satisfactory to
see that some progress is being made. This shows
the various changes in 12 months: —
1910. 1911.
Paid capital .. .. £958,172 £1,180,812
Inscribed deposits .. 1,859,022 1,730,197
Special deposits . . 429,317 416,812
All other debts . . 2,049,973 2,500,048
Liquid assets . . . . 1,185,979 1,417,934
Advances, etc. . . . 3,754,889 4,059,942
Premises .. . . 367,727 369,150
There are £ 305,053 more advances shown there; and
the new advances must have been greater than that,
because some of the old securities have been reduced
by sale. This is what the bank wants —more busi-
ness. It is at present a steam shovel ladling out
sugar in a grocer’s shop—a very good steam shovel,
capable of doing far more work and earning far
more money.

The City Bank of Sydney :

June, 1908.. 7,098 3 ~ ~ 6000 1098 13,423
Dec., 1908.. 7,030 3 ~ „ 6000 1030 14,453
June, 1909.. 7,055 3 „ ~ 6000 1055 15,508
Dec., 1909.. 7,088 3 ~ „ 6000 1088 16,596
June, 1910.. 9,063 34,, ~ 7000 £lOOO 1063 17.659
Dec., 1910.. 10,130 4 ~ ~ 8000 1000 1130 18,789
June, 1911.. 10,047 4 ~ „ 80u0 1000 1047 19,836
After the boom troubles of nearly 20 years ago this
bank detided to ’pull through without reconstructing.
It neither locked up any deposits, nor wrote oft' any
capital; it even went on paying first a 6 and then a
5 p.c. div. during the crisis. Unfortunately it was
only cracking hardy; and it would have been far
better had it admitted that it felt very poorly and
laid up for a while. Perhaps it didn’t suffer as
much as some; nevertheless, it was sick—very sick
indeed —and its books were chock-a-block with securi-
ties which were gradually thrown on its hands, at
.prices far above their real value. Instead of writing
these down out of capital (and reserves, of course),
the. bank struggled on, and still cracked hardy, even
when all the world could see that it was dodging the
undertaker with difficulty.
The wild-cat sickness was a good deal like the
dog disease from which Sydney has been suffering
lately: the banks that acknowledged they had it,
and went to bed, got through the trouble pretty
quickly, as a rule, while those —like the City—that
tried to keep going as though nothing was the matter
suffered five times as much in the long run. The
Commercial of Sydney went to bed, and it is just as
well and strong now as if it never had been ill: in
the past 10 years its deposits have increased from
£11,557,615 to £20,090,168, and its advances from
£9,771,028 to £12,485,627, while its profits have
just doubled. The City, on the other hand, has
increased its deposits only from £1,087,073 to
£1,637,602, and its advances from £1,202,085 to
£1,343,013. What actual increase there has been
in the profits it is impossible to say, because a great
deal of profit—perhaps most of it in some years—
has had to go towards wiping out the bad debts
which it had made but hadn’t acknowledged. In
other words, it had lost a large proportion of its
capital; but, instead of writing it off as lost, the
bank set about building it up again out of future
undisclosed profits. The trouble about that method
is that tue general public, seeing that something is
wrong, is apt to assume that things are worse than
they are. Then, rather than take risks, it passes on
to some other bank with its business.
Even now this paper hasn’t any idea as to the
real position of this bank. It unquestionably ought
to be in a vastly better way than it was, say, six
or eight years ago. If it had many of the old skele-
tons left in the cupboard, it couldn’t reasonably hope
for a better chance to get rid of them than it has had
lately. In fact, things that looked like skeletons a
few years ago have lately put on flesh and left the
premises with hardly a stain on their character. Fur-
ther, ordinary banking profits have been extraordi-
nary. All the same, the City’s visible profits are
only at the rate of £4 16s. p.c. on capital and re-
serves combined, or just 5 p.c. on capital alone.
That suggests that the bank has still got a quantity
of unproductive or overvalued assets on its books,
which is another way of saying that it still has some
lost capital to recover. Or it may be that the bank
is making more profit than it shows, but wants to
fortify itself against contingencies. In the absence
of information, the investor continues to quote the
£8 shares at from £6 to £6 55.; and that is 100
p.c. better than the quotation of a few years ago.
Though the City Bank has, through its own un-
fortunate action, to a great extent got out of the
swim, it has made a certain amount of visible pro-
gress in the 12 months: —
Liabilities.
June, 1910. June, 1911.
Deposits, etc. £1,390,146 £1,637,602 Inc. £247,456
Notes .. . . 102,346 ' 68,813 Dec. 33,533
Coin, etc. .. 453,830 526,398 Inc. 72,568
Gov. Securities 92,000 , 92,000
Premises .. 173,044 173,839 Inc. 795
Advances; .. '1,199,277, 1,343,013 Inc. 143,736
And, as hinted already, it has probably made a good
deal of progress that isn't visible. That is to say,
it has probably improved the, quality of every £1 of
advances and other assets on its books. Moreover,
though it isn’t fashionable, there is no reason what-
ever to doubt its soundness. It owes £l,-70,6,415,
and has £618,398 in liquid assets; so its non-
liquid assets only have to secure £1,088,017 of
debt; and the non-liquid assets amount to £1,516,852.
There may be a little over-valuation among them;
but they would only have to fetch £65 for every
£lOO of their book value to meet all claims. The
bank’s position from that point of view seems to be
really strong. Whether, having nursed itself back
to strength, it has the capacity to get. business and
make good profits has to be proved. Somehow a long
experience of the cheerless job of liquidation often
isn’t much of a training for the job of new-business
getting.

For many years the co. put aside large amounts, os-
tensibly to cover depreciation, but did not show
them as profit, nor were the accumulations shown
as reserves. Six months ago the Board evidently
smelt possible resumption by the State, and dfcided
that it might be better to make the figures l"Ok a
bit bigger. Internal reserves to the amount of
£79,410 were then disclosed, and called a fund to
provide for depreciation, public risk and contingen-
cies. This fund is now being visibly added to, and
the additions are shown in the foregoing as profit.
The total profit of £19,473 for the half-year is made
up as follows:
Added to fund for depreciation, etc. £3,916
Added to formal Reserve Fund.. .. 2,199
Added to P. and L. balance . . . . 13,358
Total .. .. .. ..£19,473
It-is impossible to say how much of that £3916 is
true profit. Depreciation is going on all the time,
of course ; and if it is not otherwise provided for, a
good deal of the £3916 is a set-off against the shrink-
age in value that comes with old age. But it is
highly probable—in fact, certain—that depreciation
is provided for otherwise; in other words, that ad-
ditions and renewals have been paid for out of re-
venue and charged to working expenses. Six months
ago ship property stood in the books at £168,630.
Since then, though the lvaludah has been lost, two
bigger boats have been launched ; and it seems cer-
tain that payments on that account have far ex-
ceeded £20,000. Yet the book-value stands now at
only £179,988. Six months ago the surplus of
liquid assets over liabilities was £43,850. To-day
it is £73,017, an increase of £29,167. Meantime,
£37,845 of new capital has been got in. The posi-
tion, therefore, is that the co. has out of the past
half-year’s profit paid £15,623 in dividends, and has
also made large payments on account of two new
ships, besides paying for landed properties, wharves,
etc., and all the new capital that has been drawn
upon to make those payments is £8678. Going
back over nine years we get these results: Excess of
liabilities over liquid assets, June, 1902, £14,974 ;
surplus of liquid assets, June, 1911, £73,017. The
cash position has thus improved to the extent of
£87,991. Meantime the following new capital has
been provided by the shareholders: 1902, £20,000;
1907, £40,000 ; 1910-11, £57,810 ; total, £117,810.
So the capital provided by the shareholders in nine
years has exceeded the amount by which the cash
position has improved by £117,810 minus £87,991
= £ 29,819. With that £29,819, supplemented, by
current profits, the co. has bought 10 new steamers
(in addition to the one or two lost), including the
Kanimbla, Koree, Kummulla, Kulgoa, Ivareela, Kai-
liai and Kirrule—in fact, practically all the bigger
ferries of the fleet. And it has also added at least
£75,000 to its landed properties, wharves, etc. Ex-
cept for £29,819, the whole of these new properties
and the 10 steamers have been paid for out of current
profits, Simultaneously with the payment of the co.’s
big dividends. There are not many gold mines like
that. As to the future —well, that’s not a matter
that one can tell anything about by dissecting the
accounts. It seems certain, however, that, no matter
which political party is in power, something in the
way of a new connection between the North and the
city sides of the harbor will come soon. But how
much that will cut into the profits of this co. nobody
can say. It is at least probable that better
facilities for crossing the harbor may so increase
the population of the North side that the ferry traffic
will go on growing. In whose hands that traffic will
be only a prophet can tell.

Besides the reserves mentioned in the foregoing, the
co* has: —
Premiums on shares .. .. . . £282,225
Meter replacement fund .?.■ .. 18,384
Total £300,609
Reserves are the value of a co.’s assets in excess of
capital and liabilities. But in this case one may
be permitted to disbelieve that the co. has only
£398,464 in excess of its capital ( £849,996) and
liabilities ( £509,712, including suspense accounts).
The truth, no doubt, is that the co. has been putting
very large sums into works, nominally as a set-off
against depreciation. Ten years ago the land,
buildings, machinery, pipes, etc., charged to capital
stood at £1,129,385. On June 30 of this year they
stood at £1,294,141. So, of - all the property
bought in the past 10 years, all the new machinery
put in, and all the mains laid, etc., only £164,756
worth has been bought and paid for out of capital
and visible profit; the balance has been bought and
paid for out of revenue which hasn’t been regarded
or treated as profit. And that is in addition, of
course, to whatever has been paid for renewals. This
paper doesn’t know how much the co. has spent on
new property, plant, mains, etc. But the amount
must have been very considerable, judging by the
fact that in the first half of 1901 the total revenue
from sales of gas, etc., was £171,402, whereas for
the past half-year it was £289,521. No doubt
some of the old plant, mains, etc., are bringing in
more revenue than they did 10 years ago, in spite of
the establishment and rapid extension of the City
Council’s electric-light business; but, given that in,
it isn’t too much to say that at least £450,000 must
have been spent in new plant and mains, etc., in the 10
years; and of that, as shown above, only £164,756
has been charged to capital account, leaving at least
£285,244 to be paid for out of revenue. Admit-
tedlv, those figures, so far as they relate to the
profit which has been put into works, are only a
guess; but this co. has been making as much pro-
gress as the Melbourne co. surely—Sydney, anyway,
has been growing much more rapidly than Melbourne
and the Melbourne co. has put £193,000 of re-
venue into works in the past five years. The Svdnev
co. meantime has been paying 15 p.c. divs. How
much more it could have paid if it had ventured to
distribute all its true profits, this writer doesn’t
know, and the shareholders are never likely to be
told either. It is enough for them to know that the
co. pays them 15 p.c., and has been doing so right
away back into the dark ages; and, though a poor
little reduction in the price of gas was made lately,
the co. is now, no doubt, better able to pay 15 p.c.
than ever it was, by reason of its vast accumulation
of secret reserves invested in revenue-producing
works. From the investor’s point of view, only two
things are worth considering: (1) What are the
prospects of the councils, or a Greater Sydney, tak-
ing over the whole business of supplying light and
power? (2) What are the prospects of electrical
light and power competing more keenly than at pre-
sent with gas? These are things to which, however,
you won’t get an answer from the published 'accounts,
even rummaged them for a month.

There is not much to be got out of this co.’s published
accounts. There is, in fact, not much to be got out
of the published accounts of almost any Gas Co. in
N. S. Wales. The law doesn’t compel them to publish
anything of much value, even to their shareholders;
and, with the municipalities hovering around, and
showing a disposition to swoop into the light-supply
business, the prosperous cos. naturally don’t make a
song about their prosperity, or give much definite
information that the public can lay hold of. In this
case, it will be seen, the 8 p.c. div. absorbs £7196/
while no less than £6340 goes to reserves. But .
whether any of that £6340 is a set-off against wear
and tear and decay, this paper doesn’t know. The';
chances are that depreciation is provided for other-;
wise. Anyway, the Profit and Loss account says:*
“Profit on sales of gas, etc., after allowing for de- 1
preciation and reserves, £7036,” and the additions to.
the visible reserve funds were £6500. From this itV
may be assumed that the co. is doing remarkably
well, though how well can only be guessed at in a
vague way. Its progress has certainly been most j
remarkable. A few comparisons:—
1901. 1911.
Paid capital ..‘ . . £106,250 £183,140
Liabilities . . . . 5,391 24,866
Fixed assets . . . . 106,816 241,615
Other assets .. .. 15,068 23,897
As previously remarked, it is not much use looking at
the profits, because one never knows how much has
been written off against depreciation; in other
words, put into plant extensions. So a calculation
based on the earnings of the co., in proportion to
book value of assets, is of practically no value; if it
were we might get some idea as to the extensions paid
for out of revenue. The liabilities look considerable,
but 30,000 new £1 shares were lately issued at par,
and 15s. per share is yet to be got in, and that will
wipe out most of the debt. The dividends have been
kept moderate —as Gas Cos.’ divs. go—and have
been pushed up very slowly, and there is no doubt .
whatever that thereby the co. has been putting itself i
into an extremely strong position.

This co. went through a very bad time, and had to
write down its £1 shares to 4s. 6d. and its 15s.

writing down to be done when a few years ago
some assets were realised. Recently, however, much

S. Australia has had so many good seasons that it is
beginning to assume that that is the only sort of
season made. It is possible, no doubt, that, owing
to improved farming methods, droughts will never be
quite so disastrous as they were in the past; but
such high prices are being paid for land that what
was a good harvest in the past will in some cases be
a poor one in the future. And, apart from harvests,
there is the matter of prices, over which the State
has practically no control whatever. This co. has
been in a rich patch of clover during the prosperous
years; and; besides putting its div. up from 7 to 11
p.c., it has added much strength to its financial posi-
tion. Out of the past five years’ profits it has spent
£13,212 on fixed assets (besides providing for de-
preciation), and has added £4806 to its surplus of
liquid assets. What depreciation has been provided
for it is, however, impossible to say, though there is
every reason to believe that the provision made is
liberal. The present surplus of liquid assets is
£8924. The capital is £76,570, in £5 shares fully
paid.

The Bigelow Patent Rock Breaker commends
itself to mine managers who welcome an effective
machine which is cheaper to maintain and repair
than Gyratory and other crushers. This machine is
of the well-known Blake type, but has some ad-
ditional improvements which have justified in prac-
tice all the claims advanced by the patentee. 'The
work of crushing is done on the down-stroke of the
pitman instead of the up-stroke; there is no strain
on the pitman cap ; two large side rods 1 resist the
thrust of the toggles, and enable the jaws tc be
regulated within a large range; the back toggle is
so made as to resist all legitimate strains of crush-
ing, but will shear under exceptional conditions and
save the machine. The shaft and eccentric bearings
are self-lubricating; the jaws are convex and con-
cave, and with the cheek plates are made of steel
and are reversible. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company, of South Melbourne, have installed a great
number of these machines, and hold the sole manu-
facturing rights for the' Commonwealth.* [3

The only announcement of any import-
ance made at the meeting of the Commonwealth Oil
Corporation in London, was that the directors had
been able to “arrange” for the £50,000 or £60,000
more of working capital needed. Asked who had
supplied it, the chairman said: “Those good friends
who had stood by them before. The directors had
no definite arrangement with those who had made
the advances as to how they were to be paid, but,
of course, the money advanced became a debt of
the co.” The co. already owed £550,000 on de-
bentures and debenture Stock.
An assay of 10.36 p.c. from the Queen
Bee (N.S.W.) winze, at 20ft. below the 570 ft. level,
caused some speculation on the off-chance that pay-
able ore will be struck in the 670 ft. level when the
south drive gets under the winze. The shallow levels
above 570 ft. don’t appear to give much encourage-
ment, the country being for the most part unsettled
and the makes of ore erratic. Still, backers go on
hoping that better prospects will be met with at
670 ft., although the crosscut is at present proving
rather low-grade stuff.
•* * * *
Kyloe (N.S.W.) has made another start
with its flotation plant; but at present prices the
prospects so far as profit-making is concerned are
not particularly bright. The last milling run was
361 tons, averaging 5.1 p.c., the recovery being a
shade under 74 p.c. of metal contents. Taking
copper at £5O per ton, the crudes averaged 51s.
per ton, and the extraction in concentrates was 375.
6d. per ton of ore. Kyloe, of course, only recovers
part of this value to pay mining and concentrating
costs —the balance goes in' freight and smelting
charges—and the profit (if any) must be very small.
Still, the shipments of concentrates and prill ore
have enabled Kyloe to put up a game struggle against
the metal market, and everyone will wish the show
good luck when it opens its level at 600 ft.
For the June quarter Vegetable Creek,
Emmaville (N.S.W.), produced 17 tons 19cwt. of
tin oxide. This sold for £2241, or at the rate of
over £124 per ton, which must be near the record
price for a period of 12 weeks. A square fathom
(36 sq. ft.) has given on the average £2l 10s.
worth of black tin. The proved area at the eastern
end, or up the lead, is approximately 520 ft. by 60ft.,
giving 31,200 sq. ft. of bottom. On this measure-
ment there are nearly 1000 sq. fathoms of washdirt
available, containing a gross value of approximately
.£20,000, or probably a trifle more. But though
£2241 has been produced in 12 weeks from develop-
ment drives, etc., expensive work has been in progress ;
the outlay on shaft sinking, driving and equipment
runs to nearly £4OOO for three months. The mine
has now a permanent main shaft 138 ft. deep, fitted
with steam winding-gear; and, generally speaking,
the mine will be worked under much better con-
ditions than before. Its handicap is that it still has
to pay a big slice of its profits to the freeholders,
not as tribute, but towards the purchase.
* * * *
The Broken Hill South Blocks Co. has
gone. The “amalgamation” has been completed, and
the mine reports come out now with the name of the
Zinc Corporation on top of them.
» * * *
Mararoa’s days are apparently numbered.
The unfavorable change of country, which sealed the
doom of almost every other mine in the Norseman
district, has practically cut out the reef at the No. 7
level—equal to about 450 ft. vertical —and even the
mine authorities (who next to the, directors are
usually the last to give shareholders the tip) seem to
despair of getting any payable ore in deeper ground. It
is intended, however, to sink on the off-chance of find-
ing something new, and a few bore-holes are to be
drilled east and west of the present workings at No.
7 level, with the same object. Meantime there is
said to be enough gold in sight to pay somewhere
near the present market price in dividends. But as
The Bulletin has already said, dividends are only
dividends when they are in shareholders’ pockets.
* * * *
The miracle of raising Westralia Mount
Morgans from the dead is about to be attempted by
its former boss (A. E. Morgans) and a local bank.
At one time this “show” was among the most pro-
lific gold producers in the West, but after yielding
bullion valued at over a million and a quarter ster-
ling, and paying £226,000 in dividends, some sort
of geological cataclysm occurred and put its light
out. Morgans and the bank are hopeful of turning
the present inky darkness into something approaching
brilliancy by means of deeper development, and pre-
parations are now being made to see if this can be
done.
* * * *
Great Fingall’s operations for 1910 pro-
duced a surplus of £33,417 ; and after deducting
depreciation ( £6459) and dividend taxes ( £5316),
a balance of £21,642 remained, which, with £2782
brought forward from 1909, made a total of £24,424.
Two dividends, absorbing £22,290, were paid, leav-
ing £2134 to be carried forward. The ore treated
during the year amounted to 140,982 tons, and
working expenses (including development) averaged
a shade over 21s. per ton. Plant expenditure
totalled £2733, the bulk of this representing the
purchase price of an automatic sampling mill, which
was got to enable the co. to buy ore from other
shows and carry on a custom business as a side-line.
Various job lots of obsolete machinery were disposed
of, reducing the net expenditure on the year’s con-
struction and equipment to £l5OO. No even approxi-
mate estimate of ore reserves is given by the manage-
ment, the excuse being that it is impossible at pre-
sent to estimate the quantity of broken ore in the
stopes or in the pillars or in the lower levels. Some-
thing, however, is said about the payable ore dis-
closed in the lower levels, which the management is
now “hastening to block out,” being of considerable
extent and value. The outstanding feature
of the year’s development is stated to be
the discovery of some really good ore in the vicinity
of No. 17 level, after ‘7ooft. of sinking had been
done on the reef, nearly all of it in low-grade stuff.
When the accounts were made up on December 31,
the book-value of the co.’s principal assets was as
follows: Property, £10,331; machinery, plant,
buildings, etc., £23,977; stores, £16,421; gqld in
transit, £3147; West African shares, £20,067;
debtors, £3244 ; cash in hand, £79,168. On the
game date there was £7169 due to sundry creditors
and a contingent liability of £18,481 for uncalled
capital on the West African interests.
* * * *
“Tumbled In”: How is it that the eastern
and western dailies still allow themselves to be
“kidded” by the Bullfinch Co. ? Tons of ink and
stacks of paper have already been used in leading
the public astray concerning the value and possi-
bilities of this auriferous deceiver, and now it seems
as if a lot more is going to be used for the same
very bad purpose. The other day 7ft. of stone,
assaying an ounce, was cut in crumbling oxidised
country at 210 ft., and the following are a few of
the loaded remarks that have got into print about
the discovery: “Mr. Morgan, who is generally re-
garded as being extremely conservative, has reported
the stone to be worth an ounce; but the stutt now
coming from the face is worth more than that. .. .
“It is beautiful free milling quartz, shows gold
freely, and is very similar in appearance to Great
Fingall stone.” . . . “The development is spoken ot
in the highest terms, and, whereas the official report
gives the width as 7ft., a good judge is inclined to
think that good values will be obtained over a thick-
ness of 10ft.” . . . “The fact that the reef was tapped
nearly 200 ft. west of where the high values were
got in the prospecting workings, proves there is a
great length of payable stone awaiting development
at 210 ft.” No wonder the cry of Enough!
Enough! We’ve heard enough! ”is beginning to
elicit a responsive echo from the taken-in and taken-
down public. No wonder questions are being asked
in respect of the whereabouts of the “over half a
million worth of oxidised ore in sight.” And no
wonder most people’s belief in the permanency of
the mine is akin to their faith in the impregnability
of a house of cards.
* * * *
Hans Irvine’s western district crowd is
poking after another fortune, this time in the Royal
Oaks, at Hancock's, near Black Range (W.A.). T.
A. Couper is still the mascotte; but, as luck would
have it, drift-ground cut at 150 ft. played the mis-
chief with the shaft. Seeing that the Faugh-a-
Ballah, next door, hit a 2-}o7„ reef at 134 ft. deep,
and only 15ft. from the Royal Oaks, Couper advises
the sinking of a new shaft to the north. As usual,
he gets what he wants, especially as the vendors are
sympathetic in respect to the time limit.
Westralia’s coaltown M.L.A., Wilson,
himself a “coalie,” has just opened up, with little
outside backing, a new (the sixth) colliery in Collie
district. With breath-taking originality he has
christened it the Newcastle.
* * * *
Great things are prophesied locally about
the low-grade deposit at Armstrong’s (Vic.), about
half-way between Stawell and Ararat, which has just
started a crushing plant. A new milling appliance is
expected to put the ordinary or garden variety of
stamp battery on the scrap-heap. The mill, the in-
vention of an old Stawell resident named Earle, is a
kind of Chilian mill, on the same lines as the Lane
mill which is well spoken of in America. It differs
from the old-time Chilian, once familiar on Austra-
lian fields, by being much larger, with several wheels
instead of two, and in having provision for loading
the wheels so as to obtain whatever crushing weight
is found most suitable for the ore. Locally, this mill
is expected to completely eclipse stamp batteries in
regard to power required, wear and tear, and output;
but, as the newcomer hasn’t had time to build up a
reputation for itself, stamp-mill supporters have sus-
pended judgment. The trial run, it is alleged, proved
that the machine, which is about 10 ft. diameter,
can reduce 2in. rock to fine sand at the rate of two
tons per hour. *lf this is correct, and other things are as
claimed, the proprietary of the Armstrong’s show
should be on a good wicket —provided, of course, the
stone contains enough gold to make it worth crushing
with anything. The directors anticipate that total
costs of mining, milling, etc., will not exceed 45.;
and it’s pretty poor stuff that won’t giVe that. They
talk locally about Bs.
* * # *
Central Red, White and Blue is the most i
puffed-up show in Victoria just now, but people out- !
side Bendigo don’t forget the Catherine. .Reef fire- ;
works of four years back. Melbourne dailies outvie
Bendigo in big heads and in repeating the old story
that things look well at the north end, and that, with
the 400 ft. level opened up, the shoot will be picked
up on its pitch; also, that when a connection is
made between rise and winze, stopes will be taken
along. All this fills up space, but it doesn’t explain
away the hang-up over the div., nor does it . prove
that when the shoot dips into the 400 ft. level it will
be as rich as at 318 ft. This is not remarked sourly,
but as a gentle reminder that with any Bendigo
scrip at 955. thoroughly good goods are expected;
and there is bound to be a sharp reaction if any
weak spot turns up. Too much beating of the tom-
tom in such circumstances doesn’t frighten the bears
away; it rather attracts them.
*** . *
Richard Ripper, who commands order at
the Christensen’s Ajax meetings, tells about this
Daylesford mine that they’ve driven east and driven
west at 490 ft., and, bar a flat leader that made into
the “vertical,” have found only water and a bill for
repairs. Where the leader hits the vertical the latter
reef bulges out to Bft., but does not prospect enough
to provide petty cash. There is always a hope in
such Daylesford shows, so rising is the programme.
The idea is to go out east and get into the run of
flat-make country, worked so profitably by the Nug-
gety and North Nuggety.
Long Tunnel Extended, Walhalla, has at
last come to the right way of thinking, but a few
white hairs arrived on Peter Finlayson’s head before
he would come up to the mark. Sam Barnes says
the old mine’s done at depth; so away goes the
Jacksonian legend that Cohen’s reef bent to the east.
But Barnes thinks there’s a chance to pick up a new
and independent lode that way. That’s what the
Long Tunnel and the Extended ought to have sought
long since. Then Cohen’s reef is to be driven on
north in search of a new shoot. Again good; but
why go so deep? If a hint can be given, it is to
find out where the lodes kiss one another. That’s
where the best gold was always got; and if needle
pyrites were seen to exist in the dyke, old Long
Tunnel traditions would be revived. Therefore, why
not spend a little cash at the surface as well as belt-
ing away at 1400 ft. and 1900 ft.? Calls are needed,
but with a decent fellow like Barnes to spend them,
there are worse specs, than trying to pick up the
Extended luck once again. #
Victorian Manganese Mines Iron and
Steel Co. is the latest suckling that intends to try
to fatten upon the hard diet of the iron industry.
Manganese mining has been tried with more or less
success in N.S.W., but only the haziest idea existed
that there was a deposit in Southern Gippsland to
exploit, until the news of the London float came to
be talked about. One J. Colquhoun Thomson, 8.E.,
is the St. John who is presenting the scheme in Lon-
don, and his conceptions are not unworthy of a
double-barrelled name. He is arranging a co. with
£750,000 nominal capital, and an issue of deben-
tures for £llO,OOO, the latter at 90, less 10 p.c.
commission. Shareholders in the Victorian “little-
go” of 120,000 shares are to get 170,000 paid-up
shares out of the London “big-go,” if the latter
comes off. Working capital is to be £350,000.
On that basis it looks as if J. Bull is to make 20
p.c. out of the debenture issue, and to bag 230,000
paids in the big float for finding £440,000. The
Bulletin won’t object to Bull doing well out of the
business. His money is to go to build up the Spiegel
steel industry, and as a bit of that stuff s wanted
for the skins of our ironclads, we don’t care much
who starts making it so long as it is made.
* * * *
Gippsland Copper and Platinum Co.
(Vic.) will issue a swag of new shares at ss. each.
The idea is to raise £10,000; but it is doubtful
whether that amount will pay for the extensive
scheme suggested. At the start the concern was
handicapped by excessively high costs for fluxes.
Then the consumption of coke was high, so one
way and another smelting of crude ore, with a
quantity of furnace slag containing fairly good
metal values, proved expensive. It is now admitted
that two furnaces are required, the second being
necessary to bring the matte up to a high enough
grade for shipment. -For the run, 970 tons of crude
ore were treated, together with a large quantity of
slag, and the product was 76 tons of matte, con-
taining from £3OOO to £6OOO worth of metals.
But matte containing copper, platinum, gold and
silver requires costly treatment, and although the
directors expect to get some more money when the
accounts are adjusted, they don’t seem pleased with
what they have heard so far.
*■* * *
Mount Lyell has three furnaces running
again, which is a quiet way of intimating that next
month’s output of metal will be up to the average.
The Chester mine is the best outside show
owned by Mount Lyell, and there is a growing idea
that its immense quantity of mixed schist rock and
pyritic ore will pay to concentrate. To put the
matter to the test, 2000 tons were sent to the Farrell
mill, and the result is said to be all right. The
Tasmanian West Coast, with its heavy rainfall, offers
some good opportunities for copper concentration.
But such propositions are often deceptive. Thus, 3
p.c. ore, with copper at £5O per ton, is worth only
305., and a 70 p.c. recovery at the mill gives 215.,
which isn’t much to pay costs of mining, milling
and subsequent smelting, unless the business is run
on big lines.
* * * *
At Charters Towers recent Day Dawn
crushings have been unusually good. Mills’ United
obtained £3661 from 1000 tons, New Brilliant Free-
hold £2333 from 817 tons, and East Mexican £3371
from 686 tons. A total of £9365 from these three
mines is a good yield for a fortnight. The East
Mexican has been extending No. 4 level in unsettled
country, so if - conditions improve to the east there
may be a make of good stone. The Mexican’s hang-
ing-wall reef is to be prospected for by putting a
rise up near the western boundary; not far on the
other side this reef is giving Mills’ United a good
innings.
The starting of the Oaks Rush battery in
N. Queensland was • delayed by a breakdown of the
electrical plant. There is rather lively talk of
amalgamating the Oaks Rush with the Acorn. The
crosscut goes right through from the O.R. shaft, the
auriferous formation being very wide, and the stone
belonging to the Acorn is being hauled up the Oaks
Rush shaft.
*** , *
North Queensland tin mines, favored by
high prices, are shaping better. Even the Gilmore
is coming along again, and may be congratulated
on having kept a good reserve in the till. The 74
tons recently sent to the mill—after a delay through
bad roads —should return a fine cheque. No. 2
stope in the Dolly Grey isn’t much good now, as the
ore is only Bin. wide; but the other section —the
Midas —looks better, and the directors intend to
open up this show again. The show is, however,
over-weighted with shares —a ridiculous 90,000 of
them. #
Yulcan (N.Q.) had £7OOO to the good at
the end of June, which encouraged the directors to
declare a penny dividend ( £1100). There is no
visible reason why the Vulcan should not pay Id.
per month for a while. The development at the
600 ft. level is good, but it is quite unknown how
fait the good things extend downwards, and a cross-
cut. at 720 ft., although in tin-bearing formation,
Hasn’t struck the richer ore-body yet. For June the
return from 880 tons was £4920, which includes a
parcel of seconds.
* * * *
In view of the railway extension, the
Hampden Co. has secured three likely-looking shows
a few miles from the Duchess. The highest price
(£5000) was paid for the Trekelano. The shaft
sunk in the ore-body disclosed what seems to be a
bulky and fairly high-grade proposition. As usual
in such cases, the assays are high, but very little is
known, about the show at present. The Trekelano is
only 10 miles south of the Duchess. To the north
two other shows have been acquired, the Ardath and
the Maseotte. The latter has a shaft down 103 ft.,
and has been opened north and south with encour-
aging results, but neither can yet be regarded as
more than promising prospecting ventures.
The Mungana (Q.) thinks it has secured
a better agreement with Chillagoe. That is good
news, for it has seemed absurd for the co. to send
away its high-grade ore, containing £5 worth of
cooper and silver, at such a small margin of profit.
For the 12 months, to March 31, the 22.489 tons
shipped contained 1530 tons copper and 350,0000 z.
silver, the gross value being over £llO,OOO, but the
net profit was only £14,700. The financial position
is now admitted to be £4OOO worse than it was on
March 31, and the co. has a lot of expense in front
of it. *
A new coal co. with a mere handful of
capital has been formed in Rockhampton, and is to
be known as the Mammoth Admiralty Co. The idea
is to acquire from the Bluff Co. three prospecting
areas situated on the Mackenzie River It was
from the mine there that a test lot of 100 tons was
sent to the Admiralty, which promptly asked for 200
tons more.
*****
The co. that owns Mount Whitty, Logan
(Q ), had an annual meeting the other day. The
report gives the assays as varying from 3dwt. to
2oz. 7dwt. per ton, though an 8-ton bulk sample
sent to Gympie returned only 4sdwt. per ton. ihe
directors, of course, do not consider this a fair re-
sult, as “a large quantity of gold had disappeared
in the concentrates.” Alas, it always does disappear
somewhere in such cases.
* * * *
Christchurch (M.L.) has been pricking
its ears at news from the Galena reefs,
peka, in the Nelson district. An area of 460 acres
is held by the Blue Creek Co.—a small development
concern —whose chairman lately told the shareholders
that “if they were to put on 500 head of stamps
they could not crush all the stone on Blue Creek in
their time.” This is tall talk, which will of course
go for nothing till the tape has been put over the
proposition bv some mining man with a name to lose,
meantime, however, though nothing can be said about
quantity, there is no doubt about the exceeding rich-
ness of some of the silver samples brought in to
Christchurch.
* * *
Stanthorpe (QA is talking about radium.
A 2oz. specimen of pitch blende was sent from there
to England, and back comes the alleged news that
there is a heap of money in it. But the information
seems erratic, and at present only Stanthorpe is sure
that it is all right. The old tin village, with a Rip
Van Winkle sleep on it, could certainly do with a
lift. *
“Windlass”: Through a local paper the
Etheridge Railway and Mines Co. (Q.) has
been expressing its profound disgust at ( t e
ingratitude of the prospectors. It says. we
S put the Big Reef battery into thorough
repair making it. an up-to-date plant, solely
in the interest of the small mining man and
he has not the good sense to respond and keep the
battery going.” If the small man raises under 20
tons the co. charges him 15s. per ton for crushing;
if he has over 20 tons the job is done at 12s. 6d.
per ton onlv. Then, recognising .that the larger per-
centage of gold is too fine for the battery to save,
the co installed a cyanide plant. . Payment is made
for these sands on a tonnage basis, the ungratefu
small man is allowed for two-thirds of the quantity
treated bv the -battery. The stuff is assayed, and
one-third of the gold contents deducted ; then, after
charging the cost of treatment, the whole of the
balanced if there is any) is handed to the man who
mined the stone. And still the wretched fellows are
not satisfied! The more you do these laboring fel-
lows, the louder they cry, it seems..
One of the finest gems ever found at Sap-
phiretown (Q.) was unearthed the other day in the
Reward claim, which is noted for the large stones
it has disgorged. The new beauty is an orange-green
sapphire, weighing over an ounce,, and is valued at
£lOO in the rough. When cut and polished, it will
be a valuable present for either Dill Moran or Pat-
rick F. Macky.

A
WOMEN’S
LETTER
Sydney, July 31, 1911.
My Dear Arini, —
Society is reported to be cheering up re
the Denman Excellencies, who have an ex-
hilarating reputation in the matter of free
food and drink.
Lady Poore has been mentioned as one of
the sponsors or spiiltual lady helps of the
infant Margaret Sydney, Governor Chelms-
ford’s new daughter. The Admii-al, by the
way, has been given command at the Nore.
A line from Suva drops in. The writer
coldly removes the halo from the native
Fijian’s woolly mop, and all on account of
a silvei’-mounted umbrella. My correspond-
ent affii-ms that when the Pah’f’l was in
Fijian waters, the Admirable attended a
local church. The congregation was all
native, and pursued the promise of a Better
World according to the Wesleyan faith.
Much touched by its devout attitude to
psalms and hymns, the Admirable asked
permission to address the prayerful native
in a short homily. When his Naval Ex. re-
turned to his pew, his silver-mounted um-
brella had disappeared. Presumably it had
been whisked away by a colored convert
who hated getting his mop of woolly thatch
wet more than he feared the Day of Judg-
ment.
The early Victorian parasol is threatening
to break into the coming season’s fashions.
The tent-like cii-cumference of last summer’s
hats threw the sunshade into disrepute. It
hung up by the handle in shop interiors, a
dowdy derelict for which the motor lady had
no further use. This spi-ing will see a timid
revival of parasols rather larger than well-
gi-own mushrooms, with a perky little frill
round their edges. The old-fashioned
handles that doubled up on hinges—some-
times when least expected—are probably
coming back with October roses.
Theatre caps are large enough to shut out
winter draughts from feminine scalps.
French flowers, twice as natural as life,
bloom finely on the more expensive lace
flaps that pull down towards the ears. A
rose perched on each ear is the effect of this
floral exuberance. A lighter lace frivol,
poised on top of the head, is another vagary
of theatre headgear; Miss Potts Point is
wearing just such a cap as Lavinia Fenton
pinned on her hair, when Hogarth painted
her portrait as “Polly Peachum” in the
“Beggar’s Opera.”
Artist Longstaff has strapped up his color-
box, folded down his blue painting kit, and
laid his silk hat to rest in tissue paper. It’s
said to have been the handsomest stove-pipe
that jaunted into the Union Club this
winter; and the Athenaeum is suspected of
serving up a sonnet to its curved brim.
Beau G. Bowen Siixxpson, whose shining
black tile hitherto reigned supreme in Club-
land, is too buoyant in tempex-ament to pine
in secret over the success of a departed
rival. Sydney’s loss will be London’s gain.
Australian artists abroad are getting
themselves into Cold Land papers so often
that they will soon have to be included
among our great natural resources; for, be-
sides producing pictures, they are helping
to break down Britain’s old idea that this
continent consists of a drought, a mining
camp, some wool sales and bushrangers, and
an army of globe-ti-otting politicians. Vic-
torian Hilda Rix, who has been exhibiting
her work in the Paris Salon and other foreign
places, gets a sugar-plum notice for her pic-
ture, “The Return from the Hunt.” It is
said to be “typical of Miss Rix’s strength of
draughtsmanship and freshness of colbr.”
The Royal Society’s Rooms, dedicated to
the gnawing of historic bones, plunged into
fried fish last week. The occasion was a
demonstration in paper-bag cookei*y by Mrs.
Glennen, of the Melbourne Domestic Train-
ing College. Learned men who had served
their country and cultivated mutton-chop
whiskers in spare moments, peered out of
picture frames at the desecration of their
sacred forum. The paper bag, by the way,
after cremation in the stove, emerges in
crisp brown flakes. Mrs. Glennen’s “wash-
up” was a bucketful of paper shavings. It
was received with acclamation by a room-
ful of women who absorbed the “no sauce-
pans to scrape” item with gleeful chuckles.
An orange souffle, popped into a paper
bag in its glass dish, was a hair-raising ex-
periment ; but it came out a perfect picture,
with no glass fragments to give it a foreign
flavor. Tomatoes —a recklessly extravagant
twopence each —blushed pale red, and hugged
their juice in their skins. A mere fowl
stuck its trussed legs into its paper coffin
and turned a tender brown. Mrs. Greig
Smith and Miss Russell helped to push this
demonstration into its place.
An Anti-Salt League is now trying to
kick the domestic salt cellar off the table.
The mere housewife, side-stepping the boiled
egg in the paper "bag, had barely recovered
from her surprise that the water doesn’t
filter through the bag, when she was face to
face with this other px-oblem. Will she take
the risk of being driven to drink by a dab of
salt in her cooked yolk, or will she take the
egg neat, and lose even her accustomed thirst
for morning tea?
Muswellbrook, after joyfully snatching the
Dudley Polo Cup for 12 inonths, has had to
put on the garb of woe. A tragic motor
accident which cost Mrs. Barrington her
life, and thus robbed the “Liberals” of a lead-
ing light, besides plunging two other fami-
lies into mourning, was preceded by a death
toll that bei - eaved Df. and Mrs. Mills
(Strathfield). Their bright little daughter
Jean, a charming child of 14, was spending
her holidays on the Whites’ station in Mus-
wellbrook parts, when a bolting pony threw
her against a tree with immediately fatal
results.
In “The Lyons Mail” H. B. Irving swal-
lows nightly about three quarts of liquid
that might be rum, bui'gundy or tinted Pro-
spect Reservoir. As the good Lesux-ques he
has a preliminary meal and liquid nourish-
ment. As the bad Dubose he has to regxx-
larly empty bottles into the good Lesui’ques’s
food chest. The terrific strain of two gx;eat
impersonations is added to the physical
achievement of sopping up fluid like a
thirsty sponge. When Irving, as the worthy
Lesurques, has had his head saved from the
sawdust, and pants aci'oss the footlights
(final curtain) in the bosom of his family,
he looks as wan as if he'd escaped from the
under-side of a steam-roller.
Miss Irene Browne, the pretty Julie in
“The Lyons Mail,” insinuates her way into
the friendly feelings of the audience with a
graceful trick of girlish mannei’s, and one of
the loveliest wigs—pale ale colored, with an
alluring curl trickling down her neck —that
has l'ecently touched the finer emotions of
the front stalls. She wears, besides, a soft
brocade gown with a foam of blue-green
scarf, in the 1797 period, hitched across her
shoulder-blades, and firmly clipped to its
moorings near a white patch of bare neck.
This froth of drapery, which clung to the
female form through Jane Austen's novels,
must have taken the heroine’s attention off
her troubles, for it needs a special training
to keep it in its proper place. Irene
managed her chiffon with considerable pre-
sence of mind, and sobbed on Irving’s coat
lapels without getting the tails of her scarf
tangled up with her tears.
MISS CARRIE LANCELEY, a Sydney
soprano, back after doing things in London.
Constance Craven, better, known in Syd-
ney as Miss Connie Simpson, recounts her
fortunes on the other side, on inflammable red
paper, with a “C” like a half-moon hung in
one corner. Constance says she is not quar-
relling with her luck, which wears a silver
lining on. treasury day. She writes from the
Lyric Theatre: “ I’ve been engaged by Mr.
Whitney to be principal understudy to Con-
stance Drever in ‘The Chocolate Soldier,’
and I am also to play the part of Nadina at
matinCes. Constance Drever, by the way, is
magnificent as Nadina.” With only one
year’s stage experience behind her, Con-
stance (a former pupil of Slapoffski) seems
to have forced her Greek cameo profile into
a position where it’s likely to be noticed. She
throws a posy at Alice Crawford’s work in
Shakspearian parts at “the Coi-onet,” and
approaches Alice Hollander’s successes in
song on bended nib. ‘ ’
Tliea Proctor’s decorative designs, notably
for fans, make bright patches of color on
the walls of the, Women-Painters’ Club
room. Mr. Hardy-Wilson lends one of
Thea’s earlier fan decorations; it is in softer
tones than her later work, which shows
more detail. “Fashions —1910,” with the
blue Directoire gown in the foreground and
fountains splashing in the perspective, is
ear-marked “sold.” It has the same French
atmosphere as the Hardy-Wilson fan, or the
handsome piece of color lent by Dr. Black-
burn. Cold-Land critics hail Australian
Thea as the successor of Conder, with the
same sense of color and better drawing.
The ballet dancer’s muscle, in color and in
pencil sketch, is evidently a favorite leg-
study of this artist, who gives to “a dancer
resting” the calves of Julius Knight and
the consistently large feet which, outside
fiction, are the stock-in-trade of ladies who
earn their living on their toes.
Cyril Monk believes he is stage-managing
an idea new to Sydney for the Austral String-
Quartet concerts. The instrumental items
may be delivered by a Celestial choir so far
as the audience can swear to the contrary.
Lights will be turned down, and the per-
formers, disguised in thin air. will be invis-
ible to the naked eye. Mr. Monk says the
human mind can only take in one impression
at a time. My Suva correspondent hints
that it can escape with a wedge of sermon
and a good-looking umbrella.
Miss Carrie Lanceley, of this city, who
took her soprano voice to Fogland four years
ago had to undergo 197 welcome-home hand-
shakes at the Australia one afternoon last
week. She admits she came back to see her
people and her country, get a little Austra-
lian atmosphere into her wind-instrument,
and sniff gum leaves as part of a rest cure.
Carrie has a bright and virile temperament,
which probably accounts for her numerous
engagements on the other side. She says:
“Beautiful women and girls with fine voices
are often practically fighting the lean and
hungry wolf of despair in England, simply
because they never get a hearing. They
can’t afford to pay agents huge fees —I’ve
never paid a guinea, by the way —and they
haven’t had my. luck. I call it sheer luck,
with the help of Australian notices copied
into the London papers.”
Carrie, in search of light for the phrasing
and reading of oratorio —her special musical
microbe —went to Santley, who eventually
sent her on to be coached by Alfred Har-
riss, who wears a second “s” to liis name, but
is unrelated to Charles of the Sheffield
voices. She is merely poising her wing in
Australia, and hurries back to London and
her concert engagements in September.
Lieut. Richard Hirst, R.N., of the gun-
boat Encounter, is engaged to Miss Kite,
who hails from New England, in this State.
This joyous happening was celebrated with
clinking glasses by Mr. Hirst’s brother-offi-
cers in the Encounter wardroom the other
day.
Miss Mattie Roth, eldest daughter of Dr.
and Mrs. Roth, of this town, has given her
promise to Lieut. Van Ilieustreik, of the
Dutch army. He’s stationed in Java, and
comes to Australia to buy horses. Inciden-
tally, he has acquired a Nice Girl.
Miss Ivussmann, who snared the Guild
Hall gold medal for her fine fiddling, was
formei-ly a pupil of Sydney’s delightful old
Kretschmann, who needs two persons to hold
him down, and a third to sit on his head,
when one of his ex-infant phenomena gets
talked about in London.
Miss Violet Alcock, who lent a hand in
mixed farming on Miss Brace’s‘alluvial patch
near Glen Innes, is returning to the Cold
Land this week at the call of urgent private
business. Miss Alcock was formerly sub-
editor of the Girls’ Realm Magazine, and
secretary of the London Lyceum Club, and
cultivated a few hundred other devices for
keeping out of mischief from the mature age
of 18. Jangling nerves sent her to Australia
for a rest-cure. Here, Violet has learned to
chop wood —nice green wood, with a soft
sinew—chase fowls out of flower beds, in-
quire into the identity of nest eggs, paper
homestead walls, hate rabbits, and love billy
tea. She was in the act of giving her con-
sent to the land tax when the overseas cable
dragged her away.
Mrs. Robert Brough, who revived old
memories as “The Second Mrs. Tanqueray”
in Melbourne the other day, got a surprise
present after the performance from the.Ham-
ilton-Plimmer-Denniston management. It
was a gold watch-bangle of much solid worth,
and the lady murmured her thanks through a
lump in her throat.
Mrs. Dalton, who died recently after com-
pleting a health-hunting trip to Japan, was
widow of Tom Dalton, M.L.C., of the big
commercial firm of Dalton Brothers, of Syd-
ney and Orange. She was a Miss Long, a
sister of Father Long, for many years parish
priest at Orange.
Rose Scott remarks, re the suggested me-
morial to Catherine Helen Spence, S’Aus-
tralia’s grand old woman: “Miss Spence be-
longed to every State, because she devoted
her life to the Service of Humanity—especi-
ally neglected children —in Australia.” Sena-
tor Walker, the National Council of Women,
and others have already parted with some
spare cash to push forward the N.S.W. end
of the Catherine Helen Spence memorial in
Adelaide.
The engagement of a Maoriland girl, Miss
Gladys Jardine, of Napier, to Captain
Anthony Rassam, now of the local staff
corps, Wellington, and formerly, of a Bri-
tish Border, Regiment, gives an excuse to
dig into the Rassam family’s archives. An-
thony’s parent, Hormund Rassam, put in
two dull years in chains—a mark of per-
sonal esteem which Theodore of Abyssinia
(1864) reserved for Special British Envoys
and other foreign cattle that strayed into
his compound.
At Premier, Asquith’s dinner to the
King and Queen, on the day after the
Coronation, Australia’s old friend, Miss
Lillah McCarthy, was the after-dinner
attraction. With her husband, Gran-
ville Barker, she put up Shaw’s “Man
of Destiny,” as well as Barrie’s new playlet,
showing how any wife having as much as
£l2 in ready money is liable to find her hus-
band an insufferable bore.
Australia's old friend, the dancing Earl of
Yarmouth, eldest son or the Marquis of Hert-
ford, has been declared bankrupt. His lia-
bilities are £12,000, mostly owing to money-
lenders, and his assets £3l2 —mostly ties and
socks, no doubt.
A recently-returned Australian song-bird
drops in a note of warning to others of the
same feather who may be meditating a
scamper to Britain. “’Ware agents!” is her
cry. “They’ll ask from one hundred guineas
upwards for giving the Australian voice a
hearing at the Queen’s Hall, a much smaller
building than our Sydney Town Hall.” The
Australian warbler in London who has a
sprinkle of gold in her cash-box should de-
posit it in a burglar-proof safe and lose the
key before she interviews the brand of agent
wno wants to sub-lease her voice for one
evening at a,minimum charge of £lOO.
The autograph fiend broke loose, the other
day, when the H. B. Irvings were fed and
stroked by members of the Women’s Club,
with Mr. Henry Gullett in charge of the
Shaksperian quotations.
