Concerning the recent ice spree at Kosci-
usko, Plain William, who was there, says
there was a frequent freeze in the Hostel
drawing-room. Percy* Hunter used to jump
with both feet on the ugly little serpent of
snobbery when it reared its baleful head.
“His absence this year,” snaps Plain Wil-
liam, “left a gap. Snob circles at Kosciusko
drew a chilly line between wholesale and re-
tail trade, and lTdeg. of frost smote various
alleged social gatherings.”
The men who write agitated letters to the
editors of daily papers, on the point of the
hatpin, its sharpness, and stabbing habits,
can now cheer up. A new female sun helmet
has been created. It fits well down on the
head, and has a wide lean-to verandah at the
back, which covers the neck. This helmet
needs no hatpin. It was designed for Cor-
onation crowds in the Thames village, but.
if it were invited, it would attend a mothers’
meeting in St. Andrew’s Chapter House, or
a pony race at Kensington.
On Saturday afternoon Lieut.-Governor
Cullen’s wife declared that the New Settle-
ment Home, in Newtown —an appendix to
the University Women’s Society—was well
and truly open. At the Girls’ Exhibition in
May, University women showed a sand-heap,
a few tables, and some small children, who
vigorously attacked the sand-heap. One
juvenile outlaw, filling his pockets with sand
for the joy of hurling it out again—a cycle
of ecstasy he pursued till his trousers split
at the sides—would have had a heated in-
terview with an angry parent if he’d broken
up his wardrobe in this fashion on the do-
mestic doorstep. The University Women’s
Settlement encourages the child to play un-
der its learned eye; so he plays with
cheerful abandon, and occasional damage to
his home-made breeches. The New Settle-
ment Home is an adult scheme with two
tails. It aims at “promoting a neighborly
feeling amongst women,” etc., and will also
incite ’Varsity students to sit up and ask
questions on social problems.
Bessie Major, writing to a Sydney friend,
says she believes “Kismet,” with the Asche-
Brayton team in charge, would have gone on
till the reappearance of Halley’s comet, if
the Asche return to Australia hadn’t been
booked for next Easter. Miss Major, who is
the Nurse in “Kismet,” chats briskly of
Coronating capers. She saw the second day’s
procession, and says it was a more gorgeous
pageant than the first, though the Royalties
had disposed of their mediaeval millinery,
and stepped off their Coronating perch into
the 20th century. Bessie took a room near
the theatre during the Coronating crisis, to
avoid climbing barricades, Venetian masts
and other signs of public joy that came be-
tween her and London S.W.
When this reaches you, the Admiralty lady
will be giving her blessing to a charity enter-
tainment in North Sydney’s State school-
room. The object is to scrape together suffi-
cient cash to present free kindergarten
youngsters—now boiled down into State
school kindergarten classes —with extras not
included in the Public Instruction syllabus.
Mrs. Hay, of the Berry Estate family, is
president of the committee.

The committee of the Sydney Home for
Babies is going in pursuit of hard cash with
a wattle dance in September. St. James's
Hall will be the place.
The Medical Congress entertainment com-
mittee is settling down to solid food-and-
drink discussions. About 800 visitors from
other States are going to mingle light re-
freshment and medical science at the Pill
and Powder party in this city in September.
Yours, with a broad nib,
VANDORIAN.

FOREWORD
TO MY
AUSTRALIAN
FRIENDS.
NOW after spending some very pleasant weeks in your
interesting country, I feel sure that this book will
find many sympathetic readers in Australia. Quite
naturally it will be discussed ; some, doubtless will cen-
sure it—and unjustly ; others will believe with me that
the tale teaches a great moral lesson.
Born as the Boy was born, the end which Fate forced
upon him, to me, was inevitable. Each word and act
of the three weeks of his parents’ love-idyll must reflect in
the character and life of the child. Little by little the
baby King grew before my mental vision until I saw at
last there was no escape from his importunity, and I
allowed the insistent Boy—masterful even from his
tion —to shape himself at his own sweet will. Thus he
became the hero of my study.
This is not a book for children or fools —but for men
and women who can grasp the underlying principle of
morality which has been uppermost in my mind as I wrote.
Those who can see beyond the outburst of passion—the
overmastering belief in the power of love to justify all
things, which the Boy inherited so naturally' from his
Queen mother —will understand the forces against which
the young Prince must needs fight a losing battle. The
transgression was unavoidable to one whose very concep-
tion was beyond the law —the punishment was equally
inevitable.
In fairness to this book of mine—and to me—the great
moral lesson I have endeavoured to teach must be con-
sidered in its entirety, and no single episode be construed
as the book’s sole aim. The verdict on my two years’
work rests with you, dear Reader, but at least you may
be sure that I have only tried to show that those who sow
the wind shall reap the whirlwind.
The Author.
Obtainable at all Booksellers
in Australasia.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, July 31, 1911.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The new G.-G. sprang ashore on Monday
amid a flutter of flags and a blare of bands.
The weather was behaving nicely, and the
daily press has recorded the fact that St.
Ivilda, where the landing took place, "looked
almost beautiful.” I suppose some hoarding
is responsible for that "almost.” Paradise
itself would only win qualified praise if the
Pale Pellets for Puce Prudes man pasted an
advertisement on Peter’s Gate. After being
held up at three points by mayors who had
addresses of welcome to get rid of, the new
G.-G. and party at last arrived at Federal
Government House, where silver-trimmed
postillions and footmen in purple livery, and
a carriage piled with golden wattle-blossom,
made a striking color riot at the foot of
the numerous stone steps.
His Ex. is smaller even that G.-G. North-
cote ; but the tall aides and Major Quilter,
his towering military secretary, bring up
the average. Lady Denman is slight and
fair and well-groomed. For her first ap-
pearance she wore a coat of chinchilla,
which almost hid her lilac silk frock. As
she climbed the steps, the bystanders noted a
pair of trim ankles beneath a smartly-cut
short skirt. Her hat was big and brown.
Lady Barttelot and Miss Quirk followed the
first division, with Chamberlain Victor Hood.
The pair were in black; but one of them
smiled beneath a violet velvet hat. What
with the feminine frippery and the war mil-
linery of the staff, the front of the talking
emporium looked like a flower garden broken
loose. A rainbow would have retired in a
huff from that competition.
Indoors the Very Best People, who had
waited patiently for a solid hour, had the
satisfaction of witnessing the clanking, rust-
ling entry and the brief 10-minute swearing
ceremony. That over, Colonel Stanley showed
the newcomer the guard of honor and saluted
him off the premises. The Very Best People
tumbled hurriedly down the steps into their
waiting motors and buzzed away; and every
taxi in Melbourne panted up in its turn and
carried off the unmotored remainder in twos
and fours. It was all over. The country
was saved again.
Major Quilter, G.-G. Denman’s military
secretary, who arrived a week in advance of
his chief, is a good-looker, standing 6ft. 4in.
in his striped socks. His regiment is the
Grenadier Guards, and when he gets his
boots and his bearskin hat on he should be
noticeable in the largest crowd. Captain Sir
Walter Barttelot, A.D.C., belongs to the
Coldstreams. Another aide, Captain Stew-
art-Richardson, of the Seaforth Highlanders,
is, I believe, a brother of Sir Edward Stew-
art-Richardson, who was on the staff of a
former Bananaland Governor. The third
gentleman help is Captain Nutting, of the
17th Lapcers. These four military men,
with Private Secretary Ronald Vernon and
Chamberlain Victor Hood, comprise the staff
which will shortly be available for large and
small functions. Prompt attention given to
all orders.
The Denmans are bringing their own maids
and Jeameses to this benighted country. A
round dozen arrived in advance; the balance
are to invade the continent later on. The
Denman children, accompanied by nurses,
maids and governesses, are travelling hither
on a different boat to that which brought
mamma and papa.
The Riverina squatter MacMicking is re-
lated in a distant-cousinly way to Denman,
G.-G.
Sub-Governor Fuller is experimenting with
the healthful outdoor sleeping fad. He has
not, however, dragged his own Excellent bed
on to the balcony, but is getting an aide to
try it first. There is a species of canvas
house, much in use along the ti-tree beaches,
which would be charming enough even for
the garden of Stonington. If the experiment
is a success, the “standing room only” sign
at State Government House may be taken
down when Spring has definitely arrived.
Artist Bunny had Toorak crowding round
his paintings on the opening day of his
show. Enthusiastic art lovers got glimpses
of the canvases between the latest weep-
ing plumes. A fashionable hat now-a-minute
fits like a pixie’s cap; but the projecting
willow branch or water-jet swishes and jabs
at the eye of the reluctant follower. The
crowds ascending the stairs to the upper
room of the Athenaeum found Mrs. Bunny
with sister-in-law Mrs. Donald Mackinnon
waiting to receive them. Mrs. Mackinnon
was in powder-blue, while Mrs. Bunny
tailored in purple velvet. Governor Fuller
motored along with Aide Awdry; Lady
Fuller was in Geelong for the day. (Per-
haps the pair have agreed to halve the social
round wherever possible, and in that way
seize more hours to play with the babies.)
The departed Carmichael was particularly
good at art-show speeches. This was Ful-
ler’s first show, and he disappointed the ex-
pectant mob by making no introductory re-
marks. An explanatory murmur of “It’s
not done in London now, y’ know,” ran
round the room, and the picture-gazing be-
gan without delay. Although his Ex. dis-
tributed no opinions or theories about paint-
ing, he caused the red spot to appear before
many eyes, and the infection spread steadily,
notwithstanding that a search through the
catalogue discovered that 20 guineas was
law-water mark.
Concerning the Bunny pictures themselves
a hardened critic sends this note: —
Rupert Bunny’s array of paintings at the upper
Athenaeum Hall is disappointing in the monotony of
its effect. The prevailing note of the 103 pictures
is sameness —same two or three girls grouped, same
general gaudiness of drapery and environment, a
superfluity of balcony, an irritating first impression
of oleographs rather than original work. Many of the
figures pall upon the eye. partly because of their
family resemblance, but chiefly because tne heads and
faces are too impressionistic to arouse human in-
terest. The picture too often ends at the point where
the hard labor of artistic craftsmanship should begin.
Of course there are striking successes here and there,
even in the impressionist style, and among the land
and seascapes, as well as among the many that have
Woman for the principal theme. In these the leading
lady is mostly Mrs. Bunny, who certainly does credit
to the artist’s selection of a model. But in the midst
of so much of a mediocre muchness, one loses en-
thusiasm for the finest pieces. Where one expected
to admire everything, only the best examples of
Bunny art seem truly good. And one has the same
old feeling that the art'st, in putting high prices on
his largest pictures, is too apt to take the will for
the deed.
RUPERT
BUNNY ;
at the
exhibition
of his
pictures in
Melbourne .
At the City Newsboys’ Ball, chief pat-
roness Lady Fuller hung brown broideries
of ninon over gold net, and perched a glit-
tersome tiara in her golden hair. Auntie
Phipps, the vital spark of the vice-regal
circle, frivolled in a fancy set as a magpie
with-a black velvet dress, powdered hair and
a stuffed bird on her shoulder. Admiral
Creswell’s wife wore grey and a committee
badge. Mrs. Guy Sherwood, who expends
much time and money on the boys, brought
two of the youngsters along to present lilies
of the valley to her Ex. She was daintily
clad in lilac and old lace. When the com-
mittee was settled securely on the dais, the
fancy sets squared up for the Lancers. Mrs.
Emmerton managed a Wattle group, most
of them recent brides, with daughter Mabel
(Mrs. Norman Brookes) in the lead. Mrs.
Stuart-Murray (Frankie Oliver) was an
ornament to this set, with gold leaves in her
hair. , Dorothy De Grut had a White Aus-
tralia set. Many of her young chums are
not back from the Coronation crush; but
she had some dainty girls in the quartet.
They wore wide hats, which were rather
cumbersome in the crowded state of the
floor, and had a tendency to get mixed up
with the rosella parrots the wearers carried
among their gum-leaf staffs. Mrs. Hodges
had the Newsboys’ set, made up of dignified
personages in black velvet who carried daily
papers folded over their shoulders. The
University crowd provided a vivid contrast.
The men were in their black gowns, the girls
in scarlet.
After the first dance, there was a parade
round the room. It proved rather a terrible
business. Two by two, like Noah’s shipload,
the performers processed towards the salut-
ing base; but although the Fullers bowed
and beamed like twin searchlights, they
only got an occasional bow in acknowledg-
ment. Alfred Deakin’s tall daughter, who is
on the committee, walked arm-in-arm with
dad, and looked a graceful circumstance in
a long-trained white dress.
The Coronation returned-empties are being
worried to death by the stay-at-homes, who
will keep on asking where they saw the pan-
tomime from. The invariable answer seems
to be “The Abbey, of course.” If I hear of
another half-dozen saying it, I’ll begin to
think the old place is made of rubber.
A note from the Coronating “Rosna” :
The circus part of the Coronation couldn’t give
many points in the matter of organisation to the
Suffragette procession that spread itself over six
miles of London the week before. The popular
fallacy that the British Suffragettes rush about
smashing windows and policemen for exercise, or
just for pure cussedness, must have staggered after
seeing that show, when 40,000 women walked
through London in a great, orderly, unhysterical
throng. Even the haughty British press, usually
silent or abusive or flippant on the Suffrage topic,
came down off its high steed, and threw bouquets at
“the best-managed procession ever seen in London.”
As a spectacle it was equally successful. The Com-
monwealth and Maoriland, being enlightened, were
headed by a triumphal car; in its wake walked the
Chief Justice Stout dame (M.L.), Mrs. Andrew
Fisher, Mrs. McGowen, the wife of ex-Agent-General
Cockburn (S.A.), Vida Goldstein, Mrs. Bowman
(Q.), and sundry other good Australians. All sorts
of British notabilities took part. The Writers’
League sent Sarah Grand, Elizabeth Robins, Mrs.
Bernard Shaw, Flora Annie Steel, and a crowd more.
Of the actresses there were Lillah McCarthy, Lena
Ashwell, Eva and Decima Moore, Gertrude Kingston,
Yvette Guilbert, and Olive Terry. Princess Bariat-
insky and Annie Besant walked in the throng; also
Lady William Butler, Lady Frances Balfour, Lady
Beatrice Kemp, Muriel Lady de la Warr, Lady
Scott Monerieff and various other behandled per-
sonages—among them Princess Sophia Duleep Singh,
who has been saying- “No vote, no taxes,” in a firm
voice to the authorities recently. Then there were
the hundreds of women graduates in gowns and
hoods, and the 700 white-robed and valorous dames
and damsels who have been to Holloway at one time
or another.
I see that a confectionery firm lately pre-
sented George V. with a cake weighing
6001 b., and that he graciously accepted it.

Lala Williams, the pretty, dark-haired
sister of a one-time politician, was married
last week to Hugh Callan, who used to play
brilliant football for South Melbourne a
few seasons b£.ck. The bridegroom's father
was a well-¥nown London Bank manager
years ago. After the ceremony, the pair
went voyaging to Cairns for a winter honey-
moon. Terang, in the western district, will
be their later place of abode.
Dr. Miller 1 ' is smiling serenely through an
epidemic of congratulations brought on by
the announcement of his engagement to Miss
Levy.
Toorak is again busy buying wedding pre-
sents. Early in August Hilda Grantham is
to marry Captain Bertram Graham in St.
John’s, Toorak. Another August wedding
concerns the Grices; but this function will
happen in Sydney. The two people most
concerned are Tom, son of John Grice, ami
Miss Clarice Hewlett.
A gallant majah, who admired her all the
time that she was growing up, lately elicited a
fluttering “Yes” from pretty Miss Marjorie
Bingham, sister of Mrs. Anthony Hordern.
Talking of smart people who spell their
names with a siphon, the Winter-Irving
household is preparing to receive daughter
Laura, the Baroness, with her husband, the
German Baron. They are due by this
week’s mail from Europe. Hubby is very
out of health, and his spouse is bringing him
for a rest cure to the paternal home. Miss
Laura-as-was is to have the company of
sister Florence a few months later, but
there is no talk of brother Cyril returning
home just yet.
Esta d’Argo and tenor Ben Davies are
coming to this city in September. Esta,
like Dolores, spent years of drudgery at the
piano keyboard learning to be a pianist, be-
fore she could persuade her teachers that
she wanted to sing and could sing. Tenor
Davies, now a burly veteran, had a career
in the church mapped out for him, and
would possibly have risen to a bishopric on
his top-note if he had not neglected to take
Holy Orders.
Gladys Baker, eldest daughter of Walter,
for many years “principal” with Bland Holt,
is a member of the “Chocolate Soldier” Com-
pany, now rehearsing in Melbourne. Miss
: Baker owns a fine contralto voice, and her
friends expect her to do things in comic
opera.
A be-titled dame, who joined in the mad
rush after a theatrical star, is bewailing a
horrible mistake. The matron had left her
card at the star’s domicile, and in due
course the latter returned the call. Unfor-
tunately for the hostess, she was entertain-
ing a small but select hen-party when the
caller was announced by her married name ;
so, taking the new comer to be some climber
of no importance, the hostess, after a per-
functory greeting, waved her to a seat on
the outside edge. Only one of the assembled
guests ventured to talk to the stranger,
who, having a few hundred visits to return,
soon rose to depart from the chilly atmos-
phere. “Good-bye,” murmured the star.
"Good-bye,” replied the hostess coldly. “So
kind of you to call on me,” smiled the star.
The hostess still looked as if she had regis-
tered a vow never to smile. So the visitor
kindly added, “I am afraid you don’t know
me. lam Miss .” Then a change came
over the spirit of the matron’s dream. She
gasped. Then she gushed. But she gushed
too late. Her visitor, still smiling, refused
compliments, attention or tea. She just
twinkled out of that doorway for ever.
“At the Sign of the Southern Cross” is
the rollicking title of a book of serious poems
by Annie Macdonald. The volume was
given a good airing at the Esperanto Club
last week, when extracts were recited by
Mr. H. Payne to a friendly audience, which
included Dr. and Mrs. Tyson Randall, ana-
lyst Blackett, artist Bunny and wife, and the
Donald Mackinnons. Miss Dorothy Gibbons
sang poems set to music, and Louise Smith
had charge of the piano. Margaret Basker-
ville read a paper on sculpture and poetry,
and the proceedings generally were on a
skyscraping plane, till refreshments and the
lift brought all back to earth again.
Goathland, the “elegant mansion” of the
late Malcolm McEacharn, at Ivew, is to go
under the hammer. The trustees offered it
to the Victorian Government, whose repre-
sentatives viewed it with some idea of ac-
quiring it as a hut for the Excrescencies.
But Governors require large dining-rooms;
and, although this apartment at Goathland
is of respectable size, it was regarded as too
small; so there was no business.
In consequence of a rush of donations to
the cashbox, Ballarat is getting ahead
rapidly with its nursing scheme. The musi-
cal city has the charity habit in an acute
form. The chemists, following the lead of
the cabmen and tram Co., have offered to
compound medicine free of charge for the
patients of the new Nightingale, Nurse
Jessie Reeves. If this sort of thing con-
tinues, she will find it difficult to get rid of
money at all. The Free Kindergarten move-
ment is also geting a lift in this vicinity.
Mrs. Alfred Deakin arrived last week with
Mrs. Camphin, the kindergarten expert who
has been doing such good work in Melbourne.
If all goes well, the babies will be able to sit
round and learn things early next year.
The muff, used in the beginning solely for
warmth, has gradually grown to the size of
a gunny-bag, and the average woman has
become so accustomed to having her hands
occupied in carrying it that the approach of
warmer weather terrifies her. “What will
we do with our hands?” is the distressful
cry of the moment. To utilise them in doing
the housework is, of course, unthinkable.
There is a rumor afoot that summer may
find us with wicker-work muffs decorated
with flowers. Here is a chance to popu-
larise the useful market basket. Let it be
the thing for fashionables to carry large
market baskets trimmed with seasonable
vegetables. If Fashion dictates that the
only -time of day to be seen taking the air
is the early morning, small-waisted house-
hold bills should come in with a rush.
A harem skirt whirled out of one crush at
the Newsboys’ Ball, and vanished in an-
other ; but I had time to notice that the
tunic was very long, and that the (no other
word for them) trousers were wide.
Managers of registry offices, where legend
says Marianne once grew wild, are missing
a chance of fortune by neglecting to run tea-
rooms in connection with their dismal
bureaux. Maid-less missuses spend hours
and much cash in adjacent fooderies whilst
waiting for help in a cap and apron. The
same women meet day after day in these
dingy halls of hope, exchanging lamenta-
tions and eyeing one another’s millinery.
Only when utterly tired out do they reluct-
antly leave to spend money elsewhere in
much-needed refreshments. All the time she
is away the helpless one is tortured by the
thought that the model maid may have
turned up and been spirited away by one of
the other cats. Let a registry office fix up a
tearoom and lounge, and strew it with the
latest fashion papers, and servant hunting
will become a social game. Why not have
bridge parties in the back boudoir, the pen-
alty for leaving the game to go and interview
a cook or parlor-maid being fixed at half-a-
crown for “Kitty,” the last surviving
missus to get “Kitty” as a consolation
stakes?
A smart Melbourne girl, of the habitually
unusual sort, seems to have tickled Rudyard
Kipling’s fancy with her blunt-nibbed pen.
In a wild moment she dashed off a letter to
him, telling him how she loved most of his
books, and two of them in particular, and
asking him to autograph the enclosed por-
trait which she had torn from a magazine.
Kip replied with the sweetest of notes to
the effect that he hoped Australia had a lot
of girls at home like her. Also, he sent
autographed copies of the two books she had
named, and pledged himself to find one of his
very best photos and forward it anon.
A Coronateur who has been wandering
around loose with the nobility, writes
heatedly as follows :
Australians say that English Society women
see nothing of their children. What a calumny!
This mother (a duchess) reads to her children for an
hour every day, visits them in the schoolroom, and
tucks them in at night with her own hands. She
never goes to bed herself without first going into the
nursery, and she assures me that nine out of every
10 mothers do the same.
There now!
Among the men who deal largely in Di-
vorce Court proceedings there is talk of
“awful revelations” to come from a suit
which is “listed for next sitting.” What the
waiter saw and the chauffeur discreetly
winked at may make matter for lovely head-
lines in the virtuous daily papers. The par-
ties are labelled “Smart Set,” and their
names have loomed large in small paragraphs
for years past.
Sincerely yours,
JOHANNA.

SHOOTING SCIATICA
Tortured by sharp, hot pains
in Hip and Legs*
——■—♦
This man crippled till he had
to give up athletics.
Tells how he was cured.
In the case of Mr. Arthur J. Grice, a
prominent Tasmanian athlete, living at
Duffield-street, Queenstown, he was jcom-
pelled to abandon football and sport by re-
curring attacks of Sciatica. After : long
suffering Mr. Grice was completely cured
by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. As Sciatica,
like Neuralgia, is a disorder of the nerves,
the direct treatment to cure the cause aims
at restoring the nerves to their proper jstate
of tone. Hot applications cannot do .this.
As Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills make :'new
blood and tone the nerves, they have
achieved great success in curing Scihtica,
and Mr. Grice’s case is a striking instance.
He said :
“Up to the age of 23 I was always strong
and healthy and a well-known athlete.
From that time forward I was compelled
to give up all sport, such as running, foot-
ball, etc., owing to repeated attacks of
Sciatica in the right hip, extending to the
right knee. I consulted several doctors who
prescribed for me some medicine to take;
some blistering me and giving me lotions.
The blistering was so severe that it took all
the skin off my hip to the knee. The pain
at times was well nigh unbearable. It: was
very sharp, like needles being pushed , into
the flesh. I used to : be worse at night,
especially if I got wet or had been working
in water. The sciatic nerve was very tender
to the touch. The attack used to vary,
sometimes lasting for a week or more,: and
at other times for a day or so. It was
always a burning pain; walking would irri-
tate it.* The muscles wasted away. I had
been in this state for about 10 years,, and
whenever it came on I used to consult the
doctors, but without receiving permanent
relief, and had come to the conclusion that
it would come and go whenever it liked.
Eventually a fellow workman who had been
cured of Sciatica after being bedridden for
three weeks, advised me to give Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills a trial, as they cured him.
I got a box, and benefited so much from
their use I kept on with them, and by the
time I had taken four boxes I felt com-
pletely cured. That is nearly two years
since, and I am happy to say I have never
had a twinge or any sign of the complaint
since, and 1 can conscientiously recommend
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills to anyone suffering
as I did, or for any other complaint arising
from a disorder of the blood, as I have
known several who have benefited by jtheir
use.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are 2/9 a box.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The other day R. O. Blackwood, of the
Victorian Employers’ Federation, got busy,
and, as a result, the “Liberal” party and
the “People’s” party foregathered and agreed
on a common platform, and, in consequence,
everything in the Cabbage Garden is lovely
—till the hot wind comes. The so-called
platform includes one plank for the re-
tention and “liberalisation” of the Vic.
House of Fat; another for a more liberal
policy of rail and road extension ; and an-
other for the encouragement of private en-
terprise. Whether this last plank is in-
tended to apply to road-making and so forth
or onlv to such things as coal mines, trams,
etc., isn’t stated —and, for that matter,
doesn’t need to be.
DISGRACEFUL CONDUCT.
There was a large amount of flabby sentiment
about the cry of child slavery. Every child born
should be taught to make himself useful from the
time he first could become useful. (Applause.)—
Wade, at the Farmers’ Conference.
N.S.W.: “Wot’s ’e done , Constable!”
CONSTABLE WADE, V.C.: “Done? The lazy
little cow wants to go to school, instead of
earning his poor father's living!”
The Anti-Sosh farmer has been flirting
with the “tiger” again! This time it was
the Shire Council of Bulla (Sunbury a.nd
the country round) which viewed with
much favor a proposal to establish a muni-
cipal sheep-shearing outfit, and would have
gone into the business but for the fear that
the eastern riding, which only becomes in-
terested in sheep when they are mutton,
would “kick up” at subsidising the western
riding, which grows the jumbucks in its
paddocks.
Quoth ’Enery Weedon in the Vic. As-
sembly the other day :
I do not approve the principle of promotion by
merit in the police force, and the police do not.
Of course not! What should merit have
to do with anything? If the stupid principle
were encouraged, some fool might try to
apply it to even the Legislature presently.
The greedy political Fatman, who thought
he was going to make a stalking horse once
more of “The Flag,” must be horribly disap-
pointed at the total absence of any commo-
tion in England over Andrew' Fisher’s re-
mark that the Empire wasn’t an Empire, but
a very loose association of nations. The
fact, as The Bulletin remarked last week,
is that J. Bull has himself been saying the
same thing so often lately that he really
doesn’t notice when anybody else says it.
Thus the London Referee, an Imperialistic
and somewhat Jingoistic paper, said on June
8, speaking of the hospitable relations be-
tween Britain and the Dominions: —
For a hundred years the foreign policy of the Em-
pire, which is not an Empire, but a System, has been
conducted from London.
So, if Fisher did say to W. T. Stead:
“We are not an Empire, but a loosely sys-
tematised association of nations,” he would
have been merely echoing the sentiment of a
red-hot “Imperial” journal.
SLIPPERY POLITICS.
The Weary-Watt Government is going to
the trouble of introducing a Bill to abolish
the unclean habit of “kissing the Book,”
and it proposes to substitute the English
practice of taking the volume in the right
hand and repeating the present superfluous
remarks. The Scottish form of oath —in
which the Book is not used, the right hand
being raised while the swearer repeats a
form of words—is already a legal oath in
Victorian courts, and all that is necessary
is an order from J. Dry Brown to magis-
trates to swear all witnesses in that fashion.
Still, the abolition of the mouthing of the
insanitary volume that has been mauled and
pawed over and dribbled on by hundreds of
more or less diseased persons is something.
Prahran (Vic.) muni-
cipal council has suffered
another Socialistic out-
break, and lately ordered
free lectures for the local
inhabitants. The first of
the “talks” was delivered
by Professor Skeats, and
was on the dreadfully ex-
citing topic of “The Re-
lation between Scenery
and Geology.” A small
house heard the wisdom
with an air of suppressed
boredom. Towards the
end of this month the
second of the addresses
will be given by Archi-
bald T. Strong, who will
tell Prahran about the
sublimities of Swin-
burne’s poems, Swin-
burne being, of course, a
person who lives a great
distance away. This
paper cordially approves
of free lectures; but, somehow, the syllabus
doesn’t seem to have begun as happily as it
might.
The prospect of a La-
bor daily is about the
. only real light in the
tangled N.S.W. political
situation —a tangle due
mainly to the Referenda
outbreak of State Spites.
The weekly Worker,
though it does good pro-
paganda work, can’t push
into the argument often
enough; the party must
have its say. Watson
saw the supreme neces-
sity of a Labor daily
when he abandoned a
safe seat and a Cabinet
leadership to work tor it.
Kitchener began a suc-
cessful invasion of the
Soudan by ordering a
railway in Birmingham.
Watson, similarly long-
sighted, sees that the
supreme weapon in the
Labor campaign is the
daily, and in that, though
so little heard of nowa-
days, he shows his states-
manship and his capacity
for leadership.

night in the N.S.W. Assembly when he

like many more, had been swindled out of
his pension rights by the Public Service
Board. When Wade was in office he strenu-
ously and successfully opposed a similar
motion on Carpenter’s behalf, and when he
was reminded of the fact the other night, he
had the nerve to reply that he hadn't the
full facta then, though, observe, he was the
Minister at the time. Was that confessed
ignorance the reason that he also fought
Giddy, Heffernan, Preville and others right
up to the Privy Council, and landed the
State in a swamp of Costs and muddied its
reputation for fair play to old and faithful
servants? What sort of man is that, on his
own admission, to truest. thgl' country to f
It moves! Even Weary’s acting-Health
Minister, landlord llazelthorn, has concluded
that the time is ripe for a municipal milk
supply, and has called a conference of muni-
cipal councils to see if some scheme can be
hatched out. Certainly, the propputy-own-
ing, Right Thinking class, which mostly
rules the councils, doesn’t seem a very likely
hen to do justice to the egg.

The dreadful land tax is still at it. A
large slice of Kilmany Park, which has been
lying like a tombstone on Sale’s chest, has
been sold to the Victorian State Government
—the fool Government, by the wmy, which
lately complained that the Commonwealth
tax w r as embarrassing it.
Melbourne Age on the aldermen of the
local City Council: —
The city has municipalised electricity for light
and power'; but there is good reason to suspect that,
instead of utilising its electrical scheme for the full
benefit of ratepayers, the City Council has restrained
the expansion of electrical power in the interests of
the gas monopoly. The aldermen are the champions
of Vested Interest.
True enough that the aldermen are the in-
struments of Vested Interest. But they are
not the champions, correctly speaking. The
champions are, in the House of Fat, which
will in due course reject Prendergast’s
Aldermen Abolition Bill when it has passed
the Assembly. The one grand, final de-
fender of public abuses in Victoria is the
House of Fat.
Waddell’s wild attack on Justice Higgins
is not his first offence. At Katoomba, in
April last, he got off this bilious piffle about
the Australian majority that returned the
Federal Labor party to power:—
What they (the Labor party) wanted was for the
vast majority of the people who were industrious,
thrifty, sober and energetic to maintain the idle,
drunken, dissolute loafers and wasters of the com-
munity. The whole influence of the Labor party all
along “the line was to drag down the energetic, in-
dustrious honest man, and to make much of the idle,
scheming loafer.
In this case the electors were able to strike
back, and they hit the Twaddellers where
the chicken met the axe. But it is still left
for the electors of Belubula to say if the
man so blisteringly described by Justice
Pliggins fairly represents them.
A recent Freetrade psean was sung in
Melbourne Argus over the growth of Bri-
tish ship-building, and this allegation was
made: —-
To secure this supremacy she has to pay no costly
subsidies, bounties, or subventions to either building
firms or ship-owners. But. . . during 10 years, the
French Government paid nearly 5-1 million pounds
in bounties and over £10,000,000 in subventions for
postal services.
This “no-subvention” yarn is getting rather
dull. The Tariff Commission in 1909 noted
that British Government payments for ship-
ping services were £2,000,000 a year, which
.passes the French average, and the Admir-
alty orders to British shipbuilding yards
averaged £21,000,000 a year. Add what
they call “administrative protection” _ (viz.,
preference to home industries), and it will
be seen that British shipbuilding is by no
means the Dry Dog it is cracked up to be.
A MARTYR TO PRINCIPLE.
Referring to the recent Treaty between England
and Japan, Mr. Bruce Smith is of opinion that the
“racial pride” of Japan is entitled to greater respect
from Australians, who should cultivate a “national
modesty” in regard to Britain’s ally.

A weary Liberal without a roost is I’on-
ald Mackinnon, the “philosophic-Rndieal,”
M.L.A. for Prahran (Vic.). He has just
mourned publicly that he can find no Party
and no Programme. Labor he curses, and
the Mixed Tories he mentions with a shud-

Labor members Tunnecliffe and Outtrim,
of Victoria, are sponsoring two little Bills
which have death written on their ,face.
One is a Daylight Saving measure, by
which the Cabbage Garden would advance
all its clocks one hour from the first Sun-
day in October until the first Sunday in
March. The idea has something to recom-
mend it, but would be an abomination un-
less adopted by all the States. The other
Bill makes provision for the application of
the Referendum and the Initiative, on a
petition signed by 50,000 electors. Thig is a
democratic idea that ought to be embodied
in every constitution; but as about the first
use Victoria, would make of it would be to
abolish the House of Fat, that mossgrown
ruin is certain to “view it with alarm” and
fall on it.
MORE TROUBLE FOR COLLINS STREET.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“A strike in the middle of the harvest.” This is
the threat now flatly uttered to farmers. A season’s
output would be lost. The factories would be with-
out raw material. The populace would be without
food. The suffering of the very poor would be the
worst, and it might lead to actual starvation. —
Ghastly pen-picture painted by the Argus, 26/7’ll.
The Collins-street farmer bent his head in terrible
grief, and sobbed ;
His scalding tears persistently among the proof-
sheets lobbed;
He wept, his face upon his hands, his whiskers
clogged with ink,
And moaned in pain: “Oh, my heart will
break for the pup-pup-poor, I think!”
From red eyes, large and sparkling signs
of ceaseless sorrow oozed;
Within his stout, well-nourished frame his
beautiful soul was bruised.
“Oh, sorrow!” he sighed, “the poor will
see starvation loom in sight
When pup-pup-prices rise. (Yes, we will
arrange for that, all right!)”
He opened his face and howled, and great
was the sorrowful noise of him,
Cursing the star of the striking serf in a
manner extremely grim,
Lifting his voice to the cobwebbed roof of
his silent and gloomy den,
For that slaves should rise and carry them-
selves at last like other men!
The Collins-street farmer’s tears flowed on,
and the lot of them went to waste;
His long, wild whiskers strayed unheeded,
and gathered up ink and paste ;
And, oh, his soul was sore that day, with the sorrows
he did endure.
“Ah, not for my own I weep,” he said, “I’m sad for
the pup-pup-poor!”

FRANK HENTY.
HOW TO REMEDY HAIR TROUBLES.
BY A SPECIALIST.
No matter how careful you may be of your
hair —how often or how faithfully you may
shampoo or brush it —the hair cannot attain
the thick glossy brilliance nature intended for
all men, women and children so long as the
dandruff germ is sapping the strength and
vitality of the hair roots. Frequent brush-
ing of the hair and a shampoo once every two
or three weeks are both valuable aids to
gaining hair beauty, but they cannot destroy
the dandruff germ. So far as known the
only way to destroy this germ is to rub into
the scalp twice a day a mixture prepared by
mixing 3oz. Bay Rum with |-dram Menthol
Crystals and loz. Lavona de Composee. As
this preparation not only destroys the dand-
ruff germ, but is also a truly marvellous hair-
■ Tower, it should be aoplied only where hair
is desired. Any chemist can prepare the above
formula, or you can procure the ingredients
and mix them at home; but you must use it
regularly; and be sure to brush your hair
frequently and give it an occasional sham-
poo. Avoid the frequent use of hot curling-
tongs. as they tend lo make the. hair, dull
and lifeless. ....

SPORTING
NOTIONS
As Sporting Notions expected, Pearce was
not a match for Arnst. He was in splendid fettle,
and full of confidence, and he rowed himself right out.
Ihe trouble was that when he had produced all that
was in him, the sum total was less than Arnst’s total;
*md that was the end of it. The longer swing of the
Maorilander, backed up by his superior strength,
won him the race.
Pearce made the pace a cracker from the start,
and led the way. Arnst seemed to lack the freedom
and dash so characteristic of him. He was not
coruscating, but he plugged hard ; and in spite of all
his fireworks, at the half-mile Pearce was not more
than a length in the lead. At this point Arnst
looked round to see where his rival was, and im-
mediately began to put more power into his strokes.
Approaching the mile, Arnst drew level, but got
into Pearce’s water, and a foul seemed imminent.
It was only avoided by Arnst practically stopping
dead to allow Pearce to get clear. This little epi-
sode, though it saved Arnst from fouling, once more
gave the challenger a load of a length as they passed
the mile beacon. The time was smin. 35sec. Pearce
thus stood forth as the equal of the great Harry
Searle for the mile, and thereat his followers took
fresh heart.
AVhen Pearce had got his clear length ahead, Arnst
became suddenly very busy. His sluggishness in the
first mile disappeared. He became brilliant. His
boat fairly flew through the water as he overhauled
Pearce. On the steamers the Arnst party was begging
all and sundry to take 2 to 1, but there was no
business. If it I>< not crushed the challenger, that
burst of speed had at any rate broken the hearts of
his backers. Gliding past, Arnst settled down with
the lead of a length and a-half. He had cut out
the mile and a-half in Bmin. 39sec., while Pearce
had taken three seconds longer. It was at the mile
and three-quarters that Pearce’s stamina raised its
first signals of distress. He seemed to slow down,
while Arnst increased the distance between them.
At the two miles, which had been covered in llmin.
20sec., Pearce made a great rally, and succeeded
in creeping a little closer to the champion. But
the race was over and the procession had begun,
and at 2J 'miles Arnst was five lengths to the good,
or, to reduce it to time, lOsec. ahead of Pearce.
Then he slowed down, and paddled home in ease
and comfort, with four lengths to spare. The time
for the full course was 19min. 46sec.—-a record for
the full distance in the championship race. The
time would have been less —much less, in fact—had
Arnst not slowed down in the final stages of the
race, when he knew that the prize was won.
After the race Arnst indulged in his usual
rhodomontade about “the hardest race of his life,”
Pearce being much better than he thought, he (Arnst)
being fit only for bed, and all the rest of it. As a
matter of fact, the big Maorilander was never really
extended after the first mile. He only came out of his
shell once, and that was when he took the lead. As
a cool-headed customer, Richard Arnst takes a lot of
beating. His eulqgy of his opponents is due to
ARNST , STILL THE WORLD'S CHAM-
PION SCULLER.
genuine good nature, no doubt; but it is excellent
policy all the same. Summed up, it amounts to
this: “This man is a marvel. It is simply wonder-
ful how he scoots along. The way that he rowed
satisfies me he is one of the greatest scullers the
world has ever known. His skill is perfection, his
strength is gigantic, his pluck beyond human under-
standing. I had to row myself blue to beat him.
Re is a wonder!” And so, by praising his op-
ponent to the skies, he gilds his own glory; for no
matter how great a man the vanquished may be, the
vanquisher, which is Arnst, must be greater!
There have been 41 struggles for the
world’s sculling championship; 21 were decided on
the Parramatta, nine on the Thames, and the balance
in other sections of the globe. Since Trickett beat
J. H. Sadler on the Thames, in 1876, £33,300 has
been put up as stake-money.
Professional sculling provides more dead-
heads than any other branch of sport. On Saturday,
it was estimated that over 60,000 lined the banks of
the Parramatta, while only a few thousands paid for
a place on the steamers. The £BOO “gate” which
they yielded will be divided between Arnst and
Pearce, after exes, are paid. Even this little lot
was the biggest “gate” since Beach and Hanlan met
’way back in the ’Bo’s.
The training for the sculling champion-
ship has pushed another sculler into the limelight.
Frank Starr —one of Arnst’s pacemakers, and yet a
novice—has been matched against young Peter Kemp
as a try-out.
* * * *
Next Saturday opens the V.A.T.C. meet-
ing, which is the same as the V.R.C. Grand National,
only less so. Acceptances for the Australian Hurdle
Race (worth £lOOO to the winner) will not be de-
clared until this paper is going to press; yet it is
safe to predict that the field for the 34-mile event
will be less than a score. The distance seems all in
favor of the light-weights, but thus far not one of
them, bar Rufus, is fancied, it being taken for
granted that Bribery (now reduced to 9st. 51b.) has
lost his pace. If he had only half-lost it, the old
hero would be good enough, anyhow. And if Rufus
were an undoubted stayer he would carry the bright
gold of the knowing ones; or Black Native might
win if he could stay. Leaving the ifs out of
the running, the most reasonable fancies are Obi,
because he carries the weight he humped into third
place for the recent Grand National, and tneets
Arquebuse, the winner, on 331 b. better terms ; Arque-
buse, because nobody knows how good he isn’t; and
Battery, because his fourth in the Grand National
gives him a bare outside chance, and his breeding,
like that of Arquebuse, makes for stamina. Battery
may be worth backing at the price. Up to now the
son of Bundook has disappointed his friends, but Sat-
urday’s race will give him a chance to wipe out the
stain.
* * * *
A satisfied air pervades Australian horse-
racing bodies, wherefore this season the retiring
committees of the A.J.C. (Sydney), Victorian R.C.,
and the S. Aus. J. 0., have all been returned to office
in a block.
* * * *
Sydney League Rugby competition saw a
fresh shuffle of positions on Saturday. South Syd-
ney and Glebe are now level with 16 points each,
Newtown has 13, Eastern Suburbs 12, and Annandale
9. North Sydney, AVestern Suburbs, and Balmain
have 7,5, and 2 respectively. The pot boiled over
twice <jn Saturday; once when South Sydney met its
first defeat this season, Glebe winning" by 9 points
to 5. The struggle between these clubs is always a
popular draw, no matter what code the ’bailers play
under. It has been so from the beginning, and
Saturday’s match, in spite of the boat race, was no
exception; no less than 15,000 people crowded into
the Sports’ Ground, and mostly howled themselves
dumb. It was a great match, too. After the first
half, which ended 3 points all, it was expected
that South Sydney, helped by the wind, would
simply prance over Glebe. To make matters worse,
Glebe was two men short. But the Glebe pack is
indomitable, and, rising more, dreadful from each
adverse stroke, fairly scintillated. It was a supreme
effort, and a successful one. For once in its life
Glebe richly deserved its victory. The other up-
heaval was when Eastern Suburbs, minus Messenger,
met the formidable Newtown team, and played a 13
points draw. It is a solemn fact, that when Mes-
senger is on the field, the E.S. team spends so much
time in admiring him, that he has to play the other
fellows on his own ; when Messenger is not there, the
team plays, and plays well. Annandale, which has
been having a bad time lately, on Saturday took
surcease. It met North Sydney and pranced all over
it —31 points to 11. Against Western Suburbs Bal-
main’s consistent ill-luck blossomed once more on
Birchgrove Oval. Western won by 21 to 1U
In the Union Rugby competition Univer-
sity and Newtown are together with 18 points each,
Manly is in the second row with 15, and Western
Suburbs in the third with 13. These four are left
to battle for the premiership. Early in the season
’Varsity had a great run of luck, which stopped
short when the team met Manly, though it resumed
again after the Village had been left behind. On
Saturday it met Manly in the second round, and re-
ceived its second doing of the season, the Village
winning by 10 points to 3. The other match of the
“A” division, Newtown v. Western Suburbs, was a
run-away affair, in which Newtown did as it liked —-
24 points to 3. The passing of the Western backs
was about the wildest animal seen in Sydtiey since
the days when the Bunyip roared in George-street
North.
The N.S.W. Union Rugby team which
journeyed to Queensland last week played at Bris-
bane before Wee Macgregor and 7000 other grains of
the salt-of-the-earth on Saturday. The weather was
brilliant. The play wasn’t. Both sides missed
chances galore. However, the Cornstalks unwound a
series of passing charges, so at half-time the
score was 15 to Queensland’s 3. When the second
half started Queensland did the unwinding. A little
more and Queensland would probably have won, for
N.S.W. added only 3 points while Queensland put on
9, and the balance of luck was on the side of the
visiting team. - Ma thus won by 18 to 12.
Balmain (Sydney) is a proud suburb. It
had two Soccer teams in the field, and one has car-
ried off the Rawson Cup and the other got away
with the Assoc. Cup. So the suburb feels that it can
afford to do badly at Rugby.
This being the time of year when the
strong teams are playing all they know, Saturday’s
’oofball scores in Melbourne indicated some notable
thrashings. The Premiers encountered Melbourne,
on the latter’s ground, and made hacks of their op-
ponents. The big club’s representatives excelled in
marking and speed, but didn’t know what to do with
the ball when they got it. When the bell rang the
scores were—’Wood 52 points, Melbourne 30. Rich-
mond went over to South Melbourne with a notion
of repeating their early feat of walloping Skinner’s
hirelings; but the latter are out for all the points
they can acquire, and the board’s final remark was—
South 73 points, Richmond 37. ’Roy is also on the
hunt after points, and though it had little the better
of ’Varsity up to the interval, its training stood to
it —’Roy 86, ’Varsity 49. Essendon, which has the
same incentive to boot goals early and often, tackled
Geelong, and came out with 61 points to Sleepy
Hollow’s 35; and Carlton impressed on St. Kilda
‘ the fact that a League team isn’t built in a day by
shamelessly piling up 129 points to 15. :
Though it made such a poor exhibition, the St.
Kilda team had the sympathy of the crowd. The old
Saints team, which started the season with a draw
of £3 3s. per player (subsequently reduced to £2
25., and then 305.) had gone on strike, nominally be-
cause of the exclusion from the dressing-room of
certain ex-committeemen, but in reality because the
committee, half of which was newly-elected last
March, had a mind of its own. It had advanced
some £75 out of its own pocket to finance the
players through the earlier matches, and, as this
sum is still owing, it justifiably refused to splash
around more coin than it could possibly help. So,
on Saturday week, after being soundly walloped by
Melbourne, 18 of the team “slung in their uni-
forms.” The committee, knowing the club’s chances
are gone for this season, accepted the resignations,
and set about building a new and lusty team for next
year. By Wednesday the malcontents had got wis-
dom, and sought to be re-admitted, but the com-
mittee viewed the proposal coldly, and sent the best
18 it could pick into the field, with orders to bog in
as best they could. So few of the League officials
boast the possession of backbone, that it is refresh-
ing to find a few who have a little.
In the Ass., Essendon, certain of first place
and the right to a “grand final,” if necessary, took
things easy, and let Brighton down gently with a
score of 56 points to 31. Port scraped together 46
to Williamstown’s 28 ; Brunswick improved its aver-
age by gathering in 103 to ’Cote’s 41, and North de-
posed P’ran from second position by notching 52 to
PEARCE , THE BEATEN SCULLER.
29. Also, ’Scray passed Preston a doing, to the tune
of 146 points to 16, though, as both teams retire
from public life at the end of the round, this ac-
cumulation of points looks like wasted energy.
Through the week the Law has been poking its
nose into various hoofball squabbles, but owing
to the honorarium’s fatuousness, with poor success.
Two pugnacious Port barrackers, who had to
be carried to the lock-up through a storm of stones
and jam tins, were fined 20s. each, and a Fitzroy
Junior ’bailer, who broke an opponent’s jaw, was
handed a similar penalty. The umpire, who super-
vised this latter match —’Roy Juniors v. Yarraville
—seems to have a will of his own, inasmuch as, be-
sides the ruffian above-mentioned, he reported six
others, while the following week he reported every
blessed member of the two teams, Yarraville and
Preston Juniors, for rough play, assault, foul lan-
guage, discourtesy to himself and general black-
guardism. That young man means business, but he
would do well to keep away from Yarraville for a
time.
N.S.W. Football League has procured 10
acres of the old Rosebery (Sydney) racecourse, for
the Australian Rules kickers. The enclosure will ac-
commodate cricket, tennis, bowls and other pastimes
throughout the year; and, if the present inten-
tions are not forsaken, the enclosure will be one of
the few up-to-date affairs in Sydney.
* * * *
The Stevenson-Reece game in Melbourne
revealed no reason why Fred Lindrum shouldn’t put
up a great fight against the champion, even with the
limitation of 25 consecutive hazards off the red.
Last week there was little or nothing to choose be-
tween Reece and Stevenson. They played much the
same sort of billiards, Reece being the more delicate
in his touch, and more masterly in his trick of put-
ting “side” on his ball, whilst Stevenson showed
superior management in making position for top-of-
the-table play, and was occasionally the steadier
general of the two. If the improving Lindrum was
about the same thing as Reece the other week, when
he didn’t introduce the “Gray stroke” into the argu-
ment, he can’t be much below Stevenson’s level to-
day. Presently he will go above it.
* * * *
“Snell” : White Australia is to be invaded
by Johnson, Langford and McVea, who are under
contract to fight in these parts. They are the world’s
best, with the possible exception of Joe Jeannette,
and he, too, is the two ends and middle of a coon.
Alas, for the prestige of the pale-face! There’s
some comfort, however, in the knowledge that the
all-blacks are coming here to belt each other chiefly,
although, no doubt, Willie Lang will be called upon
to do something for the white race. By the way,
consider Lang’s luck. Without doing anything ap-
proaching big licks, he has succeeded in keeping
right in the game, and is the only man of conse-
quence who can be called upon to cut purses with
visiting stoush-mongers. The latest arranged visi-
tation is due to the fact that Australia has money
to burn, and Jake Johnson is just the party to burn
it.
* * * *
At a recent Adelaide boxing conflict the
contestants were so ladylike, and the crowd called
for gore so persistently, that after round four the
local Mclnstoush jumped into the ring and announced
that, if more support were not given to the under-
taking biz., he’d give the purse to found a home for
Incurable Teetotalers. One boxer (the advt. called
him “Dave Smith, of Sydney”) refused to be any-
thing but a lady, and broke the rules of the game as
a protest. The incurable teetotalers, accordingly, lost
the purse on a foul, amid groans.
“Fighting Parson” Sams has at last met
his Waterloo at, Lucknow station (Q.). He has
taken his conqueror in tow, and both men are train-
ing hard and fast for the light- and heavy-weight
championship of North Queensland, billed for August
10, at Charters Towers. Sams and his prot£gS are
expected to carry off the championships in their re-
spective classes, and the former’s win would be hailed
with delight through Bananaland. Should Sams fail
to carry off the honors in the Towers, he will not
compete for the amateur Australian championship
soon to be decided in Brisbane.
Brisbane is to have a few fights during
Show week. On August 5 .Toe Russell and Sid Sulli-
van meet: on August 9 Bill Lang and Bill Squires
will settle the Australian Heavyweight Champion-
ship ; and on August 12 Arthur Cripps and Ted
Whiting will deal out stoush.
Owing to England and America deciding
to have their preliminary tennis match in Yankee-
land, only the final splash for the Davis Cup will be
played in Maoriland, between the winner of this
round and Australasia. The State Associations,
which were expecting to enrich themselves, therefore
miss the vehicle; but as evidence that the match in
M.L. is going to be a big affair, a syndicate has
vainly offered £5OO for the “gate.”
* * * •*
Last week the N.S.W. Lawn Tennis Asso-
ciation took another step towards the cemetery, by
deciding to sell up the eo. Lack , of funds left no
other way to dance to the music. However, there
are rumors that a new Association will be launched
to buy up the assets of the co., and begin where the
old body perished ; therefore, what should be a flour-
ishing branch of sport is to be resurrected “under
new management”—as they say at the hash-house.
And presumably the new Ass. j won’t hoist a “Keep-
off-the-grass-on-Sundays” notice.
* * * *
N.S.W. gathered in the' “rubber” with an
unbeaten record at the inter-State baseball dust-up,
last week, squelching Victoria in three games, and
obliterating Tasmania twice. The efforts of the men
from Murray’s strip of earth : were very poor; they
were defeated by 11 runs to 1, 8 to 6, and 13 to 1;
while Tasmania had a sorry time, being squashed
by N.S.W.’s second team to the tune of 30 to nil, and
16 to 7.
There is little doubt that J. D. Williams's
six days’ cycle race will prove successful: because
practically every first-class American rider, barring
Kramer, is coming to take part. Beside this dis-
tinguished horde, there will be a fair crowd of Con-
tinental riders, so Australians will have the chance
of seeing practically the whole of the world’s best
cyclists. And if that doesn’t draw Australia, the
national character has wholly changed during the
last week or so.
* * * »
The wholesale spreading of lucre about
Melbourne and Sydney bike tracks (including £ll5O
for the six days’ race in January) causes Arnst to
pause on his oars. He says he’s willing to bestride
the inflated rubbers again, but not on the off-chance
of the prizes alone. The drawing capacity of Dick
and brother Jack in the six days’ event would be
considerable, according to ltichard, and he would
want his price. From which it appears that Dick
regards Richard as a serious business proposition.
The Dunlop Rubber Co. has broken out
in a fresh place. The next caper is a relay despatch
race from Adelaide to Sydney, 1149 miles, in which
motor cyclists will be pitted against foot pedallers.
The motor-cycle section will concede at least 24
hours’ start to the foot whirlers. The cycle division
will be divided into 64 relays, ranging from 10 to
38 miles. Two riders will travel each section, so
that in the event of one breaking down the despatch
will not be delayed. Thus, 128 cyclists will be re-
quired for that part of the job. The motor-cycle
beat will range from 50 to 100 miles in length. The
motor men will also ride in pairs, though, judging
by the vagaries of some motor bikes, four wouldn’t
be too many. It is estimated that there will be
about 20 motor-cycle relays, and the 40 best men
in South Aus., Vic., and N.S.W. will be selected for
the job. Any handicap at all seems a mistake.
What we really want to find out is the relative value
pf foot- and power-driven cycles for long distances.
As it is, before the first motor-cyclist starts, the foot
riders will be right into Victoria, some 360 miles
away. And a little thing like this means that to
catch the cycle men before they reach Sydney, the
motorists will have to average 25 miles per hour.
It is known from past experience that the foot-driveh
bike can maintain an average of 164 miles per hour
for that journey. Under those conditions, the names
of the cyclists are far from being Mud, Slush and
Co.
The motorist’s luck! Lewis Strong,
holder of many records, recently went over an em-
bankment and "was killed through trying to avoid a
farmer’s cart, while travelling at four miles an hour.
The scarred airman who breaks his neck through
falling off a chair is yet to come.
The sporting worm of Maoriland has
turned at last on the Wowser, who has been playing
the part of early bird for years past. So mam-
bites have been tak@n out of him that he began to
fear that he would be swallowed whole .one of these
days. So a National Sports Protection League, with
headquarters in Wellington, has been formed to
chase the Wowser back to his rookery. Two K.C.’s
(Bell and Skerrett) are among the committee ap-
pointed to draft a Constitution.

In one respect, anyhow, Sydney has an ad-
vantage over the Yarra village. The latter
has only a pitiful 20 municipal councils
hashing up the civic government of Greater
Melbourne, and a mere handful of about 240
aldermen. Greater Sydney proudly boasts 42
municipal councils, a few shires, and a glori-
ous host of 500 aldermen. Sydney’s 42 coun-
cils manage to do nothing in particular just
as well as the 20 Melbourne ones. But the
possession of 20 more moth-eaten fur may-
oral robes gives Sydney its present civic
eminence, and leaves Melbourne a mere un-
weeded cabbage garden.
Several small Victorian country town-
ships have taken a step in municipal Social-
ism that has put them ahead of the big sub-
urban districts. Kerang, Rochester, Camper-
down, Terang, Sunbury, Mildura and Kor-
umburra have all established electric supply
stations of their own, and sell light to the
public at 6d. per unit, and power at 4d. per
unit, which is about equal to gas at 5s. per
1000ft. Yet fool councils like those of P’ran
and Haw-haw-thorn still hand over their
rights in public utilities to private com-
panies, which are obviously only in the busi-
ness for what they can make out of it.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, whether sent by post or handed
in; and no MS. will be returned unless stamps
(of any State) accompany.
J.G.8.: “Is an engine-driver, or anyone
in receipt of a living-wage, faithful to Labor' prin-
ciples if he mends his own boots?” Can’t say Yes
and can’t say No. Depends on circumstances. For
instance, Is a bootmaker faithful to Labor prin-
ciples if he walks when he might ride in a cab,
there being union cabbies in the town?.... J.C.M.:
(1) Money is anything recognised as a medium of
exchange or a measure of value. (2) The Bank of
England is not “guaranteed by the Imperial Gov-
ernment.” It is a privately-owned concern, with a
capital of about £14,500,000; and in exchange for
managing the public debt it escapes about £200,000
of stamp duty... .Roy M. C.: “Mavourneen” is the
nearest the English tongue could trouble itself to
get to mo (my) mhuifnin (darling) . ..Vishnu : Avoid
them. Salvarsan is dangerous unless administered
by a skilful doctor... .Back-o’-Sunset: This isn’t a
receiving home for the aged.... Pariah: Hood shot
at game that’s hard to bag. But you don t quite
score a hit... Frank: Yes; send claim to Accountant.
....Tom Spargoe: No use sending them if they have
appeared elsewhere C. .1. Greaves: In the 17th
century the Whigs supported the rig its of the
people; the Tories were for the King, rig.it or wrong.
In later times the points of difference changed, but
as Webster remarks: “The Whigs may be loosely
described as the party of change towards democracy,
and the Tories as the party of conservatism.”....
D.D. (Tas.) : Yes; but that truth has been presented
over and over again by skilful writers, and your pen
is very new. Try your hand on a less hackneyed
theme. If you can think out a new plot, or give a
new twist to an old one, you discount your inex-
perience. . ..C.M.H.: Hurriedly corked up again and
thrown overboard.... Armis: The tragedy is well
hammered in, anyhow:—
FAREWELL.
My pulse is beating slower—
Tick ! tick ! tick !
See the candle’s getting lower!
Wick ! wick ! wick !
“Oh, Nurse, do please come here
Quick ! Quick ! Quick !
I feel so very queer
And sick, sick, sick!”
The nurse in time just got his head
Only to find him dead, dead, dead.

cient humor to carry it over the bar...-E.W. (W.A.) :

D.McI.: You make very heavy weather of it. To
get a rhyme for “dawn” you have written “When
the scowl of Night’s in pawn.”... .Marion F.: Two
lines:—
And while Elfrida on the turret wept
High over all the hordes of Stribbish swept.
Never heard of Stribbish before, but guess he has a
hook-nose. Don’t know what the rest of the poem
is about....J. T. Doc.: No, Doc. To write +he good
old “days of yore” brand of poem it is only neces-
sary to wait until your head feels particularly empty.
The still, fag-end of a long, exhausting summer’s
day is the most suitable time, and it is advisable
to take off your boots. Dip your pen in the ink
and allow it to straggle along almost of its own
accord. The usual result resembles this:—
The moon has risen o’er the hills and filled the
world with light,
Ah ! I am thinking of the Past as I sit here to-night!
I wonder where my boyhood’s friends are scattered
far and wide ?
And where is she who vowed to stay forever at mv
side ?
Old mcm’ries at my heart-strings tug (And drag at
both my legs?)
The dead leaves fall. (And Sussex Street reports a
fall in eggs !)

Tanunda, one of the mostly-German
townships in S. Australia, has a “club,”
with a membership of about 500, whose
chief privilege is the right to buy whisky,
beer, or nearly any other old drink you like,
for 2d. a time. \Vith the surplus funds the
club has already built a hall, one of the
finest in the State outside Adelaide. The
success of the concern supplies a hint as
to what would be liable to happen if the
State or the municipalities took over the
liquor-retailing business, and didn’t reduce
the price to 2d. a time.
Why doesn’t the Argus, when discussing
State and Private Enterprise, in tramway
and railway matters especially, take notice
of some such things as this record by H. G.
Wells, in “New Worlds for Old” (1908) :—
Nearly all the British railway companies present
now a similar degenerative process. Years of profit-
sweating, of high dividends, have left them with old
stations, old rolling-stock, old staffs, bad habits, and
diminishing borrowing power. Only a few of these
corporations make any attempt to keep pace with
invention. It is remarkable now, in an epoch of
almost universal progress, how stagnant the British
privately-owned railways are. ... An observant
person cannot fail to be struck—especially if he is
returning from travel upon the State railways of
Switzerland or Germany—by the shabby-looking
porters on so many of our lines, and by the bleak
misery of many of the stations, the universal dirt
that electricity might even now abolish.
A few plain, comparative facts like these are
worth miles of thundering theory.
The shades of Batman and Fawkner will
probably take a trip across the Styx to
watch the arrival of the first of the two
15,000-ton Holt steamers, and see them tie
up in the Yarra. The pair of ogean giants
are to be built with comparatively light
draught, so that, after rolling across the
world, they may kick their way up the shal-
low, hard-worked canal, and dump their
loads of merchandise right on the doorstep
of Melbourne’s fluff and hardware empori-
ums. And away to the west is the fine deep
harbor of Portland, very much the same as
when the Ilentys caught whales there. One
can imagine Melbourne's pioneers looking up
in amazement at the floating mountains, and
wondering why the moderns go to such
trouble to use the old muddy site Batman
purchased, or thought he purchased, from the
Jaga Jaga family for 200 handkerchiefs and
some sundries.

Jankers of Jh£fi Grade- Pianos
THE greatest improvement in piano construction during the past 40 years was brought about by our invention of
a simple, but thoroughly efficient steel tuning system, in place of the wooden system still used in foreign pianos.
This invention is patented throughout the world, and is obtainable only in Beale High-Grade Bianos. its
success is beyond question, as any one of 15,000 buyers of Beale Pianos can tell you. We have used it for the past
nine years in all our pianos, and from the first day that a Beale Piano was issued with the Beale-Vader Patent Steel
Tuning System, right up to the present day, we have never had one single complaint in connection with the timing system.
This record is unique in the history of piano manufacture, for the tuning system has hitherto always been the weakest
part of pianos. The necessity for a steel tuning system is pointed out by Mr. Edgar Brinsmead, of Messrs. John
Brinsmead and Sons,London, makers of the Brinsmead Pianos, who says in his book (“Tlie History of the Pianoforte”)
that the driving of iron pegs into a wooden plank is “a primitive plan” which “neither secures nor retains accuracy”;
and he says the wood “ occasionally ” splits, “ frequently ” loses its holding power, and is “ invariably ” affected by climatic
changes. Yet that “primitive” system is still used by foreign piano makers. Herr Julius Blutliner, of Leipsic, maker
of Blutliner Pianos, publishes methods whereby “the risk of splitting and warping” with the wooden tuning system
may be “ reduced ’’—but not abolished. The British and Colonial Piano Journal says (March, 1911, page 75) that the
“fault” of pianos with the wooden tuning system “ not staying in tune” is “recognised by piano manufacturers all
over the world.”
Hundreds of inventions of metal tuning systems have been patented, but the Beale-Vader system is the only
thoroughly simple and efficient one, overcoming all the difficulties referred to, and other difficulties which we have not
here mentioned. And in addition, the Beale-Vader Patent Steel Tuning system secures a greatly improved quality of tone i
so free from all harsh and metallic qualities, and so mellow and so musical that among singers especially our pianos
are particularly admired for their unequalled suitability for accompanying the human voice.
If you are thinking of buying a piano, you should let us explain the wonderful simplicity and efficiency of the
Beale-Vader Patent Steel Tuning System; and let us show you, or send you, the written opinions of our competitors as to
the necessity for a steel tuning system.
Do this, for it may save you a large sum of money.

Business, Robbery, etc.
Sheriff Schomburgk, in his annual report on. the
gaols of S. Aus., dwells lovingly on an industry
which has been developed in Adelaide. Last year
about 500 gross of straw bottle-envelopes were
turned out, and the Jap goods were almost shooed
off the market. The prisoners not only make the
envelopes, but grow the straw as well.
* * * *
“C.R.W.”: Recently I visited the S. Aus. ostrich
farm a few miles north of Port Augusta, where
about 1000 birds are kept. I noticed that their
food consisted largely of prickly-pear bush chopped
into small pieces, while aloes, were given to them
for medicine. It struck me that perhaps in “the
prickly-pear country” in Queensland a big ostrich
farm might be started with advantage. The climate
is suitable, and the ostriches might do something
towards eradicating the pest, while money was made
out of their feathers.
While the private shindykit which was going to
take full control of Melbourne’s milk supply, and
the , Milk Suppliers’ Association, which was , going
to co-operate with a like intent, have been ponder-
ing the magnitude of the proposition, a dozen Car-
rum (Vic.) milk-growers have combined to butt
into the business with advantage to themselves and
the consumers. Instead of the Bd. a gallon they
used to get, they now get Is. ; while the consumer
gets for 3|d. a quart what he used to pay sd. for.
They started with one delivery cart, and now have
three, and they were at latest sprinting round per-
suading other cow-bailers to come in and increase
the supply and be independent of the middleman.
Certain energetic Melbourne persons propose to re-
open the long-closed meat-preserving works at Mari-
byrnong (Vic.) as a margarine factory, and they
thoughtfully asked Braybrook Shire Council what
it thought of the notion. The council didn’t seem
j rabidly enthusiastic, but it opined that, as mar-
garine wasn’t a noxious weed or even’ a thistle, it
' couldn’t interfere. In this great butter-growing
country there should be absolutely no demand for
margarine, and probably there won’t be —always
assuming that the goods are offered as margarine.
And that’s where the law might conveniently speak
up, \ insisting that the new substance shall be so:
dealt with that there shall be no possible chance of
its masquerading as butter. With that done, the,
good old law of supply and demand will probably
get in some work.
* * * *
Good old “Custom of the Trade” was made to
feel wretchedly sick for a few minutes at Bendigo
the other day. A firm of produce merchants had
contracted to“ supply chaff to a co-operative society
at £2 12s. 6d. a ton, which was an unpayable price -
as things are; so it tried to balance matters by
making 15cwt. stretch to a score. The shocking part
of the matter was that the society’s manager refused
to regard this as anything less than plain dishonesty ;
and when the Police Court said “Six months with
hard labor,” it looked as though the Law was be-
ginning to take the same sensible view. The Law
and the Profits have, however, been friends for so
long that the former presently weakened and agreed
to suspend sentence on the offender paying £5 costs,
and guaranteeing to behave himself for 12 months.
“liuffin Griffin”: Re the proposed Commission on
cow slavery amongst children (B. 29/6/’ll), I want
a Commission on paper-boy slavery. Recently my
morning paper was not coming as early as usual.
Enquiries elicited that the newsagent had had trouble
with his runners, and actually had to go the rounds
himself. He was quite hurt over it. Further en-
quiries showed that he paid the boys as much ias 3s.‘
a week. The newsagent gets 3d. a dozen, or 6s. 3d.
a week for 50, of which the boys get from 2s. to 35.-
Mv newsagent confessed to having 14 boys earning
money for him at this rate. He said he paid £9OO
for the goodwill of his business, and wanted some-
thing for himself. I don’t know much about com-
merce, but I figure it out that this means that he
gave £9OO for the right to sweat 14 boys.
* * * *
“Geofred”: I found it out by accident, but it
taught me more than I could learn by design. For
two unfurnished rooms; I was paying 17s. 6d. per
week, which was supposed to include 2s. 6d. for the
use of gas —cooking, lighting, ironing, etc. One
morning I could not get my gas-ring to work, and
on investigation discovered a plug of soap under-
neath the rubber-tubing. Hostilities between Mrs.
liooms-to-Let and myself started right there, and I
was not surprised a few evenings later, when I came
home, to find no tea and Mrs. Geofred in tears. Mrs.
R.T.L. had gone out, turned the meter off and taken
the, key. All she had left at home was a note: “Dear
Sir, —i said 2s. 6d. covered use of the gas, not
abuse.” I rushed out to the wood-house, secured the
axe, and turned to attack that gas meter, when the
following quarterly account hanging up in the kit-
chen caught my eye:—
“Mrs. Rooms Vacant.
“llr. to the Gas Co., Ltd.,
“8000 ft. gas at 4s. per 1000 —£1 125.”
Then I figured it that Mrs. Rooms was taking £1
10s. per quarter from me, and the rest of the house-
hold (including the lady herself) was contributing
2s. When it dawned on me, I rushed out and found
an a<vpt. house, big enough to allow me to let
rooms. I haven’t worked since.
THE BIG FAT LIE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Our income-tax schedules, our land-tax returns all
bear ample testimony to the willingness of wealthy
men to perjure themselves for the sake of gain.—
Daily paper comment.
Instances of rich men (and women) making false
declarations for the purpose of evadin''- Customs
duties are too common to call for particular men-
tion. —Henry George.
Mr. Justice Real (Q.) recently professed a pro-
found disbelief in balance-sheets. — Courier item.
It’s always been no easy thing, right down from
Ananias,
To find a man who’ll tell the truth without a shade
of bias;
We know that Satan goes about a-roaring- like a lion,
And casually begetting tarradiddlers in our Zion;
The world is full of fishing-yarns and balance-sheets
and such
Remains of old romances that would-beat the "very
Dutch—
The Truth that’s mostly down a well is stufl you can-
not buy,
But you cannot beat the Fat Man for a big Fat lie!
lie’ll smuggle diamonds in Ins hat, and swear by all
that’s blue,
No goods that bear a duty does he try' to carry
through ;
Ilis wife, who comes from Paris, with her jewels in
her boot,
Will vow by all that’s holy she has ne’er a cent of
loot;
When income-tax is mooted, he will, calm as calm
can be,
Attest he’s always living on the verge of poverty;
You may talk about Munchausen, but Munchausen’s
all my eye—
He’d never touch the Fat Man for a big Fat lie!
I love my Scott and Dickens, and the fiction that
they wrote;
On A. Dumas and Daudet I romantically dote :
But all the knights romantic that have writ their
fancies down,
Our modern man of dollars does the deepest ‘ kind of
brown ;
He standeth high uplifted on the peaks of Perjury,
And belcheth out his heavings of a mad Mendacity ;
Oh, you’ll learn this awful lesson where the mills of
Mammon ply,
That you cannot beat the Fat Man for a big Fat lie.
QUEENSLAND.
PHILANDER F.

BARRON GORGE.
MR. DONALD McDONALD (the well-known writer)
describes the trip as follows: —
I S reater P art °f the journey on the Queensland coast the steamer runs between the
1 Great Barrier Reef and the mainland, making it one of the smoothest of the sea trips of Aus-
tralasia. The route is dotted With picturesque islands, covered with foliage strange to
southern eyes, and the boat is ever rounding headlands, streaming into fine bays and estuaries. The
famous Whitsunday Tassage, between Mackay and Bowen, is extremely picturesque.”
CAIRNS
is picturesquely situated, has a health)’
climate, and the temperature in winter
seldom falls below 50°. First-class
hotel accommodation at Cairns and
Kuranda (Barron Falls) can be
obtained at a reasonable tariff, and the
fishing at Cairns is excellent. A trip
to the Barron Falls and
scenery about that part of North
Queensland should not be missed.
