IN BANANA LAND
The Denham Government is going to intro-
duce a Bill raising the age of compulsory
attendance at school from 12 to 14 years—a
change for which teachers have been pleading
for years.
Hitherto, the man who has had the bad
luck to acquire rats in North Queensland
has been dragged hundreds of miles to one
of the South Queensland institutions for
treatment. But that is to be stopped soon,
as a site for a Northern asylum has been se-
lected on the Herberton tableland.
Many Peaks, the place whence Mount
Morgan gets its copper-bearing flux, has
grown faster than any township in Central
Bananaland of late years. When 1909 was
middle-aged there were only four or five
houses in the place; to-day there are about
<)0 people, whose thirst is attended to by
five pubs.
About a year ago, four black bruddeis
were taken from Torrens Creek to Baram-
bah mission station. Three of them by-and-
bye absconded, and now word comes that
they are back in their old haunts, 600
miles away. It was Barambah and piety
i ersus barbarism and ’possum; and Baram-
bah and piety didn’t win.
“Quan”: Some of the subsidised agricul-
tural societies in the cane-growing districts
of Bananaland encourage the growers to ex-
hibit cane, I don’t think. The Wide Bay
and Burnett Association (Maryborough) re-
cently offered a whole £2 in the sugar-cane
section, although 80,000 tons of cane were
cut in the immediate neighborhood last
season; but it had a prize list of £7 in the
poodle section.
R. E. Sexton, the new Deputy Chief En-
gineer of Railways, has been 32 years in the
Bananaland service. He started as a drafts-
man ; later on superintended the building of
the second section of the Charleville-Cunna-
mulla line, the Nerang-Tweed Heads line,
Warwdck-Goondiwindi line, and others of
less importance; and during the last couple
of years has been inspecting engineer in com
nection with the building of new lines.
The Police Offences Bill, which was intro-
duced in the Bananaland Legislative Council
the other day, sweeps up and repeals 25 pre-
vious Acts. Among the things it deals with
are vagrancy, gaming, obscene and indecent
publications, protection of children, preven-
tion of cruelty to animals, preven-
tion of stock stealing, gold dealing, pawn-
broking, second-hand dealing, traffic, motor
vehicles and special constables. A cheerful
circumstance in the gaming section of the
Bill is the proposed wiping out of Patrick
Noses.
Bananaland is trying to get abreast of the
times. Its South and Western railways are
at last, to have footwarmers. And, no
doubt, when they get a bit shabby they will,
like many another thing, be passed up to
Central Queensland.
Brisbane, the Government's gift-horse to the
Royal George, started on his long journey
the other day. They hunted all and sundry
ticks off him, and sent him to Sydney by
mail train in charge of Constable Wilson,
who will thus get a cheap look at the dump-
ing ground of Europe.
Bananaland’s little stream of immigrants
trickles from all the corners of the globe.
Among the latest to arrive are a Pata-
gonian dozen who have taken up some blocks
a couple of hundred miles west of Rock
iiampton. Up to now they have spent most
of their time chasing dingoes.
NOT WHOLLY ABANDONED.
ACTOR FELLOW: “Yes, when I am on the stage I think only of the fart I'm flaying,
and the audience entirely disappears."
OTHER FELLOW : “Oh, cheer up, old man. You've always got the ushers left, you know.”
Tiie 1 lealth-in-Mines Commission, which
said that, so far as it could see, lung disease
wasn’t extra-awful in Bananaland, might
have had a lot more data to go upon. In-
quiry forms were issued to 572 mine mana-
gers, and only 90 replied; to 217 mill and
smelter managers, and 27 replied ; to seven
union secretaries, and five replied ; to 14,039
miners, with 1894 answers; and to ,186 doc-
tors, of whom only 38 had a word to say.
Still, some of the things it saw for itself
were shocking enough. For instance, con-
veniences “are not provided for the men,
either above or below ground, in any of the
collieries visited.” As to Croydon, where
lung trouble seems to be worst, the Commis-
sion probably gets near the truth; the pre-
valence of the trouble may be “due rather to
the decline of the mining industry there dur-
ing late years, and the consequent concentra-
tion of men suffering from physical infirmi-
ties, than to any special occupational condi-
tion at present existing.”
Rockhampton, which was told by the
Sugar Mills Commission that it was dis-
tinctly not the “best sugar lands in the
State,” has decided to tackle the business on
its own account. A co. with a capital of
£20.000 has been formed, and is going to
show the Commission a thing or two.
Eighty acres have been set aside at Yer-
onga as a site for the zoological gardens that
Brisbane is alleged to hanker after. The
Government 'will put its hand in the people s
pocket to the extent of £2OOO to help the
elephant along.
Bananaland’s Railway Commissioner Evans
is dropping some Yankee notions into his
new billet. A couple of persuasive “agents”
are to be cut adrift as drummers, chiefly
among oversea steamship passengers, their
job being to coax travellers into making rail-
way tours into Bananaland.
The curse of poverty! Dr. Eleanor
Bourne, Queensland’s State medical inspec-
tress, finds that nearly 30 per cent, of the
youngsters she examined are growing up de-
fective for want of timely surgery. A corps
of Government surgeons is just as necessary
in a healthy State as a squad of State
school teachers.
Peter Murphy (who represents “the
Trade” in the Legislative Council) is indig-
nant with Denham for proposing to amend
the liquor laws. In the opinion of Council-
lor Murphy, “we should wait until other
countries had settled the liquor question be-
fore we attempted the task” —a beautifully
frank confession that Mr. Murphy thinks
Queensland should be merely the tin tied on
to th'e dog’s tail.
For a long time the Brisbane Courier
has been careful, when any charge against a
Labor representative has been brought on.
to shriek in large type, “Labor M.L.A. in
trouble!” or something of that kind. Re-
cently Cottell, M.L.A., was haled before the
P.M. on a charge of having been found in a
common betting house; but the Courier
even forgot to mention that accused was a
member of Parliament. Cottell’s defence
was quaint. He alleged that he had written
time and again to Premier Kidston and the
Home Department urging them to suppress
betting shops—a clear evidence that he w r as,
in spirit if not in the flesh, an anti-gambler.
He was ruthlessly fined all the same.
The all-consuming Cow is pushing her
way into Bananaland schools. Teachers
have long been expected to cram a 10-hours’
curriculum into a five-hour school day.
Lately, Biblical instruction was stuffed into
the schedule, and now, under Butterman
Denham’s regime, pedagogues are under-
going training courses at Gatton in “agri-
cultural studies,” which means chiefly the
alleged good points of Strawberry, and the
diseases'to which the lady is prone.
vae Victis!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The King is dead!” and Consols fell a point;
The Stock Exchange was somewhat out of joint ;
Some hapless speculator met his fate;
A broker, more or less, was fortunate....
“Long live the King!” for since he gained the throne
The market shows a reassuring tone!.... 4
And kings are crowned for this, and coffined ! Fools !
We and our dummy kings! ’Tis Israel rules t
His name of old a synonym for crime,
How lias the creaking whirligig of Time
Brought vengeance to the persecuted Jew !
He holds our honor on his I O U!
His cash-book is our corner-stone of State,
His counting houses to our Courts dictate.
And not from thrones, but from his office stools
Goes forth the fiat —for ’tis Israel rules!
With all the world’s contumely on his head,
Outcast and exiled since the day of dread
That saw the meek Messiah crucified,
“How long, O Lord! How long?” has Israel cried.
Unused to sicklej and unarmed with swords,
Their sordid Ghettoes to the pent-in hordes
Have proven bitter but efficient schools.
For Patience taught there ! So ’tis Israel rules.
Not to the victor always goes the spoil.
When captive Israel did in Egypt toil.
Was Pharaoh fain to pay a grievous fee
! Unto the Chosen for indemnity !
Eye for an eye, a tooth for every tooth —
Such was their law, and is, in very truth.
The pliant patience theirs that ridicules
The wrath of centuries —so Israel rules.
QUEENSLAND.
J.W.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, July 29, 1911.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
Our Sub-Excellency Macgregor is not on
the list of the unemployed. So much has
had to be done lately that the family has
taken to dividing itself up to cope with the
rush of “work.” And, just when everything
was running nice and smoothly, his Ex. must
needs be attacked by microbes. A slight
attack of measles, dear. Think of it!
Had it been mumps we might have over-
looked it, but measles! However, there was
nothing for it but to tuck him up and leave
him with the nurse while the Vision, arrayed
in black velvet, sallied forth to the Railway
ball, accompanied by a white-satioed mamiha
and the Scarlett aide. A night or so after-
wards, the same black frock and a bored
expression were taken to the Yacht Club
ball. You must understand that the Yacht
Club ball is quite cornered by The Best,
and the sight of so many with whom
she would like to dance, added not to
the Y T ision’s joy. She dances rarely, the
chieftain, it is said, having objections to
anything but a Miriam fling, or, a Highland
Song of Triumph, and only . then when
daughter is not held in manly tjrms. Reels
and other contortions, so it ist understood,
are in a class apart. However, all the other
people enjoyed themselves —until the morn-
ing, when the yachtsmen woke to the start-
ling knowledge, supplied by a callous woman
reporter to her paper, that “the club's
bargees were suspended from the stage.”
When the police rushed round to cut down
the bodies, nothing was found but a string
of “burgees,” which, as everybody doesn't
know, are small flags.
The Railway ball, on Tuesday, was the
finest mix-up of polished railway material
and pretty frocks and girls that the Cen-
tennial liall has seen. The mayoral Did-
dams (she in graceful grey), and the Com-
missioner and his deputy and their missuses,
helped to keep things running without a
mishap or even a collision. <
Apparently there is a shortage in the sup-
ply of good health in the cold end of the con-
tinent, for the number of Southerners ap-
plying for it up here is steadily increasing.
Among the latest are Grace Mackiehan and
her father, from Melbourne. Last year the
lady penetrated the Spice Islands, and came
back with some interesting reading concern-
ing the ways and doings of Dutch Island
potentates. She has a fancy for out-of-the-
way spots* and a blotting-paper mind which
absorbs impressions.
Possibly success has gone to the head of
the vice-regal lady junior, or perhaps the
energy which is denied outlet in other
directions pushes her on; but certainly
the Vision’s interest in good works
waxes apace. To the much-rehearsed
Society girls who recently tableaued,
she has announced her intention of
going one better than all previous efforts.
This time a kindergarten is to be given a leg-
up.
More dances! The Secretary for Lands
Macartneys gathered the exclusive Govern-
ment House set of revelling youngsters on
Saturday night, and made them happy with
an excellent dancing floor and a similar
kind. of supper. The Vision was amongst
the guests.
Said Governor Macgregor at a late func-
tion : “One of the glories of Queensland is
the provision made for its hospitals.” He
might also have said that one of the disgraces
of Queensland is the chronic cadging tor
hospitals in almost every town in the State;
and he might have added that Brisbane,
prosperous Brisbane, enjoys the proud dis-
tinction of having nearly starved its in-
firmary out of existence several times in the
last few years. -■*
Outback produces fine women, as well as
big men. A man employed on Evesham sta-
tion, near Longreach, was getting'firewood,
when the tree he was cutting down fell on
him, and split his head. He staggered into
the home station, and, as all the men folk
were away at the woolshed, Miss Murphy,
sister of the owner of Eyesham, dressed the
map’s injuries, put him . into , the sta-
tion motor-car, and drove him over-the rough
track to the doctor at Longreach, 63 miles
away. Then, having seen him in the doc-
tor’s hands, she wheeled back to Evesham
again, and didn’t reckon that she "had done
anything out of the way.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
The doctor’s bill for the Cairns-Kuranda
line, which was invalided during the last
wet season, is to hand. No. 10 tunnel,
which had the falling sickness, cost £11,223.
and maintenance and repairs for the whole
line from December 1 to date ran to £61,200.
Still, when old John Robb handed over the
line and uncorked a hogshead _of cham-
pagne, they all referred to the job as the
usual permanent undertaking.
The Sugar Workers’ strike, now prevail-
ing, is a tolerably mixed business. In a de-
bate in the Assembly, Philp, with Jiis C.S.R-
eye conveniently fixed on the piece-work
cane-cutters, said he had no sympathy with
strikers who could earn as much as £6 a
week. The strike, however, isn’t for £6 a
week, but only for £1 10s. and found, with
an eight-hours day, instead of the £1 2s. od.
and the 10-hours day, which is the lot: ot
the non-pieceworker at present. I hup
reckons the industry can’t stand the extra
7s. 6d.; but he didn't take time to explain
how the inflated C.S.R. stands the £6 with-
out collapsing.

Nowhere more urgently needed than on the farm!
Wherever you find a successful agriculturist, you will find evidence, in the shape of Artificial
Power, that he has realised the truth of the above.
And on the farms of MORE THAN ONE HUNDRED AND SEVENTY-FIVE THOUSAND
of these successful men, the artificial power in use is an “HI. C. " Gasolene Engine.
Astonishing figures, truly ! more especially when they concern an Engine practically unknown
six years ago - but figures that represent Ice Cold Facts.
Their position—a long way in front —is due solely to their Deeds and the good words their
users have for them.
“1.H.C.” Engines have invariably proved their superiority.
They've proved it to their users on the farm and elsewhere.
They've proved it to the entire satisfaction of the judges and spectators alike, in Open Compe-
tition against the world's “next best" Engines, at Aurora, Illinois, at Amiens. France, and twice
at Winnipeg, Canada.
They've proved it in a Non-stop Day-and-Night Ploughing Exhibition held at Werribee, Vic., on
August, 1910, by ploughing 66 acres in 56 hours, at a fuel and lubricant cost of 2/3UI. per acre
To such an extent have they proved it that the International Harvester Company's Factories,
although manufacturing more than One Thousand of them per week, cannot fill the orders of those
to whom “More Power" represents “1.H.C." Engine power.
In Australia, as elsewhere, the position these Engines hold is “right in the front." Last fiscal
year their sales were extraordinary; and to further popularise them, we started this fiscal year, July
Ist, by making considerable reductions in both cash and terms columns of our Jan. 1911 Price List.
In addition to this, we have now determined to put an “1.H.C." Engine within the immediate
reach of any responsible but “can't-afford-it-just-yet" engine buyer who acts promptly.
To do this, we are making an

When “Bully” Hayes was at Strong's
Island, in the Carolines, his vessel was
visited by a party of Yankee missionaries
from the mission ship Morning Star. One
of them was accompanied by a gimlet-eyed
wife, who was snocked at the number of
young Polynesian girls on board the Leo-
nora. “I guess that you ought to be ashamed
to allow sech a state of things,” she drawled.
“Madam,” said “Bully,” with his tongue in
his cheek. “I allow it in the interests or
humanity.” “Haow is thet? Well, you
see, all my officers and all my white
crew have white wives and large
families, and if I shooed off all these
native women the ship’s company , would de-
sert and become beachcombers, and their
poor wives and families would either be de-
pendent upon charity, or bedriven to do
tiungs which I blush to think about.

A CIGAR is the BEST
SMOKE OF ALL!
But why can’t you get a
Good Cigar in the Country?
auft\
ALL over Australasia the only places you can
buy a really high-class Cigar is in the big
cities. In the country districts a good Cigar
cannot be had; because it’s only at the pubs that
you can buy Cigars at all in the out-back districts.
The publican knows that selling liquor pays him
better. He reckons that his customers only call
for a Cigar when theyve got a few drinks in and
want to dodge getting “fu’ the noo." Any old
Cigar does for that.
So he buys a few hundred at a time from
some “back-yarder"—real “Flor de Cabbage"
with big gold pictures all over the box and
mighty pretty bands round them—made from the
waste leaf and the cheapest growths, but called
by some high-falutin’ name. (The making of these
Cigars is another story—l’ll tell you about it
another time.)
The storekeeper or tobac-
conist in country towns does
not sell good Cigars either.
If he does, he has to add to
the selling price of the Cigar,
the maker's profit, the profit
of the warehouseman he
buys from, the profit of the
traveller who calls and sells
them to him, and his own
profit.
So that a good Cigar
that sells in the city at 6d.
he’d have to charge gd. for,
and not grow fat on it then,
because the number he would
sell would be sqpii; and
good Cigars havejto be kept
in a uniform temperature,
free from exposure to the
air, or else they'll go off.
Those are just some of
the reasons why you can't
get a good Cigar in the
country parts of AusttaHk,
and also why so very few
Cigars are at present smoked
in the country.
Buy a 100-box of my
" DIADEMAS,” smoke 5 or
6 of them, and, if they’re
not as good as any 6d.
Cigaryou’ve ever smoked,
you needn’t pay a penny.
That’s the way I’m in-
troducing my Cigars into
Australia. ; I know Cigar-
making from A to Z. I know
these DIADEMAS Cigarsof
mine are just the coolest,
smoothest and most luxu-
rious smoke any man could wish for. Cigar-
smokers of a lifetime say so; and the more you
know about a good Cigar the better you’ll like
them.
My DIADEMAS are made from the very
finest, mild, rich, mellow, Java leaf, the filling or
inside heart of the Cigar being all'straight long
Brazilian. The Cigars are hand made by skilled
workers, and every box has to pass me before it
goes out to a customer; and is guaranteed to
burn free and draw easily. The Cigar*is 4 inches
long, and the picture at the side is a copy of it,
exact in size and shape.
Now, this DIADEMAS Cigar is as good as
any 6d. Cigar, sold in the cities by high-claSs
tobacconists, as I sell it direct to the customer —
saving the cost of wholesalers’, salesmen’s, and
retailers’ profit. I only charge you 22/6 the box
of 100 DIADEMAS, postage paid right to your
address.
Send 22/6 to-day, and get the box of 100
DIADEMAS by return post. In ordering, say
whether you like the mild, medium, or strong
grade. Please add exchange to country and
interstate cheques.
My Only Address is
LOUIS KAUFFMANN
34 QUEEN STREET, MELBOURNE

yyELLINGTON RUGBY FOOTBALL UNION.
Particulars as to Application for Caretaker & Groundman
1. The salary is £l3O per annum, with free resi-
dence on the Athletic Park Property.
2. The Athletic Park consists of two Football
Grounds, enclosed on three sides with rails, and by a
Grandstand some 170 yards long on the other side.
The whole ground is enclosed within fences.
3. In addition to the Caretaker’s house, there are
three players’ dressing rooms, two referees’ rooms, and
stable and outbuildings and public urinal.
4. The duties of the Caretaker are:—-To prepare
and mark the football area from April to September
in each year. Games are played on Wednesday and
Saturday afternoons. From October to March to pre-
pare and keep in order, up to four cricket pitches and
practice ground. To keep Grandstand, dressing rooms
and other buildings clean. Generally to look after
the ground and appurtenances, to effect minor repairs
to buildings and fences, and other work on the
ground which may be necessary for any purpose for
which the ground may be let.
5. Applicants to state fully their qualifications and
experience in preparing cricket grounds.
6. Duties commence as from September 22, 1911.

Dr. X. La Motte Sage, a Noted Scientist, Donates
Five Thousand Pounds to be Used for the
Publication and Free Distribution of a
Valuable WorK on Personal Magnetism
and Hypnotic Influence.
Wants to Demonstrate the Practical Value and
Power of This New Science in Business, in
Society, in the Home, in Politics, in Love, in
Diseases, and as a Factor in Influencing
and Swaying the Minds of People.
Prominent Business and Professional Men,
Ministers of the Gospel, and others Heartily
Endorse the Movement—A Noted College
UndertaKes the WorK of Free
Distribution.
Anyone May Get a BooK Absolutely Free so long
as the Edition Lasts, and Master the Hidden
Mysteries of This Marvellous Power at
His Own Home —Many Jealously
Guarded Secrets Made Public.
Carnegie is giving away his fortune for libraries.
Dr. X. La Motte Sage purposes to put the most use-
ful book in all libraries free of charge right into the
homes of the people. For this purpose he has just
donated £5OOO, and a big publishing house is busy
day and night turning out the books for free distri-
bution. The book Dr. Sage wishes to distribute free
of charge is entitled “The Philosophy of Personal
Influence.” It is heartily endorsed by the ablest
business men, ministers, doctors, and lawyers of two
continents. It is beautifully illustrated with the
finest half-tone engravings, and every page is brimful
of intensely interesting and practical information.
It is a book which should be in every home. It is by
far the most remarkable work of the kind that has
ever been written, and it has truly created a sensa-
tion in the book world.
It explains numerous instances in which people
have been secretly and instantaneously controlled by
hypnotic influence. It tells you how to learn to pro-
tect yourself from the use of hypnotic power over
you. It tells you how to learn to develop and use
your magnetic power so as to wield a wonderful in-
fluence over those with whom you come in contact.
Men like Vanderbilt, Morgan, Rockefeller, and
other millionaires have studied the very methods ex-
plained in this book, and have used them in their
business to pile up millions. This book lays bare
secrets in the lives of rich men of which you have
never dreamed. It reveals all the hidden mysteries
of personal magnetism, hypnotism, magnetic healing,
etc., and discloses the real source of power and in-
fluence in every walk of life. It contains secret in-
formation of inestimable value to every person who
wants to succeed in life. Many of the most promi-
nent public men in this country have obtained this
book, and read every line, and they are daily using
its teachings to their own profit and gain.
It explains to you the power by which you may
cure yourself and others of all diseases and bad
habits without the aid of drugs or medicines. It
tells of a secret, instantaneous method by which you
may produce a state of insensibility to pain in any
part of the body, so that the teeth may be extracted
and surgical operations performed without the use
of cocaine, ether, or anaesthetics of any kind.
It tells how to learn to induce sleep in your-
self or others, at any time of the day or night
that you may desire. It tells you of a subtle
by which you can develop your mental facul-
ties, improve your memory, eradicate disagreeable
tempers and habits in children, strengthen your will
power, and rise to a position of prominence in your
community. It will truly make you a leader of men.
If you have not achieved the success to which your
abilities and talents justly entitle you, if you would
like to secure a good paying position, obtain an in-
crease in salary, if you care to rise higher in your
accepted business or calling, if you desire to wield
a greater influence over others, if you care to win
the love or friendship of someone you know, or if
| you long for fame or renown, you should write for
a copy of this remarkable book at once.
The following extracts from a few of the letters
( received from some of the people who have read the
book give an idea of its unusual character and gTeat
value:—

G. S. Lincoln, M.D., 101 Crutchfield Street, Dallas,

are marvellous. I have used them on my patients
with wonderful results. They cure diseases when
medicine and everything else fails.”

At least sixteen hours each day the brain labours.
With most of us its work is strenuous, wearing away the
tissues at a rapid rate.
This waste must be made ffood.
The brain and nerve centres are delicate and sensitive and
demand their own particular, delicate food.
The most important is phosphate of potash, and nature has
almost hidden this in minute particles in the wheat kernel, up
close to the outer coats of the grain.
GRAPE-NUTS fully cooked food is rich in phosphate of
potash, which combines with albumen to feed the brain and nerve
centres.
Fact! Fact! Fact!
Ten days use will set up a tired brain in a most unmistakable
manner and rebuild it on food, not on a false stimulant.
Grape-Nuts
There’s a reason.

The Harvest
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
summer pageant passes^
I The Harvest-time is here;
The corn waves golden banners,
The V heat r is \ in the ea T- • •
But see, limned on the sky-line ,
With lilt of fife and drum
ind 'flash of bit and sabre,
The grey ltough-Riders came!’
\ happy-hearted people
In low or high emoloy,
Our songs are songs of freedom,
Our heritage is joy ;
’Tis said we have forgotten
Ancestral ties ... I say
The spirit of our fathers
Sits at our hearth to-day 1
'Tis not unmeaning, surely,
That sun-gods’ passion tires
The aureole of glory
Investing city spires;
'Tis then I hear the voices
Of “choirs invisible,”
That honor, worth and wisdom
And sovereignty foretell.
The silver moon-led waters
That lullaby the sands
Of long red-burnished beaches
Spell me with strange demands,
Till an unearthly rapture
Uplifts my soul beyond
The ken of mortal wisdom,
The chafe of earthly bond.
'Tis then by strange enchantment,
Ineffable and sweet, .
I see the Nation’s glory
Resplendent and complete ;
I see with fire-new vision
The twin-lords, Right and Wrong,
Sway multitudes whose passion
Shall live in Art and Song!
The yellow corn is waving,
The grape is big with wine,
The sun-beams kiss the vineyards,
And red, red lips kiss mine;
The day is blue and golden,
The Harvest-time is nigh . . .
The pulses of the Nation
Are beating fever-high !
But, look! limned on the sky-line,
The grey Rough-Riders come
With flash of bit and sabre
And lilt of fife and drum!
Their message is Destruction,
Death forged their gleaming arms. . .
The happy-hearted people
See nothing that alarms !
How shall we portion Pleasure,
How discipline Desire —
With honey-sweet indulgence
Or soul-consuming fire?
I love the happy people,
I love the radiant skies;
The heart that is too niggard
Of pleasure surely dies !
Jn earth and sky when sunrise
Sweeps on in roseate floods,
A thousand joys are bursting
Like sweet September buds ;
The trees gleam green and russet,
The wet pearls gem the sod,
Till color is a passion.
The outward sign of God.
What shall the Harvest render,
What our profusion raise—
A monument of sorrow'
To dark our children’s days?
Re our attainment worthy . . .
When grey Rough-Riders come!
Let every true man follow
The runrnle of the drum !
N.S.W,
W. M. WHITNY.
An Ornament of the Bar.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
AN ungainly man, with feet like a
duck’s, and with trousers feathered
to the knee, like the legs of a
Brahma hen, had been prowling and pecking
among the lunch without qualifying, and
Patrick Mackaboy had refrained from smit-
ing him with a squelched lemon. Some of
the legitimate loungers criticised this as a
dereliction of duty. To them the free lunch
was largely food for amusement, and they
hated to have the best know r n sharpshooter
with squozen fruit miss his opportunities.
“That waster was plainly as empty of
sustenance as a dried husk,” said the bar-
man, “and it is not in me to stall off the
truly ravenous with offensive missiles. I
was an actor myself once.”
Patrick disregarded insolent, derisive and
derogatory comment, and continued smoothly :
“It seems 1 was marker, wood-and-water
Joey, and lighting editor for Scanlan, at his
Peep Inn, Pipeclay, when the Orion Dra-
matic Organisation (sole manager, Angus
Trevor) lost its gay soubrette. The young
lady succumbed to the repeated attacks of
a miner with a 2oz. quartz lode and a habit-
ual 20 quarts load, and so created an opening
in Art for P. Mackaboy. No, Art did not
command me to sit on kitchen tables, kick-
ing short skirts while eating a large apple
with astonishing fidelity to nature. Ihe
nice boy of the co. was promoted to those
giddy heights; the rendering of heavy
fathers, heavy villains, bulky uncles, and
such massive and unwieldy stuff fell to my
little lot, and I rendered them down with
the deadly certainty of a road-roller.
“1 suffered for Art’s sake over a space of
27 days, and then at Dandiloo, which is,
as you know, somewhere between Ipswich,
Queensland, and the Eight Hours monu-
ment, Trevor established illegal connections
with a keg of fermented molasses, locally
fsppwn as scrub rum, and started a unique
collection of lizards, ancient and modern.
Trevor specialised in lizards; he had all
the lacertilia scurrying through his hair,
from the pensive glow-worm ( Anf/uis fratj-
IU«) to the Oft. monitor that swallows a
whole hen, aud they ,occupied so much of
Angus’s valuable time that his company
succumbed to neglect and general inability ;
and presently 1 found myself at the fag end
of a long romp down the I nsolvents’ Track,
with the current completed between the
sore soles of my feet and the great he. rt of
and so short of applied nourishment
that my backbope was pushing the buttons
off my fancy vest,
“Night finds me dossing in a ruined sum-
mer-house adjacent to a somewhat palatial
afiode, as bush abodes go. I had dined
sumptuously off five immature carrots and
half a peck of green peas fresh from the
vipe, and was, feeling as comfortable as a
Central American republic with a revolution
raging ip the interior.
“It was still early evening when I awoke
from my first nap, with an uneasy conscious-
ness of something amiss quite apart from
the reaction of a purely vegetable diet. In-
stinct had not played me false; a rude fel-
low in the garb of a journeyman photo-
grapher was climbing the house wall with
the aid of a down pipe and a grape-vine. He
gave a few deft, scholarly touches to the
window sash, opened the crib, and slipped
in.
“While I was ruminating the situation
and revolving possibilities, a portmanteau
came creeping down the wall. With no in-
centive beyond the horrid prospect of enter-
ing Sydney wearing asphalt pavements next
the skin, and haunting town without a con-
jecturable possibility of overtaking one meal
per diem, I stepped briskly from my latticed
retreat, undid the blind-cord from the port-
manteau, tied it to the handles of my grip—
which contained 14 medium-sized, budding
potatoes, a quart of green peas in the shell,
and various worthless odds and ends of rai-
ment —caught up the goods reasonably sup-
posed to have been stolen, and refreshed my-
self with a long walk.
“It was fully five minutes later when the
qualms of conscience began to play. Funda-
mentally, I am an honest man. I can say
without fear of contradiction, I am as
frightened of being found out as the most
righteous elder in the kirk, and that is the
true basis of consistent upright conduct. I
was weak and as stiff as a steeple after
my impersonation of the walking gent in
Pilgrim’s Progress! Never were two
feet more reluctant to perform their office.
However, the prospect of three years’ hard
and other probable outcomings of the novel
plot in which I was involved urged me on.
“Three miles later that load of iniquity
weighed a ton, and my feet filled me with
Christian sympathy for the bear that is
taught to dance on a hot cauldron. Where
scrub crept in on the track and silence
dominated the scene, I set down my burden
of sin to investigate and appraise. That
settled it. The ’manteau w T as full of silver
tinware of the kind with which sumptuous
ladies love to deck their wash-stands and
dressing-tables, and there was a glittering
entanglement of bangles, chains, rings, ear-
rings, brooches, lockets, and watches worth
a king’s hansom.
“I closed and restrapped that receptacle.
My duty was plain before me. I would
chuck the swag into the scrub, and journey
on, friendless and alone. To attempt busi-
ness dealings with those valuables anywhere
within 1000 miles would mean
’“My wise reflections were never finished.
Something came excitedly out of the night
and hit me, and when I came-to, a large, fat,
anxious man was discovered trying to revive
my drooping spirits, breathing on me a
quantity of breath liberally saturated with
whisky, by the light of a large motor lamp.
“ ‘You’re not hurt, Casterton,’ said the
plump Samaritan.
"‘Ain’t I?’ said I.
“‘Not you,’ said he, ‘the motor wasn’t on
you a second.’
“ ‘I accept your assurance,’ I said faintly.
I was in no mood to argue.
“When I came-to again, we were sitting on
the front seat of the motor. The fat man
was holding me down with his left and
driving with his right, and we were punc-
turing space like a projectile from a 200-ton
gun.
“ ‘Bear up,’ said the large, fat man, ‘we’ll
be able to get a drink in a minute. All you
want is a stiff whisky.’
“‘ls it?’ I said.
“‘Why, of course,’ said he. ‘Don’t be a
damfool. All anybody wants is a stiff
whisky. All I want is a stiff whisky.’
“He pulled up at a wayside pub, with a
jolt that must have precipitated me into the
most exclusive Antarctic circles had I not
been moored to a massive brute who might
have stood for a diagram of the week’s meat
supply of a dense population. The motor-
hog seized and propped me against the
counter of one of those wayside bars, lit with
a smoky, explosive ninepenny lamp, where
the bedraggled barmaiden searches for the
marks of identification on the bottles with a
guttering candle, and a large, black spider
coldly scrutinises and reproves your bad
habits from each corner of the room.
“ ‘Mr. Pope !’ piped the elderly female at-
tendant, with the glad note the trade is wont
to assume when welcoming a prodigal son
with the beans which the swine did not eat.
“ ‘lt’s all right, Mrs. Milling,’ said Fat;
‘don’t faint for joy. Your visions of wealth
are mere illusions this trip. I’m only a pass-
ing incident. I’ve been scooting about in my
car for 40 hours, chasing the Demon Drink
round the country. Now I’m for Sydney,
with my old friend Chudleigh (Chudleigh.
my old friend Mrs. Milling), and Ive
merely dropped in for whisky by the way.
A blessed trinity for me, and a measured
dose for my dear old friend Chalmers.’
“He gulped three whiskies in one, and
hoisted me aboard again. I noticed, with a
pang of remorse, that the portmanteau or
loot was carefully disposed in the back of
the car. . , „ , , ~
“ ‘Of course you re goin to Sydney, said
Pope, letting his motor off the chain once
more, and heating up the atmosphere with
its meteoric velocity. ‘Everybody’s goin
to Sydney. I’m goin’ to Sydney. Once
there we’ll have them broken legs of yours
seen to, and buy you a new eye, Chrisholm.
Then we’ll smear ochre all over that doomed
city. Sydney thinks she’s a sagacious place,
but when Owen Pope arrives she’s just a
huge mug for his special delectation.
Whoop!’ , _ .
"We barked a tree, aq.d took a flying leap
at a bit of a creek. For a moment the pro-
peller whirled in air, while the motor de-
cided between turning a forward somersault
and resuming her flight. Her better judgment
prevailed. ,
“ ‘I live round there,’ said Pope, throwing
one arm back into the streaky film through
which we were being projected ; ‘and tor 4U
hours I’ve been running circles round my
happy home, picking up refreshment en
route Sir, a motor’s a magnificent imple-
ment in the hands of the unhappy married.
It’s tlm great get-away. Wife’s a chronic in-
valid, with 134 complaints. I’m 133 of them.

PEPS ARE INVALUABLE IN THE HOME.
COLDS have a habit of “ running thro’ the
house ” because of the germ origin
of these complaints. The first sufferer
walks into the house and scatters broadcast
countless germs, which are then breathed
in by the rest of the family. Then brother,
sister, father or mother begin to complain
of a cough, and it is a piece of good fortune
if this does not leave behind some more
serious chest weakness. There is no limit to the conse-
quence of a cough or cold, and no wise alternative
remains but to treat the first symptoms promptly
with Peps—the wonderful medicine which is breathed
from pleasant dissolving tablets into the cough-torn
breathing-tubes and lungs.
With a box of Peps always handy, coughs, colds
and influenza are banished from the home and kept
away. Their throats strengthened by Peps, the
children can enjoy the sharp, bracing winter air,
while old folks find in Peps a quick escape from that
old-age winter cough.

Matchless for the Complexion.
Mother-in-law’s a mix-up between a Cumber-
land wrestler, a Presbyterian convention, and
a dragon with gastric influenza. When she
gets down to either of her simple elements, I
run for the motor, and so mote it be. Whoop!
Was that a house we passed through, Chom-
ley ? So glad I ran across you, old boy.
Remember, have your broken spine shored
up, and get the best eye in Sydney. I pay.
VVhoop ! It’s a fair thing. Motor in your
neighbor’s eye. Whoop!’
“We had skimmed all the pickets off some-
body’s front fence in an out-and-outer sub-
urb, and uprooted a sleeping policeman, and
the lights of Sydney flickered below us like
fireflies in a marsh.
“Well, we tore into Pitt-street somewhere
on the right side of midnight, and everywhere
Owen Pope was welcomed with the glad yell.
I was Owen Pope’s bosom friend Clinten, and
Collard, and Cresswell, and my semi-de-
tached footwear flip-flapped on grateful pile
carpets, and my shocking trousers and back-
broken coat were reflected in bevelled mir-
rors too numerous to specify. We were
doing the rounds of the polished back bars
and the halls of spice. At first I sought to
hide my shame in the dust-bin, but, after
taking three pints of champagne for it, those
symptoms disappeared, and I discovered that
a man’s a man for a’ that, and it seemed to
me I was actually 33£ per cent, a better man
than any other man living. Which shows
that it doesn’t matter what a gentleman
wears if he’s only drunk enough.
“There were alarums, excursions, pot-
barneys, and cursory entanglements with
the police. During the course of one skir-
mish with a publican and a sinner, the bottle
of soda with which the young lady usually
settled all disputes exploded prematurely.
After that Pope was a victim to delusions.
He strung 4cwt. of bulk beef about my neck
nnd wept.
“ ‘You shaved my life, Carrol,’ he said.
‘Yersherdid! Never forget. Come 'n' live
wishme. Shaved my life, fell ashassin’s
hand. Risked yer own life shave me. Have
ehigar!’
“He took me to his hotel, and half the
police of Sydney seemed to be chasing the
motor before we got there. I hit bed in one
piece, and dear old Owen pulled off my
boots for me. At least he pulled off the
loose soles.
“I awoke in the garish light of 10 o’clock
and was some minutes recovering my
recollection and establishing my identity in
a dormitory of unaccustomed splendor. Owen
Pope occupied and overflowed a bed at the
other end of the room.
“Memory came back with a dull, sicken-
ing thud, and my horrified right eye fell
upon the incriminating portmanteau. It
stood over against the opposite wall, and on
its side was, what I had not noticed before,
a glittering nickel plate, with a name on it
in bold, black letters.
“I slunk out of bed; I sneaked to the
fatal luggage; I glared for one awful mo-
ment at the printed name; and then turned
the name to the wall, and it was off and
away for Patrick Mackaboy. Down stairs
I paused a second to tell the floorwalker I
was going to have a swim, to be measured
for a new gold tooth, and to order medical
comforts for O. Pope; then I proceeded to
nut the greatest space between myself and
that house of call in the shortest time pos-
sible to a man with sore feet in bottomless
boots.
“Cos why?” said the barman after a dra-
matic pause. “The name on that purloined
portmanteau was OWEN POPE!”
EDWARD DYSON.
The First Axiom.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
CONSTABLE Murphy was a man of
resource. It was he who had ar-
rested Ah Pat at Ivilmore. He made a
brilliant night attack upon the odorous ruin
in which the offending heathen dwelt, and
seized him while asleep upon the cabbages.
Ah Pat, after committal, was sent to vege-
tate in Pentridge until the Sessions were
held in Kilmore; and in due time the intelli-
gent officer who effected the arrest was de-
tailed to bring him back for trial. Daniel
was also told that a new-chum bobby named
Doyle would return with him to take up
relief duty at Kilmore.
Dan met the new man at the depot, and
together they journeyed out to Pentridge.
On the way, Dan explained to his new com-
rade the first axiom of the force —“Shut
mouth, no sound” —and gave him some ad-
vice as to the treatment of prisoners. “Just
moind th’ way Oi’ll dale wid th’ Chow that’s
to come back wid us. O’im doin’ this,
Tim Doyle, bekase ye’re a Clare man like
mesilf.”
At the Stockade, Ah Pat was handed over
with the usual formalities, and Constable
Dan immediately tied his pigtail to Tim
Doyle’s wrist. As they went along, Dan
said: “Give him a bit av a twisht now an’
again, Tim, just to show him he’s in th’
hands av th’ Law.”
When they got to the Wheatsheaf Inn,
near by the Broadmeadows Railway Station,
Dan halted his little party. “Bring in th’
Chow, Tim. We’ve two hours t’ wait.
Here, Mr. Publichouse, give th’ Chow a
drink, an’ see that we don’t die av thirst on
yer hands.”
A stiff nobbier was handed to Ah Pat, and
the new constable showed that he knew how
to treat prisoners by jerking the pigtail so
that all the fiery stuff went down in one
immense gulp. Dan laughed loudly, and re-
marked : “Tim, that was nately , done.
There’s promotion ahead f’r you. Bring
him along now to the bar parlor, and tie th’
divil to the sofy be th’ tail. What’ll
ye have? Do ye play euchre? Ye do!
Good!”
The cards and drinks were brought,
and they settled down happily. When it
occurred to Dan to look at his watch, he
found that more than two hours had flown
and taken the train with them. “Never
moind, we must wait till th’ mornin’. Oi’ll
spin th’ sargint a yarn whin we get to Kil-
more. But Oi can’t go on playin’ with that
damn Chow grinnin’ at me. Bring him out,
and we’ll put him in th’ stable. Come
along, me burrd of Paradise.”
Ah Pat was lugged out and ti<»d by his
convenient pigtail to a post; then his feet
and hands were tied with lengths of rope,
the door was closed, and a large stone rolled
against it. “Go to shlape, ye haythin,”
grunted Dan; “we’ve missed th’ train
through ye. If ye wake up thirsty, whistle
an’ Oi’ll give ye another drink. ‘R.I.P.,’ as
th’ praste says.”
Back the pair went to the parlor, where
they drank and played till they fell asleep
at the table.
Housed by the shout of “Milk-o” 10 min-
utes before the morning train's starting time,
they had a couple of drinks for break-
fast, and proceeded to the stable to collect
the prisoner.
“Now, then, Tim, rowl away th’ stone,
an’ bring him out Holy smoke ! Where is
he? GONE!! Well, there’s no use
lookin’ for a cunnin’ hay then that could get
away like that. No use standin’ here.
We’ll have to get back by this train, any-
how. For the station now, Tim, at the
double !”
By running hard they just managed to get
to the station in time. Together they fell
into a carriage, and flung themselves down.
Then they stared at each other disconsolate-
ly till they got their breath back.
“What are ye goin’ to do about it?”
gasped Tim.
Dan wiped the sweat from his forehead.
“Disremember everythin’ since ye left th’
dippo this mornin’ be the first trainman’
kape silence while Oi think av somethin’.”
Tim Doyle was not happy. His introduc-
tion to duty had commenced by helping to
lose a prisoner, and he looked forward with
dread to the interview with the unknown
sergeant at Kilmore.
At last Constable Dan spoke up. “Now
moind me, Tim. O’im a sort av godfather to
ye in th’ foorce since yesterday, an’ Oi’ve
a right to advise ye. Ye’re breakin’ yer
heart wid th’ fear of th’ sargint whin we get
back widout Ah Pat. Be aisy in yer moind,
for Oi think Oi can fix it.”
When they arrived at Kilmore, Dan did
not lead his brother officer direct to the sta-
tion, but proceeded instead across the rail-
way line towards a large vegetable garden.
A young Chinaman was seen working near
the fence as they drew near. Dan knew he
had arrived in Kilmore only a week before,
and could neither speak nor understand Eng-
lish. He climbed the fence in a leisurely
fashion, and, stepping carefully over a row
of cabbages, seized the unsuspicious toiler.
“Come along with me, ye haythin,” he said
sternly. “There’ll be no more burnin’ fences
for you !”
The owner of the garden, seeing the
struggle from afar, ran down with a long-
handled shovel. “Wha’ for !” he screamed.
“Him my cousin! Him jus’ come! Him
all li’!” .
“Moind now!” roared Dan, gripping his
writhing captive. “Before a witness ye’ve
threatened me loife in the execution of me
jooty wid a long-handled shovel! Look ye,
Tong, Oi’d arrist ye for it this minit only
Oi’ve me hands full wid this divil. Now,
Oi warm you that if ye’re seen in th’ town
or near th’ coorthouse before General Ses-
sions is concluded, Oi’ll have you on a
charge of attimpted murder. Come along
you. Ah Pat!”
The two constables with their new prisoner
soon reached the police station, where Dan
the resourceful formally reported himself
with Ah Pat the second, produced the neces-
sary papers, and introduced the young con-
stable, whom he described as an officer full of
promise.
“Th’ papers are all right,” said the ser-
geant, doubtfully. “But are ye sure that’s
Ah Pat? He looks a lot younger than when
he was here last.” Then, to the bewild-
ered Chinaman: “Here, you! Is your name
Ah Pat?”
The prisoner, who probably thought he
was being welcomed, gazed at Dan, who
grinned amiably and nodded. The pris-
oner did the same.
“All right,” said the sergeant. “He ad-
mits he’s Ah Pat, and he ought to know.
Lock him up.”
“Ah, sargint,” said Dan, a little later,
“it’s wonderful th’ effect th’ Pmtridge air
has on the Chows about here.”
The case was tried next day, and Ah Pat
the Second was awarded six months. After
he had been conducted to the cells, Dan
turned to his pupil and remarked solemnly:
“Now, Tim, ye’ve learnt th’ first axiom f’r
promotion in th’ foorce. Besides, thim
Chows is all cousins, anyway, an’ has flat
noses, glory be t’ God. It’s a great hilp in
idintification.”
D. L. H.
The Flower of Sleep.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
LAST night there was light on the ocean,
The storm being done,
And color and music and motion
Were blended in one.
The moon silver-splendid was raying
Her glory serene ;
No star, not a planet, gainsaying
Her right as night’s queen.
The beach, like a shield, lying under
The moon was agleam;
All was beauty, a marvel, a wonder,
A trance and a dream.
I said (thinking then of land-roses
And sweet-scented bowers) :
The sea in its blue depths encloses ,
A garden of flowers.
And this, maybe this, is the reason
Men, broken by Care,
Or pierced by the pain of love-treason.
Seek quietude there.
t
Grown tired of the stars in their splendor,
The night and the noon.
The sunset, the dawn, green and tender,
The sun and the moon ;
Heart-sick of to-day and to-morrow,
Of rain and of wind,
Of Joy fleet of foot, and of Sorrow
Fast moving behind,
They ask not the world’s tears or pardon,
But haste to the deep,
To seek in its twilit sea-garden
The pale Flower of Sleep.
N.S.W.
